         PROFESSOR R. D. RANADE AND HIS
                    SPIRITUAL LINEAGE

                      INTRODUCTION

                                I


      What is this enigmatic book? Is it history, philosophy,
science, or religion? Does it tell us how to enhance our
material prosperity, comfort and ease, along with lawful
honesty, efficiency and perfection or how to develop an
attitude of mind which will not only defy any defeat or
calamity in life, but also increase the power of the soul
so that it will soon learn to be in tune with the Infinite?
Indeed, no book will ever make us prosperous or spiritual.
At the best, it will persuade us to go by the way which
16205 to prosperity, to divinity or to both. For, man 18
free to go one way or the other, and none can compel him
to do a particular thing, Persuasion is enough; to go beyond
it is silly, and this is what the writer of the present book
wishes to do,

       As one born in India, and as one who has had ample
 opportunities   to watch and study several saints and
 Scoundrels, rituals and truthful modes of worship and seve-
 ral falsities, pretensions and juggleries of words, I think I
 am, to-some extent at least, able to discern the distinction
 between grain and husk. I wished to know whether the state
 of affairs in the most civilized countries like the United
Kingdom and United States of America is different from
that in India. But to my great disillusionment I found
that the content of these so-called civilized countries
                                                        does
not differ in any fundamental manner.           Dutifulness,
honesty, efficiency and perfection are not so much virtues
 as   compulsions   of the   affluent   social   structure   in   those
 countries. There is perfection because there is neck 10 neck
fight in business; there is efficiency because there 18 {he
2         Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


steam roller of the supremacy of Law; there is sense of
duty because there is the greed of the dollar,                        or, of the
pound: and, there is honesty because there are other men
and agencies who are keen ‘to detect and expose dishonesty
From my experience during the six months stay in the
U.S.A.     I can say with a feeling of 07166 that there was no
adultery or pollution in food, water or medicine, nor was
there any occasion on which relief did not come within a
few minutes,        nor any lack of courtesy and good manners,
appreciation or encouragement.          But at the back-of this‘all
the motive-force was the greed for the, dollar: and it hag
becomé      .so   deeply’   ingrained   in   all-in:    the      schdol-going
child?én;    in “adoléScerit boys and girls,           in. adults         “atid “old
men and women it has produced the only one result of
making them cry at one voice ‘I want’,            ‘I want’,        This exer-
cise of extreme self-assertion has gone beyond the limit of
virtue,    and it has surreptitiously, violated the freedom of
parents,    ‘teachers,   and all other fellow-beings:+In asserting
one self and his interests he does not care forthe ‘tender
feelings    01" 1115 parerits; his wife and his innocent children
but gets himself married to a so-called young woman who
has been divorced thrice or four times. Her only qualifica--
tion is-that she too, in asserting herself has actedin the "
same way. How many mothers and how many fathers should
one then care 1077, It will be enough to observe a ‘Fathers
day’    and a ‘Motheis day’      in a-year                        ~


       After ‘all, what can be said refers only to secular life
which obtains in the permissive society of the- Western
countries     and    ‘which   might become       the        pattern      of- the
universal secular life in course of time.               =    '   |” ~!


     Quite ‘opposéd’ ‘to this however is thé picture of the
spiritual life in Indix‘and-especially’in the school of Profes-
sor ए, D, Ranade.’ One peculiar feature of that school which
I wish to mention in the introduction is that the spiritual
life is not opposed'to secular-life’ but is inclusive of it
There have been saintsof the school who are almost’ un-
                  हैं      _ Introduction   द ae त OH              ~ 9

kniown'to the world. but that does not-diminish. their. vaiue
in the; least. ‘Ini fact, it is. with. the views of-mjiaking ‘them
known to the world at large that sthis book’ is sintended.
Kadasiddha (Revanasiddha), .Nimbargi :Maharaja, Bhau:
58111 ‘Maharaja, Amburao Maharaja.and Professor R.” D.
Ranade-aré not only spiritual heroes of asprovince’       of Inidid;
put theylare self-illuminating     light-houses  which  will  shine
vver the, whole plobe. . ..                    7
   ५       dla,                                              + ४

        They are not a whit behind the great sages, of yore,.
who are considered as the founders of the Brahmavidya.
Sankaracarya has told us in his ‘Satsloki’ that Sannyasa is
of two kinds, namely, one consisting of the abandonment
of one’s own house and household affairs, and the other
consisting of disgust even for one’s own 9049, Following
in. the foot-steps of Sri Bhausahib Maharaja who had left ,
his own house at Umadi and made his residence at Inchgiri, |
Baba too had. left Jigjivani and come to reside in Inchgiri, `
only to make the life possible according to the second kind_
of sannyasa.      -Baba used.to say     that,one should always.
consider himself as a dead body; the truth of this was seen '
by those who had closely observed him’ to the end of his
life.    He never felt that he had a body which should be
preserved, nourished and fondled.       His detachment from the
worldly life as well as from the bodily pleasures and pains
was exhibited throughout his life by way of disgust which
he felt for them.       This was not the index of sorrow and the
miseries arising from these objects, but it was the natural
reaction of his realization of God. The education of these
two great saints -was’ not ‘beyond -the Vernacular: final “ex-
amination; but they showed as great an insight ‘in the
Spiritual life as‘Sankaracarya had shown. Another instance
 which we wish to cite is this   Sankaracarya has told-in-vhis-          >
‘Treatise -on- direct ‘realization’ during : the .course: of ‘his
definition of Pranayama that one should .consider himself
to be the same -as ‘Brahman while he-inhales the breath,
and should consider that he is not the ‘Not-self? while
                                                        ex-
haling the breath. This is, no doubt, .a good counse
                                                     l to a
` 4      Prof. 2. 2. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


 novice in the spiritual life, but being dissatisfied with this
 half-hearted instruction, Sri Bhausahib and Baba (as Am-
 burao      was    fondly     called    by     his    disciples)     used    to
 instruct    that    they     should     repeat      the     name    of     God
 and     think      that    they   are       identical       with   Brahman
 during     both     the    processes    of    inhaling       and    exhaling
 their    breaths.     Why     should        one     think    at    the   time
 of meditation that he is one with Him while inhaling the
 wind, as recommended by Sankaracarya, and suppose that
 he is not ‘not-self’ while he is exhaling the wind?                      Why
 should he not utilize both the acts of inhaling and exhaling
 for thinking, ‘I am the Brahman?                    Why should one be
 satisfied with Non-attachment (Vairagya) if it would only
 rise out of the sorrows and miseries of the worldly life ?
 He should be equally unconcerned with them on account
 of the bliss of being one with Brahman.                     Similarly, why
 should one leave his own house and be tempted to make
 another    home     instead    of making his habitation              in any
 place as God would like?               One can show himself to be
 unconcerned with. all his bodily conditions throughout his
 life. Considering this, we can say that Bhausahib and Baba
 have shown us that the devotional meditation as instructed
 by the Guru involves the real type of Vairagya and the real
 type of Sannyasa even at the tender age of the boyhood,
 and that this should continue to the end of one’s life. They
 also have thought like Sankaracarya that Sannyasasrama
 can be accepted even from the state of Brahmacarya, and
 not necessarily after the condition of a house-holder is over.

       To raise this question regarding the superiority of one
 to another from among the great pillars of spiritual life —
 Nimbargi Maharaja, Bhausahib Maharaja, Amburao Maha-
 raja and Sri Gurudeva R. D. Ranade is a sign of ignorance
 which can be excused; but to attempt to give an answer to
 the question is to exhibit poverty of intellect. Who can say
 whether the Alps is greater or the Himalayas ? For the defi-
 ciency of one may be the excellence of another. The height
 and length are as necessary for determining the greatness
                        Introduction                         5

as the breadth is. Similarly, the spotless virtuous life is as
necessary as a life of unflinching devotion. Who can take
into consideration the host of virtues and the heights and
depths of devotion and faith in order to judge. and decide
who is the greater of these saints ? We can say in a general
way that each one of them is as great as any other, Nimbargi
Maharaja spent sixtyfour years, that is two third of his long
life of nintysix years in doing the penance of meditation
throughout almost the whole of the day and night. Bhau-
sahib meditated for fiftysix years out of his total span of
life of seventy years. From twelve at midnight to two am
he used to devote this period of two hours in meditation
for the spiritual good of his disciples, And this he did’ while
standing, without a single exception for eighteen years
in all, he used to meditate for nine hours daily and to spend
four or five hours in doing Bhajans thrice a day and in
reading spiritual literature and in telling the essence of it
to others. Amburao Maharaja did exactly as his Guru did.
He began his meditation one hour at least before sun-rise
and ended it when the sun came overhead; for he had no
watch to indicate that six hours were over.       Ranade too,
notwithstanding his life-long pernicious disease, devoted his
entire life since he was fifteen, either in meditation or in
reading and writing philosophical and spiritual books or
in discussions and sermons to all those who approached
him at Nimbal with humility and with the purpose to
realize God

    What then is the criterion which will enable us to
determine the superiority of one saint over another, if, for
all of them, meditation on the divine Name as imparted
by the Guru is the only Reality ? Who can say that one of
the four dazzling lamps is superior to the remaining three,
if every one of them is capable of lighting as many lamps
as one wishes to light, when, in other words, the common
property of these all is to illumine itself as well as other
objects which come in its purview? Should one seek to
find out the first lamp ? Will not his purpose be served by
6       Prof. R. 13. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


the second, the third and the fourth lamp?                        What does
it matter if Ranade happens to be the Guru of one, or if
Bhausahib or Amburao happens to be the Guru of another ?
When there exists no difference between the disciple and
the Guru,     or between the Guru and God, when they form
the trinity in one, 10 choose any one as the Guru is not to
belittle the others, but to realize that they are non-different
from the one who is chosen.                 Extending the argument we
can say that there is no difference between a Christian and
a Hindu saint, for God is neither Hindu nor Christian. All
differences and distinctions in the realm of the spirit are
man-made distinctions and are confirmed by fanatic
followers. They look to the body-complex of a particular
saint, which is formed by the perishable Prakrti, namely,
the   five   elements   of    earth,    water,    air,   light    and   space
(Akasa) and the three gunas of sattva, raja and. tama.                   Had
they realized God in the saint, they would have put a stop
to their fanaticism and useless argumentation.                    011९ resid-
ing in Varanasi will not hesitate to say that the river which
flows there is the Ganga, though he might be knowing that
other rivers such as Alakananda, and Yamuna are pouring
their waters into it, Similarly, the quest of the spiritual life
ought to end in cognizing and realizing that the ultimate
Guru of any human being is the vision of God who resides
in the hearts of all the great saints in the world.              That vision
remains hidden first;        but it is by the grace of that hidden
God that the saint awakens the slumbering and the ignorant
disciple into the broad dazzling day light of the Real.                 One
who realizes this and enables others to realize it is the Guru,
whether he is a Hindu, or a non-Hindu.


                                       Il


 A SAINT AS GREAT AS ANYONE OF THESE IS YET
                             TO BE BORN

      1. Jnanadeva said that he had a contact with a woman
only once in his life, that is at the time when he was born.
                        Introduction         *              7

    2, The great sage Swami Narsimha Sarasvati is said ‘to
have brought a dead man back to life and to have given, the
explanation that he borrowed that much of the span of 1115
life from his future (next) birth.   As if this is not enough
Sri Bhausahib postponed his death by ten years as is clear
from what he said to Amburao, “For your sake I have post-
poned my death once by ten years and I shall not do it
again.”             -                                       +

    3.    Namadeva, the founder of the Bhakti School in
Maharashtra has said in one of his Abhangas that one may
cut his throat if the Brahmadeva would not come to wel-
come and worship the devotee of God. Someone said to
Bhausahib, ‘Namadeva is dead and gone, who is there to
vouchsafe the utterances of Namadeva ? Bhausahib told
the person, ‘Why
               ? I am still living, if one would not attain
to God-head, let him cut my throat.’

    4.    Once Bhausahib was indisposed and someone re-
commended him to have some medicine. Bhausahib said,
“As I have not committed any sin in my life, I do not wish
to take any bitter medicine.’

     5.   The whole of the world extols Prhalada as the
greatest of devotees, but Bhausahib Maharaja asked us if
Prhalada who was remembering that he hada father, was
really so great a Bhakta, 1.6. a devotee of God.

    6.    At Jamkhandi Bhausahib used to go outside the
town and sit in a place surrounded by thorns on all sides.
How is it that you go all alone at the dead of the night?”
a disciple asked him, The reply was “I am never alone, the
vision of God goes before mé as if it is a toreh in his hand
and I go after him,”

    7.    Bhausahib used to stand up for two hours in the
night from 12 to 2 A.M. and meditate for the spiritual good
of his disciples.

     8.   At the end of the Sravana Saptah which lasted for
प्पट month and during which time-several persons meditat-
8         Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

ed at Inchgiri, Bhausahib used to go at the terrace of the
matha at Inchgiri to watch the people going from Inchgiri
with his eyes full of tears. Is this not enough to indicate
his love for spiritual life ?

     9.    Amburao Maharaja was once running after the horse
on which Sri Bhausahib Maharaja was riding from Vijapur
to Inchgiri, a distance of about twenty miles. On reaching
Inchgiri, Bhausahib said to Amburao, “I have finished my
today’s meditation, but you, Amburao, were looking hither
and thither. Do your meditation for three hours first and
then      take   your   meals.”   Without      a    word       of   complaint
Amburao did accordingly.

    10.    Once    on   account   of   great       rush   in    the   matha,
Sivalingavva, the saint of Jath was requested to sit for
meditation in the room of Amburao Maharaja by hanging a
curtain between the two. When Amburao Maharaja came
to know that some lady was sitting in his room, he quickly
got out of the room and went outside the village and finished
his meditation there.       When he returned to matha some-
one of his disciples said, “Why should you have left your
room and gone outside the matha for meditation, when
Sivalingavva and yourself are both spiritually advanced
and sufficiently old ?” Amburao replied, “Yes, I have grown
old in age, and she in knowledge, yet she has a woman’s
body and I have a man’s body.             It was not desirable to
trust the body and so I left the Matha.” Amburao Maha-
raja was then sixty years of age.


    11.    Amburao initiated a person and asked him to go
outside the village and meditate, and not to return to the
matha till he had some spiritual experience. Amburao told
the people in the matha that he would not live in the matha
even for a short time if the person who had gone outside the
village for meditation would not have an experience. Besi-
des, he did not take his meals till that person returned.
When the man returned, Amburao told him what he had
                          Introduction                          9


experienced, even before that man told him anything about
it.

   12. In Poona in 1926, Baba was seen dancing and nod-
ding with great joy. Someone asked him as to why he
was doing so. He replied that he was imitating the vision
of God before him.
   18. Ranade knew that he had to leave the body on a
particular day. He told his wife eight days before that
he would leave his mortal coil, but did his sacred duty
of initiating the aspirants in spiritual fold even on the last
day just a few hours before his death and meditated for
three to four hours in the morning of that day. He did
his spiritual duty till the last moment of his life. He asked
the assembly to do the night bhajans and breathed his last
at the end of the Arati.

      14,   When Rajakars came to Nimbal and burnt the rail-
way station which was at half a furlong from the Nimbal
Ashrama,-Ranade asked all those present to sit for medita-
tion on the Name of God.         A miracle took place and the
Rajakars went without looking at the Ashrama.

      15.   Another similar event happened when Ranade was
in Allahabad. Rangoon was bombed, Calcutta was bombed,
and the next site to be bombed was Allahabad.             Ranade
asked the congregation to meditate. This being done, there
remained       no fear   and   there   was   no   bombardment   at
Allahabad,

   16. On another occasion, the Civil Surgeon as well as
other doctors from Sholapur said that there were T.B. germs
in the blood of Professor Ranade. But the very next day
the same team found that there were no germs in the blood.
The doctors surprisingly asked, “Where have those TB.
germs gone ?” Ranade asked them, “Tell me where
                                                from
 they had come, and I shall tell you where they have gone.”

    17. It was Professor ह. D. Ranade who for the first
 time had given us the clear interpretation of “Tatvam
                                                      asi’.
10         Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

that is you are what you see before you.               It was he who
has pointed out that what is seen intuitively before you
cannot be said to be an event in space and time as it is
connecied with Spirit, but really consists in the relation
between the two, meaning thereby, the seer and the seen
so that the relation is more important than the terms related
or that the two terms, the subject and the object, owe their
very existence to the relation between them, In other words,
there      is   no   ‘Triad’   or   (Triputi)   of Jnyata,   jneya   and
Jnana.      Both the Jnyata and the Jneya (subject and the
object) must be dissolved in the bond between them, name-
ly, that of love.        It is Jnana which bifurcates itself into
subject and object, or we may say, that it is love which
bifurcates itself into lover and the beloved.            This love too
is not an event but an eternal process which is capable of
including into itself infinitesimal and infinity.

     18.    Another point worth noting in Ranade’s philosophy
is that the subject or the Atman is as perfect as the Absolute
or the Brahman is.             Had it not been so, it would be im-
possible for the Jiva to become Brahman. Atman and Brah-
man are not different in nature in as much as both of them
are perfect, omniscient and eternal, and so they are inter-
changeable and synonyms of each other. The Atmadarsana
is not different from the Brahmadarsana. Naturally, the
distinction between the Atman and the Brahman is only
nominai and not real.            So, we can make distinction but it
would be without differences. Ghatdkasa and mathakasa are
not distinct from Cidakasa for, there is one akasa only and
so there is no subtraction: or addition possible.            Similarly,
the Atman and the Brahman are not different and there-
fore incapable of being added or subtracted.

                                     ‘TII

    At the outset I must say with humility and gratitude
that God has fulfilled his plan by making me an instrument

          SAMADHI OF

 SHRI   KADASIDDA MAHARAJA

AT SIDDHAGIRI, NEAR KOLHAPUR.
                           CHAPTER           I


      FOUNTAIN-HEADS OF THE SPIRITUAL LIFE

    (Revanasiddha, Kadasiddha and Nimbargi Maharaja)


    Section       A: REVANASIDDHA AND KADASIDDHA

     The spiritual school of Professor ए. D. Ranade took its
 rise in the giddy heights of the reputed school, the Natha
 Sampradaya, the latest and the relevant exponent of which
 is Revanasiddha of Revanagiri in the district of Satara in
 Maharashtra, or as one might say it was Kadasiddha a very
 great saint of Siddhagiri, a place twelve miles away from
 Kolhapur. Kadasiddha is said to be a historical-cum-mytho-
 logical person, but’ Revanasiddha appears to be still more
 mythological     and   less   historical.       Strange   to   say   that
 Revanasiddha himself, as’ tradition tells us, was afterwards
 known as Kadasiddha. because he achieved the highest goal
 of the spiritual life by practising penance in ‘Kada’ mean-
sing forest in Kannada language. Since not much is known
 about the lives of Revanasiddha and Kadasiddha from his-
torical records, we are forced to depend upon the available
songs by them which show their spiritual eminence.


 Songs by Revanasiddha

     In the first song, he says that it is a great fortune to be
 born in India, and greater still to be a devotee of the Lord
 Sankara. He exhorts us to have the vision of a bright star
 in the middle of the two eyebrows and be merged in the
light of it after abandoning the consciousness of our body.
In addition there will emerge several other supra-sensuous
 experiences like the lightning, the blazing fire and colours
 such as, green, yellow and red from the thousand-petalled
 lotus situated at the top of the brain.           One should get his
 mind engaged either in observing them or in listening to
_ the unstruck,   melodious sound which also is heard simul-
14       Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

taneously. The mind will then cease to fly from one object
to another; for, the objects of sensuous knowledge also will
make no effect upon the soul. Notwithstanding these supra-
sensuous experiences, one should be constantly merged in
the meditation on the Paramatman, who is at once exist-
ence, consciousness and bliss.


     In the second song Revanasiddha describes the splend-
our of the beautiful pearl which is like fire concealed in the
wood and which can be seen only on account of the grace
of the Guru. But no sooner is he graced by the Guru than
he sees this brilliant pearl even in pitchy darkness and
wherever he happens to cast his glance. None can see this
pearl without undergoing spiritual discipline of meditation
and without transcending the three gunas of sattva, raja
and tama.     There is neither any rising nor any setting of
this sun of Reality in the form of this pearl which is beyond
all scriptural knowledge.   One who has softened his stone-
like heart by means cf meditation can alone possess this
pearl.



A Song by Kadasiddha


     We menticn only a solitary poem of Kadasiddha which
is of extraordinary spiritual value. He asks the learned men
of his time if they have seen Brahman which is the original
cause of existence and which yields unlimited bliss. Sow
the unbroken seed of the divine name imparted by the Guru
deep in your heart and then reap the abundant harvest of
the spiritual experiences, says he. He asks us to discern
the six formidable mountains in the form of the six enemies
of man, namely, desire, anger, infatuation, greed, arrogance,
and jealousy. by being content by listening to the unstruck
sounds and the words which might come out of them, and
above all by looking at the form of God which is unknown
and unknowable to the world.
Shri   NARAYANRAO alias NIMBARGI MAHARAJA

     Section 8: NARAYANARAO ALIAS BHAUSAHIB -
                  MAHARAJA OF NIMBARGI

१.   Life

      We now come to a real historical person by name,
Narayanarao Maharaja of Nimbargi. The life-history of this
great saint will not be complete unless we refer to a very
important episode in his life, namely, that of his spiritual
initiation just as the description of the river Ganga at
Bhsikesa and Hardwar will not be adequate unless we
describe it from its very source namely, Gangotri.*

      It was in the year 1790 A. D. that this great saint was
born at Sholapur        (Maharashtra).    He was Lingayata by
caste, and as the custom required, a Siva-linga was tied on
his person on the fifth day of his birth.        But it signified
more than the customary ritual, It is said that the person
who performed the ceremony was the saint Kadasiddha in
the guise of a Lingayata Sadhu.          Whatever might be the
truth in the popular belief, the metallic Sivalinga proved
to be in the later life of the saint an harbinger of the illu-
minational column of light (and this is the real meaning of
the word ‘Linga’) which he used to see constantly before
his eyes.

      Well-built, strong, fair, tall and intelligent as the boy
was, Narayanarao was very fond cf playing with boys of
his age, and very often he had to face his angry father for
reaching home late in the evening. This habit of going late at
night continued even after his teenage was over. So once
his father became very angry with him. Narayanarao took
it to his heart and left his home without letting know any-
one about it, and straightway went to Pandharpur, the seat
of Lord Vitthala. There he fasted for three days, and pray-


*    Gangotri is the visible place where the river Ganga takes its
     rise, though beyond Gangotri there may be another place
     which is the real source of Ganga. But that piace is generally
     believed to be inaccessible.
16    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


ed to the deity.   On the third night, he was advised in a
dream that   he should   visit   Siddhagiri.   He   started   the
next day from Pandharpur with the intention of going to
Siddhagiri. The way to Siddhagiri is via Mangalvedha and
Nimbargi. At Gopalpura, at a distance of about two miles
from Pandharpur, he met an old man who told him that
he was going to Mangalvedha. As Narayanarao also had to
go to Mangalvedha they both became companions of each
other and began their journey together. The old man was
walking very fast    and Narayanarao was following him.
Anyone would have expected that the distance between
the two would be greater and greater in as much as the old
man was walking very fast. Narayanarao was however sur-
prised to find that the distance remained constant, inspite
of his endeavour to over-take the old man.     There was also
another surprise for Narayanarao.      Mangalvedha is at a
distance of about eighteen miles from Pandharpur. Natur-
ally one would require four to five hours. to reach that
place, but both of them reached there in much           shorter
tirne. As it became dark, after the close of the evening, the
old man took leave of Narayanarao asking him not to forget
him and told him that he was in the service of the Mamlat-
dar there, On enquiry, however, Narayanarao found that
there was no such man in the service of the Mamlatdar. He
was therefore all the more puzzled as to who that old man
might have been.   During night when he was asleep, some-
one told him in his dream that the old man was none else
but the Lord of Pandharpur. Surely we think that this
grace of God came upon Narayanarao on account of the
purity of his mind. Afterwards he went to Siddhagiri with
the plea that his young daughter’s locks of hair were to be
cut before the deity at Siddhagiri in accordance with the
traditional custom of his family. There at Siddhagiri after
his obeisance before the deity, he happened to pass by a
small cave near the temple when all of a sudden he saw an
old sage calling him by the gesture of his hand. Not knowing
whether he was called or some one else, Narayanarao did
              Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life             i7

    not go forward towards him. But seeing the old man calling
    him a second time by the movement of both of his hands.
    Narayanarao went towards him hurriedly with curiosity.
    The old saint, Muppinamuni, led him down inte the cave
    and initiated him into spiritual life by imparting unto him
    a Name of God on which he was instructed to meditate.

        Narayanarao returned to Nimbargi, but for five or six
    years he did not pay attention to meditation, To remind
    him that he must meditate on the divine Name, Muppinmuni
    came to Nimbargi. Narayanarao invited him for dinner,
    and offered him a quarter of a rupee. But the saint asked
    him to give two rupees. Narayanarao managed to do so
    with some difficulty. The saint, however returned the two
    rupees to Narayanarao and told him to spend one rupee to
    maintain himself and his family, and the other for devo-
    tional purposes. He also assured him that the grace of God
    is competent to fulfil his desires even of the mundane life.
    As a result of this, Narayanarao began to devote all his
    time and energy in the meditation on the Name of God. He
    did so with all the zeal for thirty-six years without any
    sign of fatigue. Verily, it is a great surprise that he could
    keep his spiritual endeavour a guarded secret even from
    his nearest relatives. He was always the first to go to bed
    and the last one to get up. However, unknown to others, he
    would sit for meditation as scon as all others were asleep
     and keep on meditating till it was time for others to get up.
     This device of his succeeded in keeping even his wife
     ignorant about his remaining awake for meditation
     throughout the night. The motive of behaving in this man-
     ner cannot certainly be to deceive anyone; it was to keep
     himself away from the possible attacks of egoism and pre-
     tense which would have totally undone what he had done
     and had achieved spiritually by the grace of his Guru. A
     fruit which is protected by foliage can become ripe on the
    ‘tree; otherwise, even while it is raw it may either be pluck-
     ed by an impatient person or pecked by birds. Even so
`    Narayanarao believed with conviction that meditation had
18    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

to be protected from undue exposure in order to allow it to
ripen into the realization of God. It came out to be literally
true after a long and hard penance of thirty-six years.

     The next twenty-eight years, he spent both in doing
his meditation and in imparting spiritual instructions to
others in accordance with his own mature wisdom. During
this period he came to be known as Nimbargi Maharaja.
His disciples were very few in number. He used to spend a
couple of hours in explaining the hidden spiritual meaning
of the verses in the ‘Dasabodha’ and the ‘Manobodha’
 (Verses addressed to the mind) of the saint Ramadasa to all
those who were present, whether disciples or not, urging
upon them the necessity of meditating on the Name of God
and of reading spiritual literature daily for fixed periods
and at the appointed time.

     As described above nights provided an excellent op-
portunity to him for doing his meditation for continuous
long hours. He wished to do the same by day also and so
in order to achieve the objective he gave up the hereditary
profession of a dyer in which he had made a name for the
sake of meditation and accepted the occupation of a she~
pherd. Every day, early in the morning, he along with two
or three boys, took his flock of sheep to graze in the neigh~-
bouring low-lands and charging the boys to take care of the
sheep, he used to retreat to some lonely place and meditate
till the sun went down the horizon.

     We narrate here a few incidents which occurred in his
life. Once after having taken his bath in a deep well, as he
was coming up by a very narrow path, a huge cobra of agg-
ressive variety was seen by him coming down by the same
narrow path. Being aghast with fear at the sight, the saint
stood where he was, closed his eyes and surrendering him-
self to God, meditated on His Name.  A short while after-
wards, when he opened his eyes the serpent was no where
to be seen.   Being grateful to God he composed a poem
          Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life          {9

which says that an unflinching faith in God which expresses
itself in the meditation on His Name will save a man even
from the jaws of death.

   Another great and noteworthy incident which occurred
in his life-time became the turning point in the Hfe ‘oi
Raghunathapriya who later became a very devout disciple
of the Nimbargi Maharaja. Once a yogi from the Andhra
Pradesh by name Raghunathapriya alias Sadhubuva came
to. Sonagi, a village near Nimbargi. Although born in a
very wealthy family, he had renounced the world, visited
several places of pilgrimage, and had done a great penance.
It was said about him that whatever he spoke used to turn
out to be true, and that he had several other miraculous
powers. Naturally, his fame spread far and wide, and when
he was residing in the temple of Hanumana at Sonagi, peo-
ple from the neighbouring villages thronged there to get
themselves cured of their ailments and diseases. The sadhu
used to provide food to everyone who came to see him. He

performed several miracles such. as bringing sight to the
blind and making the lame walk. Soon did the fame of the
sadhu reach the ears of Nimbargi Maharaja also who being
pressed by his disciples went to Sonagi to pay a courtesy
visit to the sadhu. Notwithstanding that the sadhu did not
pay any attention to him Nimbargi Maharaja asked the
sadhu as to why he was distributing food and curing the
diseases of the people. “Because it brings on great merit
(Punya)” replied the sadhu. “Do you see this punya, and
how much of it have you collected till now?” inquired
Nimbargi Maharaja. Whereupon the sadhu became non-
plussed and could not reply. Impressed by the import of
the question, Sadhubuva became curious to know more
about Nimbargi Maharaja. Before Nimbargi Maharaja and
others returned to Nimbargi the sadhu shrewdly obtained
all information about Nimbargi Maharaja from his disciples,


   Not Jong after this, one day, early in the morning
Sadhubuva went to the place where Nimbargi Mahar
                                                  aja
20       Prof. R. D. Rariade and His Spiritual Lineage

used to go for meditation, in order to verify for himself
what the disciples had told him. Nimbargi Maharaja came
and sat under a tree.     Unknown to     Nimbargi   Maharaja
Sadhubuva too took his seat on a branch of a neighbouring
‘Neem’ tree. As usual, Nimbargi Maharaja got up from the
meditation in the evening and saw the sadhu, who too
had then come down from the tree.        Nimbargi Maharaja
learnt from the sadhu that he had remained on the tree for
the entire period. “What did you eat when you might have
got hungry at noon?” asked Nimbargi Maharaja. “I ate the
bitter leaves of the tree” came the reply. “How could you
eat the bitter leaves ?” asked Maharaja.    “I am quite used
to satisfy my hunger in this way” answered Sadhubuva.
“This can be done by camels also but would that be enough
for realizing God?”     was the prompt rejoinder from the
saint.    Immediate was the reaction.   Sadhubuva prostrated
at the feet of the saint and implored to save him.       The
compassionate saint showed his      grace   by imparting the
hame of God to him and told him to meditate upon it.
Embarking upon the novel but the real path of the spiritual
life, Sadhubuva stayed for some time at Nimbargi, but soon
moved to Umadi and stayed there for twelve years doing
his meditation regularly. It was through this Sadhubuva
that Nimbargi Maharaja initiated afterwards, Bhausahib
Maharaja of Umadi and Ramachandrarao Yaragathikar of
Cimmada.      Bhausahib Maharaja however used to say that
his   Guru was    Nimbargi   Maharaja and not    Sadhubuva,
though he had received the spiritual instruction from the
latter.

    It was unfortunate that the wife of the Patil (headman)
of Nimbargi used to hate Nimbargi Maharaja. Seeing that
this is a favourable circumstance, some local temple priests
known as ‘Guravas‘ took her with them and instigated one
evil-minded pseudo-saint, known as Kanaka to tease and
persecute the Maharaja. Accordingly Kanaka approached
the saint and abused him for allowing the Brahmins to fall
at his feet although he was himself of a lower caste. “If
            Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life          2]

you are a true saint then come with me in a room, and let
us get it latched from outside. Whoever would come first
out of the room without unlatching the doors is a true
saint” said he to Nimbargi Maharaja. Twice did the saint
say to the mischievous Kanaka that he had neither mira-
culous powers, nor did he ever claim to be a saint. This
encouraged the unholy person to be all the more insistent
upon carrying out his test of saintliness. As the Maharaja
irritatedly gave his consent to get himself confined along
with Kanaka, the latter withdrew and went his way on
the plea that he would come the next day. Nimbargi Maha-
raja felt very sorry about the entire incident and standing
before the deity said, “O Lord, what have I done that this
wicked man should abuse me ? Do not remain concealed in
the idol of stone. Come out and do the justice.” It is said
that Nimbargi Maharaja heard the unstruck sound of a
conch, as an indication that God had heard his prayer and
that He would do the justice.     Kanaka was to leave Nim-
bargi for some business the next morning. But at midnight
he suffered from a severe attack of cholera. Thinking that
this was the divine punishment for annoying and insulting
a great saint, some disciples of Kanaka approached the
Maharaja and begged of him to be merciful 10 ` Kanaka.
Filled with compassion, Nimbargi Maharaja told the dis-
ciples to mount the patient on a donkey and parade him
through the streets to the southern side of the village, as
doing this was considered to be most humiliating. This be-
ing done, Kanaka was saved.

     Never did the Maharaja neglect his duty to the mem-
bers of his family, but as the main purpose of his life be-
came the realization of God, he had to get himself free
from the burden of carrying on the house-holder’s duties
one day or the other. He did not renounce the world and
became a wandering “Jangama” as the Lingayata Sadhu is
 called.   He stayed in the family, but only as a stranger or
a guest. As his sons became able to manage the house-hold,
he handed over that responsibility to them and never again
22      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

looked into their affairs.    How he behaved on a particular
occasion justifies what we have observed.       A certain lady,
Bhimabai by name, had lost her golden bangles. When her
brother who was an Inspector of Police inquired of her if
she suspected anyone, she hesitatingly told-him-that the
old    man,   meaning     thereby    Nimbargi   Maharaja   lived
in the adjacent room and that he used to give discourses
daily on the passages of the ‘Dasabodha’ and that she won-
dered if he was involved in the theft. Calling the Maharaja
at the police station, the Inspector told him respectfully
that a search should be conducted at his house. Nimbargi
Maharaja took the Inspector to his house, but when the
search was made in the presence of Bhimanna, the eldest son
of the Maharaja, the Inspector was surprised to find that
innumerable ornaments of gold and silver were lying un-
cared for on the floor of the central apartment of the house
and so he     concluded    that a person possessing so much
wealth would hardly think of stealing petty bangles.        So,
approaching the Maharaja, he apologised and left without
making any further inquiry.         The Maharaja however said
to the Inspector. “I am very thankful to you for making me
know that my son has become very rich. I never knew that
he possessed so many ornaments of gold and silver.” Such
was the unpretended unconcern of the great saint after
he had once mentally renounced everything in the world.

      Let it not however be supposed that this unattachment
to the external objects of the world was allowed by the
saint to go in vain for, it did enable him to be more and
more steady on the path of the Spirit. Nimbargi Maharaja
used to perform his arati (waving of the lights) by burn-
ing some camphor on a coconut.         One day the wife of the
village chief approached the Maharaja and said to him
angrily. “Are you not ashamed to allow your Brahmin dis-
ciples to disregard the deity and burn the camphor before
you ?”   So saying she kicked away the coconut on which
camphor was lighted.      The Maharaja could not tolerate this.
He said to her. “Till now you were disrespecting my body.
           Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life             23
                                                      १       >


ने did not mind 1081. But today, you have insulted God.
Let us wait and see the consequences.” Shortly thereafter
her, family lost the headmanship of the village, suffered
sheavy financial losses and her condition became more and
more miserable in every respect. Having totally lost the
social and the financial status, she became a butt of ridicule,

     Narayanarao was known for his expertise in dyeing.
Other dyers used to come to him to make their dyes per-
fect and enduring. He used to do it with great pleasure
but did not charge anything for his service.

    When the father was on death-bed, the younger brother
of Nimbargi Maharaja complained that the only thing they
will inherit from their father is a huge debt.     Thereupon
Nimbargi Maharaja said to him.       “Do not worry about it,
I shall clear it off without putting any burden on you”. He
however, gave his share of father’s property to the brother
and cleared off the debt by himself in course of time.


2.   The   views of Nimbargi Maharaja on the relationship
     between morality and spiritual life.

     Nimbargi Maharaja is not interested in giving us an
ethical theory which may be applicable to the mankind as
a whole, but in stating simply that morality is necessary
> an indispensable requisite for the realization of God.
His view on morality should, therefore, be restricted to the
aspirants of spiritual life. What happens to a soul after
death is not at all his concern, nor is he interested in pre-
vious lives of man.   What a man can achieve morally and
spiritually in this very life is the only important thing
according to him.


     One might be tempted to compare this view of Nimbargi
Maharaja to that of the great German idealist, Kant and
say that Nimbargi Maharaja is superior to Kant in as much
as he recognizes that the future is uncertain and the past
is dead and gone. But even this comparison is irrelevant
24         Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


because the ethical problem before Kant is to show how
morality is categorically imperative, whereas for Nimbargi
Maharaja ethics though necessary, is only a handmaid of
spiritual life. As Professor Ranade sums up...... a mystic
without morality, if such a one were passable, might only
be a hideous creature who is a blot on the spiritual evolu-
tion of man.’”*

      It is   on this sound philosophic background that we
shall note some sound        and very   outstanding utterances
of the saint indicating mature thought and insight :—

      1.    The pricking of even a thorn cannot take place, if
the conduct is good; nor will there be any misery even in
a dream.       If the conduct is good, not even the combined
powers of Brahmadeva, Visnu and Sankara can do you any
harm.       On the other hand, if the conduct is bad, not even
the three great divinities can save- you from their evil
effects.                                                      `

      2.    Diseases, calamities and dangers are as if, numerous
weapons in the hands of God; but what can they do to
you if they are not wielded by God.

      3.    People will behave with you in accordance with your
behaviour with them.        Therefore, it will be for our own
good, if we behave with them without any deceit or arro-
gance, and       treat them with humility, respect and love.
As there is one immanent Atman in all, whatever we speak
or do with reference to others we do it with reference to
the Atman only, and so we get back what we give as if
it is an echo of our voice or words.

    4. There is nothing worse than anger in us, which,
therefore, must be avoided at any cost. One becomes angry
with his children and scolds them in order to improve them.
This is alright. But the anger outside should contain love

*    R. 1. Ranade: A Constructive Survey of Upanisadic philosophy
     —2nd Ed., 1962, page 211 (Published by Bharatiya Vidya
     Bhawan).
                 Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life         25

    inside, so that physical punishment or very bitter words
    will naturally be avoided. Pride for anything, whether for
    secular or spiritual reason is suicidal.   It destroys itself
    after destroying the thing or the experience for which one
    becomes proud. Likewise, it is greed, lust and infatuation
    that make a man do an unjust thing but the unseen conse-
    quence of it will be most painful. The excuse that the un-
    just or immoral act was done inadvertently would not save
    one from the consequence. For, a burning. coal is equally
    painful whether it is trodden consciously or unconsciously.
    in showing that He is just, God has to become as merciless
    to the unholy as He is full of mercy for the just and the
    good, If one is really anxious that justice should be done,
    why should he be anxious about his future? He ought to
    be certain that nothing unjust will ever happen in the
    kingdom of God.      The only desire he might have is as to
    when he will be graced by God, and when he would have
    some experience of God

        5,   This kind of anxious desire for having the grace of
    God will, however, be fulfilled only when one would pray
    to God to control his mind and the senses by employing
    them in the service to God.      No doubt, there may arise
    occasions when the devotees of God are tested.       Abuses
    might be hurled at them, yet there should be neither re-
    taliation nor any change in the attitude of love. One should
    suffer in the name of God and continue to have full faith
    in Him. Verily, the saints endure the insults given by the
*   people and do not allow the equipoise of their mind to be
    disturbed. The recompense of this is not far to seek. God
    showers his grace all the more on a saint who suffers for
    Him,


       , 8. One should not be lethargic and postpone doing his
    duties. The moment sleep, idleness or infatuation overtakes
    him, that very moment he should nip it in the bud and not
    allow it to grow. This constitutes his daily sacrifice. Never
    should he tell a lie, excepting on an occasion when he would
26        Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

save the life of another person.         One should be prepared
to lose everything that belongs to him for the sake of truth.
For, what is lost will then certainly be bestowed by God
again: Likewise, one ought to feel compassion for all. One
cannot be said to have shown compassion for his pet dogs
by being merciless to the birds which he kills and feeds to
the dogs. Ahimsa cannot be based on himsa, nor can truth
upon falsehood.     To do anything which will hurt the feel-
ings of others constitutes in fact, what is known as himsa.


     7.    To counteract tendencies towards doing harm to
others, one should devote his time and energy in doing
one’s duty, be contented with whatever is given by God, and
serve with heart and soul God, his devotees and the elderly
good people. One who does not do his duty well and con-
scientiously, is not content with what he has earned by the
sweat of his brow and feels loss of dignity in serving the
saints and devotees, will hardly get anything of spiritual
worth.     One should feel that he is a servant only while he
is doing his duty, that the doing of duty is itself the ser-
vice to God, and then alone he should consider himself
worthy of enjoying what he has earned, no matter how
small, and that he should enjoy that like a King.         To dis-
charge the duty honestly without idleness or hypocrisy is
itself    a pleasure-yielding pursuit.    Besides,   not only the
employer but God also will be pleased thereby.

     8.    This is the way how the doing of works will lead a
man gradually to become devoted to God and the saints.
This is the way which will teach him to find the spiritual
life even while he is doing his routine duties by offering
them to God. And this is the way which will teach him to
meditate on the Atman, to visualize the Atman, and do his
works in His presence. In course of time, his grip on the
worldly pleasures will be loosened, and gradually he will
be merged day and night in the contemplation of the Atman.
With God on his side, he will easily conquer his interrial,
ehemies such as desires, lust, anger, arrogance, and malice,
          Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life                27


and soon find everlasting and unadulterated joy and the
peace divine.

    In short, man should consider that the human life is
not meant for enjoying the worldly pleasures, but that it is
an opportunity to realize God.     First of all he should ap-
proach a great saint who has realized the Atman and be
initiated in the spiritual fold by obtaining a divine Name
from him for meditation.     He should then meditate upon
that Name for three to four hours daily without letting
anyone know about it. None will know that one is meditat-
ing if one mentally utters the Name along with the breaths
which he inhales and exhales, and none will know that
one is looking at the Atman even if he is physically engag-
ed in doing some work. This will make him more and more
disgusted’ with the sensuous pleasures of life and induce
him to dévote more and miore of his time in silent meditation
and in looking at the various forms and light of the Atman.
Man is but a servant of God, so he should do all his works
in the presence of God and as He would like them to be
done. If the vision of God goes away even a        little this or
that side, he should know that the particular work he
wishes to do is not to the liking of God, and, therefore, he
should desist from doing it.   One should always wait upon
the Atman, and try to please Him by meditation and con-
templation upon Him.     To be in constant touch with the
Aiman either by meditation or contemplation, or to be in
the company of those who have realized the Atman is what
is meant by being in the company of the good and the holy,
an ideal which all the Hindu saints in India have stressed

upon.   To experience identity between the soul and the
Atman, and to experience the identity between the Atman
and the Brahman are, in short, the twin spiritual ideals
5९६ before us by the saint of Nimbargi.      But these cannot
be achieved unless man has first gained victory—over his
Senses and mind.   What the saint believed firmly, and what
he has left to his followers is this twofold discipline, namely,
morality on the one hand and spiritual exercise,
                                                   on the other.
28     Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


One should either choose both or lose both for, there is no
compromise.

3.   Important songs of the saint :

     The composition of a few songs is attributed to this
great saint.   Mr. Manoharpant Deshpande of Athani has
translated them very ably in Marathi from the original
Kannada and we have borrowed some of them which are
so impressive and pregnant with meaning that we cannot
resist the temptation of quoting them here :—

     Already a reference has been made to a song which
tells us that the Name of God has the power to save us from
the jaws of death. Nimbargi Maharaja became aghast with
fear and stood motionless at the sight of a huge cobra, but
he began to meditate.    The cobra disappeared.   This may
strike anyone as a miracle, and as due to the power of the
grace of God. The courage and confidence in God he showed
at that critical moment is remarkable and that was certain-
ly the fruit of his devout meditation and faith in the sav-
ing power of God who is consciousness and bliss at the
same time.

     The three other occasions already discussed in the life
of the saint, namely, when Kanaka abused and insulted
him, when the wife of the Patil of Nimbargi not only insult-
ed him but also the God whom the saint worshipped and
when the Inspector of Police searched his house, ought to
convince us that the faith, the courage, the endurance and
the calmness of the mind were all due to his most sincere
devotion and to God’s acceptance of the same with delight.
That God understood his anguish and showed him the sign
by means of the unstruck sound of a conch, ‘can be appre-
ciated only when one has the capacity to understand that
there exists a living tie of love between the devotee and
God.

     We wonder if the saint’s other songs also have some
reference to other incidents in his life or in the lives of
           Fountain-Heads of the Spiritual Life .         29

others.. But we give them here because we think that they
are very valuable from the ethico-spiritual view-point.
    In one song the saint implores God to come to his help
and control his wayward mind saying, ‘Day in and day out,
1 have given myself to censure others including the saints
and in depriving them of their wealth. Putting on the dress
of a sannyasin, I started to visit the holy Kashi, but on my
way, I met a prostitute and remained with her only. I
flatter the rich and abuse the poor; I have forgotten you,
Oh, my Lord; but now that I am tired of wandering hither
and thither through wilderness, come and save me soon.’

    ‘Why are you so drowned in anxiety? Is not God near
you ? Has He not given motion to the Sun and.the moon £
Has he not protected you till now since you were in your
mother’s womb ? Who creates the world, who dissolves it ?
Remember, God is omnipresent, here, there and every-
where.’                                  हु

    ‘Do not say that you are stone-hearted. You do not know
that it is very soft inside; you do not know that it contains
within it the spring of ambrosial juice; you will find that
it is nothing but the wish-yielding tree or the Parasa stone
which transmutes iron into gold,    provided, however, you
surrender yourself to the spiritual Teacher and are sin-
cerely devoted to-him.’

     ‘When you are with me, O Lord, how can I be in strange
lands; how shall I be poor indeed ? I know you are eternal
and imperishable; and on that very account I am fearless.
You are my, real parents and relatives; you are in front of
me and at the same time behind me; you are the giver of
all good things; why should I then worry whether my de-
sires will be fulfilled or not ?

    ‘Not at any time have I been away from you, my Lord..
You are without any definite form; I am without any emo-
_tional changes.   I bow down before you, because you have
now assumed the form of a Person; you have pervade
                                                   d the
30         Prof. R. D..Ranade and His Spiritual -Lineage

universe; but am I not in that very universe? Saints say
that Pranava (Omkara) is your form; so it is that I medi-
tate upon Om, You are known as Gurulinga-jangama; but
Iam not different from you, O Lord.’

4.    Spiritual advice of Nimbargt ‘Maharaja :
      1.    To seek happiness in the wordly life is like expect-
ing to irrigdte.
              by the waters of a mitage.-                    ^

      2,    The real happiness and ‘the ‘Summum bonum’ of life
lies in surrendering oneself to a realized Guru, in keeping
oneself morally good and in being befriended by the Atman

      3. ‘Let my conduct be morally good,’ ought to be the
constant prayer to God and then there would be no calamity
at all                                              शि               ~




      4.    To follow in the footsteps ofthe saints constitutes
the moral goodness                                               ॥

      5.    Remember God after bidding good-bye. to idleness
and anxieties                     ;           = *        ~               ॥

      6,    Meditation on the divine Name’acts as an effective
weapori iit fighting with the vices.

      7.    Meditation on the Divine      Name along..with the per-
ception of the Atman alone will be useful and nothing else.

      8.    One must have absolute control over. his. senses.

      9.    Be content with what God has given you and do not
hanker after the possessions of others

     10.    You can have spiritual life even while you are en-
gaged in the Samsara, i.e. activities of the worldly -life.

     11.    Kingdom of the Self, means the imperishable bliss
of the Atman

     12.    Sadguru is he who has identified himself with the
concealed vision of God.              a

                        CHAPTER     II

     SRI BHAUSAHIB MAHARAJA OF UMADI
                      Section A: LIFE


    There is a village by name Umadi in the District of
Bijapur in Karnataka.   In that village Bhausahib Maharaja
was born on Ramanavami day in Sake 1765 (1843 A.D.) in
the well-to-do family of Deshpandes. His two elder brothers
were Nanasahib and Dajiba. Both of them were initiated by
Raghunathpriya Sadhu Maharaja. As Sri Bhausahib Maha-
raja was accustomed from his early boy-hood to worship the
deity of Hanumana he could not appreciate the Nirguna
bhakti of his elder brothers.   Whenever he used to meet
Raghunathpriya Sadhu Maharaja, the latter used to praise
Bhausahib’s devotion to Hanumana and bow down before
him. This was exactly like what a great wrestler does with
a novice and gets himself deliberately defeated by him.      It
was in this manner that’ Bhausahib was drawn by Raghu-
nthpriya Maharaja to the real spiritual path, a fact which
Bhausahib himself expressed with         gratitude   on several
occasions.   Naturally, in course of time, he came to meet
Nimbargi Maharaja who could foresee the potentiality of a
great spiritual power in the person of Bhausahib Maharaja.
He, therefore, got him initiated by Raghunathpriya Sadhu
Maharaja, a Brahmin by birth, perhaps because being a
Lingayata by caste, Nimbargi Maharaja did not wish to in-
cur the displeasure of the public at large for initiating a
Brahmin.     Subsequently, an offer had come from the Gov-
ernment to Bhausahib Maharaja for the post of a Revenue
Officer, but he gave up the idea of accepting the post and
carried on his spiritual activities, which to him was the
only important thing.   Since then he devoted almost all his
time and energy in meditating on the name of God and
succeeded on account of it both in the spiritual and the
worldly life. In fact, his success in the worldly life was
nothing but the result of his success in the spiritual life.
                                                                                  |




                                                 ण्य



                                                 ae        “४४
