          SHRI     BHAUSAHIB                    MAHARAJA

                             OF          UMAD!

Birth              : Umadi ( Karnataka )

Ram Navmi: Sake 1765 (1843 AD)
Death              : Thursday, Magh Sud 3
                    Sake 1835
                    26th January 1914
                                                                          ’




               Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                      33


Or, one may say that his worldly life was thoroughly per-
meated by the life spiritual. To an external observer it
appeared as if he lived two lives, one spiritual and the other
worldly, but in fact he lived only one life, namely, the
Spiritual. He did not neglect the duties which came to his
lot according to the customary rules and practices which
prevailed in the household. He could thereby win the sym-
pathy and affection of all of his relatives and friends. In
turn, he used to oblige them all. The result was that people
began to gather in large numbers around him and waste
his time in talking about worldly affairs. Bhausahib became
so much tired of the people that he began to avoid them by
going outside the village in order to meditate. Meditation,
reading of spiritual literature and bhajans three times a
day, discussion on spiritual matters, giving instruction to
others and meeting the people by night only, so that a few
only would come and meet him, was his daily routine. All
the same, he did not allow his spiritual life to be shadowed
by his being busy with the people. His spiritual experiences
grew day by day and disgust for all Godless things was
on the increase.     His time for meditation also increased
gradually and he began to feel that the worldly life was but
a vain dream. People could not understand him and there-
fore they began to speak ill of him.             Some censured him
for having a non-brahmin as his Guru.             Some said that he
had come under the influence of black-magic which his
Guru exercised upon him, while some others said that his
spiritual endeavour was nothing but pretense. His in-laws
also sided with the general public and began to tease him
in many ways.       How could they understand the difference
between a true Brahmin and a brahmin by birth only ? How
could   they    understand    that   one   who     has   realized   the
Brahman     alone    is   a   Brahmin      and    not    others? How
could they understand that Tukarama, a Vaishya or Chow-
khamela, a cobbler, both of low castes, were the brahmins’
in the real sense of-the word and that all others who con-
34     Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

sidered themselves as brahmins, perhaps by reason of birth,
were not brahmins at all?

     Bhausahib Maharaja was of such a saintly character
that he did not mind people censuring or ridiculing him nor
was there any break in his meditation. His spiritual power
grew to such an extent that, on the one hand he felt ex-
treme love for God, and on the other he felt extremely
disgusted with the worldly life.   On one occasion when he
was sitting by the side of his Guru and meditating, a cer-
tain wild bee attacked and bored deeply in his lap. Bhau-
sahib did not move an inch from the posture he had taken
for meditation.    Many a time while he meditated outside
Umadi, scorpions and serpents used to climb on his body,
but he did not shake in the least. Let there be torrents of
rain and his body be completely drenched, let his body be
scorched by the sun, or let there be cuts in his body on
account of extreme cold, Bhausahib was not in the least
disturbed in his meditation. For eighteen years from twelve
to two every night he meditated for the spiritual good of
his disciples.   His love and service to Guru increased day
by day. On every Thursday he used to go to Nimbargi in
order to bow down at the feet of his Guru and serve him
in every way possible including undertaking menial tasks.
He did all this with great joy.


     Bhausahib was present when Nimbargi Maharaja pass-
ed away. He was extremely grieved at the loss. But as he
had received the orders from his Guru to spread the cause
of Paramartha, he began to meditate with increased zeal
throughout the day and night, and the fruit was near at
hand. With the realization of being one with Brahman, the
influence of Maya on him was completely wiped out. Bhau-
sahib thought of building a temple of his Guru at Inchgiri
in order that it may be a centre of spiritual knowledge, and
that it would help him to spread his Guru’s fame far and
wide. Moved by this noble idea he begged of Vitthala, the
God of Pandharpur    — ‘Let the devotion to God be on the
              Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                35

increase. I shall reach to your feet by covering the distance
with repeated prostrations.’

    As the grace of His Guru was fully upon him, and as he
x




was meditating with full faith, the fruit was obvious and
immediate. On the strength of spiritual experiences, he
travelled throughout the length and breadth of Maharashtra
and Karnataka awakening people to the real spiritual path.
He removed their miseries and ignorance, and so became as
revered as Saint Ramadasa. In more than one way, he was
able to confer divine grace on the people. He showed them
the way to please God by being devoted to Him. He taught
them how to meditate and do the bhajans, Accompanied by
his most faithful and foremost disciple, namely, Amburao,
he used to visit several places and create new and ever
new centres of spiritual enlightenment. His only mission
in life since the death of his Guru was to spread spiritual
knowledge as ordered by his Guru.

    He used to do meditation for nine hours a day and spend
about four hours a day in bhajans and reading of spiritual
literature. There was not a single exception to his activities
during thirteen years according to the diaries which he
maintained,    Whenever he had to go outside Inchgiri for
the propagation of spiritual life, he used to meditate in
advance and fulfil the requisite quota.     For spiritual pur-
poses, all were equal to Bhausahib, He made no distinction
between a Brahmin and a non-brahmin, rich and poor or
learned and illiterate. He firmly believed that the life divine,
had nothing to do with caste, creed, wealth and learning,
and that it would be the possession of all who put in the
necessary moral and spiritual efforts by being entirely de-
pendent on the grace of God.  He would, therefore, go with
equal ease and joy to meet a king in his palace or a pauper
in his thatched hut of mud.    If he found devotion anywhere
that was enough for him.    But so far as the practical life in
the daily affairs was concerned, he made distinctions bet-
ween individual and individual in deference to the preva-
36        Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


lant social customs.       For example, the brahmins and non-
brahmins were served food separately, the men of learning
were given due respect and no one was permitted to show
disregard      towards     the   elderly   persons.     However,   this
practice had     no    bearing on Bhausahib’s behaviour            with
people in spiritual matters. The moon never withdraws its
light from the house of a fallen man, as Madhvacarya has
said.    To behave with distinctions in the everyday worldly
life, and to behave without any distinctions in the spiritual
life was the unbreakable rule.          Integration and disintegra-
tion, unification and division were not the irreconcilable
instruments of bringing man together under the one banner
of spiritual life.


        Notwithstanding this, Bhausahib was keen enough to
distinguish the pretentious and the cheats from among the
seekers     of the    spiritual life.   If he   found    any   genuine
sadhaka who would like to meditate and do bhajans as
instructed by him, he would be so generous as to tell him
his own spiritual experiences, to others who joined the as-
sembly just to see what was going on or who were interest-
ed in mere verbal knowledge, Bhausahib never spoke a
word, but simply saluted and dismissed.           Large number of
people who merely heard his sermons, but were not his
disciples, could hardly know that the real spiritual life
consists in the meditation on the Name of God as imparted
by a powerful living Guru. They were interested in having
a verbal knowledge only. But whoseover went unto him for
being relieved of his distress, disease or ignorance, he used
to console him and remove his difficulties by giving him
counsel, medicine or the Name of God for meditation. Him-
self as we have said above, mediated for nine hours a day
at fixed hours and spent in addition three to four hours
daily in doing bhajans           and reading spiritual literature
thrice a day.        He never put his personal burden of any
kind on anyone, not even on his loyal and foremost disci-
ples.
               Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                37

    Never did Bhausahib Maharaja manufacture miracles,
but as Ramadasa says, miracles happened everywhere in-
asmuch as he had acquired a great punya (merit) of medita-
tion on the name of God. ‘To hanker after to possess or
to exercise miraculous powers is a blot on the spiritual
life’, he used to say very often. Nevertheless, God being
mercy incarnate, fulfils the desires of his devotees. The de-
ceptive and hydraheaded maya had no longer any power
over him. Reaching the pinnacle of spiritual life namely,
unison with God, his doubts were resolved, he became fear-
Jess and he was in possession of unlimited and unspeakable
bliss. Even then he did not cease to meditate. Bhaurao was
also known as Bhauraya. The word Bhauraya which con-
sists of four parts ‘Bha’, ‘u’, ‘ra’ and ‘ya’ can be ingeneously
interpreted as follows. The four parts respectively mean-
ing ‘shining with lustre,’ ‘auspicious and protective,’ ‘capable
of being bestowed,’ and ‘bringing about the union of the
soul and God’    stand ultimately for the Guru, the spirituai
teacher. So, the chief characteristics of the spiritual teacher
accordingly are that he must first shine by the lustre or
glory of God-realization; secondly he must be able to pro-
tect his disciples   from all sins, miseries, and dang-rs in
life; thirdly he must be capable of imparting the name of
God to his disciples; and lastly, he must be able to bring
about the union of his      disciples with   God, Verily, the
status of Bhaurao was precisely this.

   Indeed he was very fortunate in that even when he
was a boy of fourteen he was initiated by Nimbargi Maha-
Yaja and that he could live the life of the Spirit in the com-
pany of his guru for a period of next twenty-eight years
without any rest and without succumbing to the bodily
temptations.  How extraordinarily difficult for an ordinary
Ian it is to understand that Bhausahib used his body in
the service of the Almighty as if it were a fragrant paste
of the sandalwood.    Befitting the description of a saint -by
Saint Tukarama,      Bhausahib not only saved himself but
saved the entire families of those who surrendered unto
38     Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


him, One is tempted to ask if there can be any other saint
in the world like Bhausahib Maharaja who used to stand
for two hours from twelve to two in the night, meditating
and praying to God for the spiritual good of his disciples.


A few other noteworthy points are given below :—

     Most of his time was devoted to spiritual pursuits.   He
could hardly get any time for unnecessary discussions or
social activities.

     He was a man of few words.       Like the ancient sage,
Vasistha, he    also   did not believe in the   reality of the
phenomenal world which consists of nothing more than the
mixture of five elements.    Once a kerosene stove, a novelty
in those days, was brought by a gentleman to Inchgiri.
People of the village gathered round it and wondered how
the burning fire could be moved from place to place, and
how it did not require anything except Kerosene oil. Being
excited by that novelty, some of them approached Bhau-
sahib and requested him to see the stove.       Bhausahib did
not pay any attention to that but went      into his room for
meditation. When he was pressed by some of his disciples
to have a look at the stove, Bhausahib replied bluntly, “T
do not think it is made out of: any extra element beyond
the five elements known to us.     If anybody wants to see
something beyond the phenomenal world, let him meditate
and see the vision of God.”     So saying Bhausahib became
silent and began to meditate again.


     Professor Ranade used to say about Bhausahib that
whatever word he happened to utter came out to be true.
Ranade tells us his own experience, how on one occasion
when Bhausahib said to him at Inchgiri, “You will reach
Poona in the morning tomorrow,” Ranade did not wish to.
tell that he would reach at about 2 in the morning and so
he said, “Yes”. What happened afterwards at the Railway
Station, Hutagi, was that Ranade could not get entrance
             Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi              39,
in the Madras Mail and he had to go by the passenger
irain which reached Poona in the morning of the next day.

    On no oceasion did he speak even a white lie, Not only
did his words in the waking life came true, but whatever
he spoke or did in others’ dreams also came true, The well
at Nimbal had been abandoned as it did not strike water
and Professor Ranade was using the water of the well be-
longing to the Railway. One day at Allahabad, Professor
Ranade saw in his dream that Bhausahib was digging the
well with his own hands.     Forthwith the next morning
Ranade wrote a letter io Mr. Jagannath Lele at Nimbal to
dig the well deeper still. Within a few feet of digging there
gushed such a big spring of water that the well was full to
the brim within two hours. As water could not be stopped,
they had to partially plug the mouth of the spring in order
to control the flow of the water.

    When Professor Ranade was discharged from the civil
hospital in Poona because even the expert doctors there had
lost all hopes, he went to Inchgiri. As soon as Bhausahib
saw him, he said that he did not think that there was
any danger to his life for sixty years more.      And so it
happened,

    A certain man had to attend a court at Bijapur, but
when he left the Inchgiri Matha in the early morning,
he saw a widow coming in front of him with fire on a cow-
dung-cake.   Thinking that it was a bad omen, he returned
and waited for some time. When Bhausahib came to know
about this, hethrold the man that there was no better omen
than the one he had witnessed. As it was customary in those
days to call   a widow by the name, ‘Ganga-Bhagirathi’,
Bhausahib told the man that he had the darsana of the
pure Ganga and not of a widow, and that the fire which
he saw was nothing but Jnanagni, the fire of knowledge
and: that he would, therefore, have success in the case. It
happened exactly as Bhausahib had told. The court gave
the judgement in favour of the man.
40 ,    Prof. R. 2. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


       His judgment about men and about their nature was
precise and unerring.    When some of his disciples did not
understand why he loved Amburao more than anyone else,
he demonstrated before them the secret of it in order to
remove their doubts.     Assuming anger, he asked Amburao
in the presence of other disciples as to why he was sweep-
‘ing the temple at odd hours. Amburao stood where he was
and implored Bhausahib to pardon him. After a few minu-
tes again he asked Amburao, “Why are you just standing
there ? Will you not finish the work ?” With folded hands
Amburao apologized and replied, “Yes sir, I shall finish,”
and he began to sweep the ground again.       At this, Bhau-
sahib said to other disciples, “Just see, whereas you wish
to be treated with some regard, Amburao cares only for
the love of his Guru and does not mind his harsh and bitter
words.” Inordinate was his affection for Amburao,


       When on one occasion, Bhausahib was seriously ill on
account of fever, he stopped at Jigjivini on his way to
Umadi and asked to bring a cup of gruel prepared from
parched. grain of Javar. Bhausahib insisted that it should
be prepared at Amburao’s house and when he took that,
his fever came to normal before he reached Umadi.         He
then lived for ten years more for the sake of Amburao.


       He never did anything without the sanction of God. On
one occasion when he sent some holy ashes during the ill-
ness of Professor Ranade’s sister at Poona he did it after
he got some indication of the sanction from God.


    His love for truth was so great that he did not hesitate
to correct himself. He used to say very often that he had
no evidence to believe in rebirth. But on one occasion he
said to Professor Ranade, “I was saying till now that one
cannot say definitely whether there is rebirth or no. Today,”
I say that rebirth is a fact.” He was then looking at the
sky, as Professor Ranade had told us more than once.
                                                               1




            Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                  41
   He used to ride on a horse-back, but his attention was
always rivetted on, the vision of God and in meditating on
the Name of God. Once he said to Amburao Maharaja after
they had reached Inchgiri from Bijapur, “I was meditating
though I was on the horse-back, while you were looking
hither and thither.”

  To those who made any distinction between him and
Amburao he used to tell that they should rather try to
understand the common Spiritual life between the two,
which is not only between the two, but among all the saints
and that, therefore, they should not make any distinction
between a saint and a saint.

    It is peculiar that similar incidents happened in the life
of Amburao and Bhausahib Maharaja. Bhausahib Maharaja
could explain the meaning of passages in English, a langu-
age which he did not know, while Amburao explained the
meaning of a certain Upanisadic passage not knowing a
word of Sanskrit.      Both of them, however, explained the
mystery, as due to the power of God, who knew all the
languages   and   their meanings.      Anyone    who   meditates
throughout his     life   will have   also   similar experience.
Nothing is beyond the ken of .God.

    Seeing that Nimbargi Maharaja interpreted a parti-
cular passage from Dasabodha in four different ways on
four different occasions, Bhausahib Maharaja was struck
with wonder. Nimbargi Maharaja explained to him that
there was nothing wonderful in it for, a man who knows,
looks in all the directions, whereas         a man who does not
know, looks in one direction only.


     Section 5: DISCIPLINE IN THE SCHOOL OF
                          MEDITATION

    Sri Bhausahib Maharaja used to utilize the whole of
the month of Sravana for meditation every year, All those
who participated     used to meditate for nine hours everyday
42     Prof. R. 7. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

from six in the morning to three in the afternoon. Before
coming to the hall of meditation, everyone was expected
to have taken his bath. And when all had joined at               about
half past five in the morning they performed the first Arati
(waving of the light of camphor)              of the early morning,
known as the Kakada-Arati, which was supposed to dispel
the slothfulness of the body and mind, and so to invoke the
blessings of the Lord. The meditation was to be done along
with the ingoing and outgoing breaths in one’s own throat
or in mind withoul any articulate or audible sound.               One
may say that in this kind of meditation each individual
was concerned with himself only.            But it was not so.    For,
it   did   involve   the    collective   aspect of meditation    also..
Every one of the aspirants was expected to listen to the
spiritual passages         that were being read throughout the
nine hours by one who was                appointed for the purpose.
He was instructed to read every line, clearly, distinctly,
loudly and with rhythm, so that every one who listened
10 it would understand the entire meaning with ease.               As
every verse was read with precise speed and pauses which
were usually three or four, it afforded an opportunity both
for the reader and the listener to utter mentally the Name
of God three or four times during each verse. Thus, the
meditation done by every one was neither simply intellec-
tual, nor simply spiritual. It was both, intellectual so far
as the understanding of the passages that were read was
concerned, and spirtual so far as the repeating of the Name
was    concerned.     And     naturally, as    what was   read    was
through and through intended to have the moral bearing
on the minds of the reader and the listeners, the meditation
was necessarily moral and devotional. Thus it was how the
individual act of meditation became necessarily a collective
performance at the same time.

      This conclusion will come upon us with greater force
if we take into consideration the additional duties of the
reader. (1) At the end of every two hundred verses which
he was expected to finish in about one hour, he was to
              Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                      43

utter loudly some such Name of God as, ‘Hari Narayana
Guru Narayana’ so that all those who were in the assembly
would receive an additional stimulus to become more and
more devoted. (2) He had to have an eye upon every one
of the sadhakas with the view to awakening any one of them
if he was found to be dozing, by calling him by a secret name
was so that he should utter in response another secret name
indicating that he was full awake and not sleepy. If no res-.
ponse to the first call was available the reader was to make
a second call with his voice somewhat raised, but if that also
failed to get any response then he was to utter the divine
Name ‘Govind’ loudly in chorus with all others, as this
would certainly awaken the sleeping sadhaka. (8) After
every half an hour he had to announce the correct time.
 (4) He had also the duty of welcoming new comers and di-
recting them to sit in proper places according to their posi-
tion and dignity in society. (5) After having read about fifty
verses, he had to ask someone to sing such a song as would
.either increase    the     devotion or bring conviction      to   the
devotees     that   their    spiritual   experiences   are   genuine.
 (6)   And finally, he had to rememher that he was not
to leave his seat for nine hours, nor to remain silent for
even a few minutes, nor to show any sign of being tired,
or sleepy.

       Add to this meditation of nine hours, four or five hours
during which there would collectively be three bhajans in
the morning, in the afternoon, and at night. Thus where the
minds of all the participants are knit together through the
reader to the common search of God realization, and where
all of them become deeply engaged in meditation or contem-
plation with faith, love and regard for about thirteen or
fourteen hours a day for one full month, will it be too much
to say that God is amidst them? No wonder if Bhausahib
Maharaja had expressed the hope that all the participants
of that unique spiritual month-long activity would enjoy
perfect health, happiness, joy, and peace for the balance of
the year. There were several persons who had seen him
44     Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


shedding tears when the participants left Inchgiri after the
month was over. He used to go to the top of the building
and watch them from there till the last of the devotee be-
came invisible. The pangs of separation were too great for
him to bear.

     In the case of others who became egoistic on account
of their spiritual meditation or experiences and thought
in   their minds that they had- become as       great as their
teacher, he used to feel pity, because he thought that they
would suffer for being puffed up with pride.

     So far as the course of his own life was        concerned
Professor Ranade tells us that he did not find in the Maha-
raja’s personal diaries    of thirteen years a single day on
which he had not done the meditation for nine hours and
other spiritual activities like bhajans and discourses for
additional three or four hours a day.

     “My eyes become moistened with tears of joy, when I.
see a person with all his heart performing the mere ritual of
devotion to God.” This was how Bhausahib used to appre-
ciate the quality of      devotion in others, though he used
to keep silence throughout the month of Sravana.

     He was aware that it was neither possible nor desira-
ble for him to attend personally to all the details of the *
arrangements which were being made for making the de-
votional programme of the month a great success. ‘But, if
I do not do so,’ he used to say, ‘people would behave in any
manner they please, and that might be the cause of bring-
ing a blot on devotion itself.    He wished to have perfect
but smooth working in all the functions of the devotional
yajna (sacrifice). Therefore he wished that every man who
participated in that great sacrifice be necessarily an intelli-
gent, courageous, and obedient servant of God. If any one
was tired, he allowed him to go outside and then return to
meditate after taking rest for some time. But why should
the man sit in a slothful condition, look at others here and
             Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                 45

there and create disturbance to them? Himself being
tired he would make others also tired. To those who were
awakened from being sleepy during meditation, he used to
tell that they should be very thankful for this to the end
of their lives.
    It will not be out of place: to mention how Shri Bhau-
sahib Maharaja issued instructions to different disciples
om different occasions in order to ensure that proper disci-
pline was maintained. “How is it that you are making
jokes while people are devoting their time to meditation ४
Remember that you will be put to ridicule by all. Instead
of being humble and polite before the elderly persons, you
sic with them in a dignified posture and move in front of
them even while they are intently looking at the Arati (the
waving of lights). Be wise and respectfully bow down
before the devoted and the elderly persons,” he used to
say in a general way to all.

    He    told    all   that   everyone   should   protect   his
own kit in his room, tie down his horse or bull in a proper
place, and after performing the Arati along with others, sit
for meditation for six hours and not move from his place.
God will certainly take care of his devotees and protect
them in all possible ways.

     “Amburao told me,” said he once, that all the three
Aunappa, Krsanapa and Bhimappa had gone into the village
to do the work of accounts and so they did not attend the
muxlitational programme.       And now you tell me that you
had come here, but that I had made no inquiry about you.
What then is the truth ? Any way, stay here for eight days
and meditate in the company of all.”

     “And what about you, Nagappa? Do you wish to do the
reading tomorrow ? But in that case, read in advance what
you have to read tomorrow or the day after. Do not waste
your precious time; otherwise the greed and anxiety you
Teel for the objects of samsara will wipe away the joy of
meditation you might have done till now.”
46       Prof. R. 22. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


       To the reader he said, “How bad it is that you announc-
ed the time wrongly and did not notice that your watch
had stopped.”     That shows your attention was somewhere
else which explains why you were reading “Jnanesvari” and
“Dasabodha”      mechanically as if, and without correct pro-
nunciation
         of words.      When you do not yourself, understand
the meaning of words, how do you expect that the listeners
would understand what you read? Regarding the singing
of devotional songs there was no proper selection of them.
So, see that you correct yourself in every way and thereby
please all the people who have assembled here for the pur-
pose of devotion.    All of you, including Amburao, should
know that the responsibility of making the meditational
programme for one month a great success lies on each one
of you.     When each one would think that he is for all,
there will not arise any egoism in him.

       “Remember the Name of God with every morsel of
food which you eat, and do not speak a word till your din-
ner is over.    Let not your secrets be known to any one
except to God.     Nor should you be angry with any one,
but,    on the contrary, you should cultivate the habit of
enduring harsh words of others.       To become angry with
others or with oneself is the sign of Tamoguna which is not
at all desirable even for a great devotee.     Under the in-
fluence of Tamoguna, Gora Kumbhara, a great saint is said
10 have cut both of his hands, but when he wished to clap
his hands with joy at the time of doing Bhajana, he prayed
to God to bestow him again his two hands; this he did
under the influence of Rajoguna. What however is required
for maintaining Bhakti is the predominance of Sattvaguna
over the other two gunas, though Sattvaguna is, like the
other two gunas the cause of expanding the Upadhis, and to
that extent is the cause of bondage.      In a way however
all the three gunas are useful for devotion, Tamoguna and
Rajoguna in destroying what is harmful to Bhakti, and
‘Sattvaguna helped by Rajoguna in realizing the secret of
devotion, namely, the direct realization of God.     All this
          त     Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi.           AT

will however be clear to him who is intelligent and pre-.
pared to avoid the evil and accept. the good without, any
loss of time. Therefore, one who does not believe in the
distinctions of gunas as sattva, raja and tama will hardly
understand how the spiritual life of being one with the
Reality goes beyond sattvaguna itself. He will only move
in the realm of verbal knowledge, and without the realiza-
tion of God, will speak and write about'God and interpret
the scriptures in a learned manner only, thereby confus-
ing himself will confound the others..This has been the
way in which the ignorant and the egoistic fools have been
behaving since the ancient times.

    “Out of slothfulness and stupidity one should not there-
fore say that the reading and understanding of the scrip-
tures and the writings of the saints as well as Bhajans,
Arati, and meditation are all an idle pursuit. On the con-
trary, if he wishes to have a concrete proof of the value of
meditation on the Name of God imparted by the Guru, he
should meditate regularly, daily, intensely. and with regard,
faith, and love and see for himself that his body too be-
comes sound and healthy. He should go to bed soon after
the singing of the Bhajans at night and after enjoying a
sound sleep get up a couple of hours before sunrise and sitt-
ing in a steady and comfortable, meditative posture do the
meditation silently, In fact, he should guard his own interest,
and not allow himself to be deceived by what others might
thoughtlessly say, or by what he might himself imagine to
be true. For, to remember God is, in a way, to become
God, whereas to imagine and to indulge in inference is to
make room for illusion.

    And even during meditation, one should be carefui
neither to forget the Name, nor to change the direction of
his gaze from the vision of God he might be seeing before
him. He should make up his mind not to Jook even at the .
person who might have been sitting close to him for medi-
 tation, For, if the other person were to appear as tired, or
48        Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


lethargic or sleepy, he would himself be induced to be so
consciously or unconsciously; and thus the contagion of the
feeling of tiredness might spread over all the assembly.
If one were to feel tired, he should go out for a while, re-
fresh himself and join his companions in order to meditate
again.     If he were to feel sleepy, let him hold the inhaled
breath and meditate mentally in that condition for a few
minutes, so that he would be able to overcome the feeling
of drowsiness.     Let him do this practice of meditation for
six hours at a stretch for two weeks at least, and he will
soon come to have to some extent the signs of the presence
of God.      It would be desirable, though not necessary, to
have a bath before sitting for meditation, inasmuch as the
bath brings with it a bodily energy and freshness of the
mind which would be highly useful for carrying on the
meditation.     It will be wrong to say therefore that upadhis
like bath etc. are not of any relevance or value for devotion.
So long as the consciousness of the body persists, and so
long as devotion cannot be carried on without even the
minimum consciousnes of the body, the upadhis, however,
perishable they may be, are useful for enhancing the devo-
tion to God.


             Section   C: GLEANINGS OF WISDOM
     1.    A bunch of white hair of the tail of a horse is used
as one of the insignia of royalty and waved with respect
over the head of a deity. Apart from the custom does it
mean that the bunch of hair indicates any merit on its part ?
Implied meaning is that white hair in the bunch symboli-
cally represent the white hair of a lifelong loyal and de-
voted servant of God.    If the whole body of a devotee can
be considered as an ornament fit to adorn the Godhead,
much more valuable ought to be the white hair which
covers the best part of his body, namely, the head, and thus
the must auspicious object fit to be offered to God.

    2. A thief approached a saint and asked him whether
it would be possible for him to commit robbery without
               Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                      49

being arrested. “By then, I shall be held responsible for
the theft and punished severally,” complained the theif.
Thereupon the saint said, ‘In order to ward off this fear,
meditate on a mantra which I shall impart fo you.’ Being
satisfied with this assurance, the thief entered into the
palace of the King at night by telling the three guards,
one after another that he was a thief. The guards thought
that he must have been the guest of the king inasmuch
as no man would say that he was a thief, even though he
were really so. The thief saw a sturdy horse which was kept
for the purpose of hunting, which he stole and rode away.
On being enquired by the keeper of the horse as to who he
was, he told that he was a thief. But the keeper said in his
mind that no thief would say that he was a thief and like
the guard he too thought him to be a guest of the king and
that he was interested in seeing how far the horse was
trained.     Thinking    so,    he    helped   the    thief   to   ride
upon     the   horse.   Later    on    when    they    realized    that
the horse was stolen, the king sent his men after the thief.
The men of the king found the horse tied to a tree and
the thief sitting under it and meditating. But they did not
do anything because they were not sure whether that was
a stolen horse, The thief, however, after his meditation was
over repented for his act of theft and returning the horse
to the king never again thought of stealing anything. Thus
was he saved by resorting to truthfulness and meditation.


    3.     If anything has to be accomplished, it has to be
attended to minutely in every respect. You must have been
convinced of this, Amburao, when you were busy in having
a wall built in the Inchgiri math in spite of the opposition
from the village people.        Remember always that whatever
is done with a selfish interest which necessarily involves
the mind and the intellect arises out of the various interests
and upadhis (limitations) of the individual soul; but what-
ever is done without any selfish interest will be experienced
as the Grace of the Sadguru.           Hence, utmost alertness is
560   = Prof. R. 0. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


required for getting oneself uncontaminated by the upadhis,
in spite of his being in the midst of them.

      4,   One who has fixed his gaze on the vision of the
Atman and has become one with him may appear external-
ly as bound by the three gunas of Maya, as being conta-
minated by the affairs of the world; and as being extremely
busy in doing his duties and other various activities. But in
reality like the unborn, immortal Atman he ever remains
internally as unbound uncontaminated and inactive.        He
is the real servant of God who being one with Him is
ever loyal to Him, and so, he meditates on his Name with
utmost faith and love.

      5.   One who forgets his individuality totally whether
he is engaged in doing the duties of the world, or in doing
his daily spiritual exercise of meditation and contemplation
is alone entitled to be called a sannyasin. None becomes a
learned man in a day; nor one a saint. One must toil and
toil to make himself known whether it is in the worldly or
in the spiritual sphere. Greater still must be the labour, if
one wishes to realize that both the parts of life constitute
ofie indivisible Maya of God, and that therefore. the real
doer and the enjoyer in both is God and not the tiny, egcis-
tic individuality of man.   Thus, one who goes beyond both
these aspects of Maya is a real saint.   & this is however
possible only if one goes by the way pointed out by the
spiritual teacher.

    6. It is almost impossible to behave in this World with
absolute moral goodness so long as one clings to his body,
mind, senses, intellect and egoism, and pays scant attention
to know and realize the Atman. But is there then any norm
or standard of behaviour in the spiritual life? There is
none except perhaps the attitude with reference to which
the depth of the spiritual life might be measured. That
attitude is of love and faith. To give an instance of this,
Janabai the maid-servant of the great saint, Namadeva used
to receive the dish from which it is said both Namadeva
              - Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi            51

and the Lord, Vitthala used to dine together. But before
she took her share from that dish, she used to reserve one
or two morsels of it for Vitthala. This ought to be con-
sidered as the high water mark of love for God.

                         CHAPTER       II

Section D: LETTERS OF SRI SAMARTH BHAUSAHIB
                     MAHARAJA, UMADI

 1. To Balkrishna Pandit of Numbargi.       December 21, 1869.

      Do your duties in this worldly life with the faith that
whatever God wills will alone take place.

 2.   To Ramchandrarao Despande of Karajagi, May 25,
      1885,

      It is unfortunate that due to the negligence of our
forefathers half of the land income is almost lost.       One
does not know if this is the repayment of what was taken
on loan in our previous births!       But whatever has to be
done now must be done intelligently and guardedly.         No
amount of arrogance or quarrelsome or aggressive conduct
will be of any avail. For, the reputation, if it is once lost,
will never be      regained.   One must have therefore friend-
ship with all while preserving one’s importance and dignity
in the society by behaving honestly and up-rightly.       One
should keep his word, but should neither trust in persons
of low character, nor confide one’s secrets in them, nor ever
act according to their advice or remain in their company.
For this will only help to get one’s position lowered in
society. All.the same, one should as far as possible, do good
to all.

      Meditate on the Name of God three times a day, in the
morning, in the afternoon and in the evening to the maxi-
mum of your capacity and without any exception.          Read
some spiritual literature in the evening every day, and burn
a piece of camphor as a mark of worship to the Lord. You
82      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

will then be free from all doubts and live in constant joy
and peace.    Have faith in Him and in whatever He does.
For, no one till now has gone out of this world after having
made secure all his interests for his relatives and the same
{fs true in the case of everyone of us. So, what is possible
for one is to remember and pray to God on all occasions and
continue to do his duty. This will help him to surrender to
God physically, mentally and intellectually, and to become
convinced that, not he, but God is the real and the only
doer of all things.

 3.   To his elder brother Nanasahib.    October 6, 1895.

      The mind will be at peace and without any agitation,
if one does his duty with joy and tries his best to give joy
to others.

 4.   To Dr, Balajipant, of Savalagi. November 1, 1895.

      Natural it is that man should suffer on account of his
attachment to body. But if he gets himself associated with
his Guru who being magnanimuous asks him to meditate
on the Name of God, and thereby affords him occasions of
joy and peace, he will come to know that there is nothing
greater than the spiritual life, which puts a stop to ali
sorrow and grief. All his time will then be spent in joyful
meditation on the Name and in peaceful contemplation on
the divine Forms. His greed, attachment, and affection be-
come all centred on the devotion to God only.

 5. To Kashinathpanta Cardavadkar. January 10, 1896.

      Be a little indifferent to the body, harden the mind and
controlling the senses, get yourself devoted to the medita-
tion on the Name of God. Do not be anxious about anything
else; meditate from the bottom of your heart with faith
and love. You will then come to know how the body, the
mind, and the senses can all be employed in the service of
God, and how on account of the subsequent realization of
Him, real Variagya (disgust for all Godless objects) will
             Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi               53

atise in you, and how health, joy and peace will of them-
selves come to you.     No doubt, your mind might wander
for some time, but do not allow your body to move from
the meditating posture, and wander along with it. You will
soon find that your mind also will obey you, and that you
will not feel like wasting your time in idle talk or be de-
ceived by what the thoughtless might say.

6. To his eldest brother, Nanasahib, September 13, 1898
      and February 6, 1899.

      As you are elder to me, it will be wrong on my part
to advise you in any way.      Yet, I am tempted to request
that you too should give your time to the regular and deter-
mined meditation on the Name of God.

      If one were simply to obey his conscience, I fear it may
not be possible to pacify the mind. But in case he has the
experience of the presence of God, he will be free from
doubts and his mind will be at rest.

 7.   To Basapppa Katagi, January 19, 1899.

      When   people   request me   that they should not be
thrown in the clutches of Maya, I do not understand what
they really wish me to do for them.       Whereas God him-
self becomes subject to His own Maya and reincarnates
again and again in each of the aeons, who are you and J to
say that we need not be made subject to the influence of
Maya ?

 8. To his eldest son, August 2, 1899.

      If you forget to meditate on the divine Name, you will
naturally be drawn towards the sensuous objects of plea-
Sure, As a consequence of this your merit will be exhausted,

sins will accumulate, and you shall have to suffer various
miseries. Be contented with what God has given you, and
do to your maximum capacity meditation with resolve and
delight especially during night. Be sure that you will have
joy and peace in both the worldly and the spiritual lives.

Of course, you should be always. alert to do your duty, in

 9.   To Narayanarao Godbole, Febrauary 8, 1900.

      You. know it already that God protecis his devotees
from    all   dangers   and   difficulties   by   assuming   various
forms and qualities. And you know that this is being veri-
fied in your own worldly life.        He is ever doing what He
thinks to be the best for His devotees. While doing all his
activities the devotee is to consider Him’ as residing always
in his heart and therefore to remember Him at all times
with love and joy, and make every effort to visulize Him
even though He is not capable of being seen by the physical
eyes.



10.   To Parvatibai of Jamkhandi, March 5, 1900
      and March 21, 1900.

      If the devotee has no other desire except to enhance
and spread the glory of God by being devoted to Him more
and more every day, God protects him and helps him in
every way.     He becomes proud of his devotee.         Therefore,
it is very necessary that devotion to God should increase
day by day, both in quantity and in quality. Unless this
happens no spiritual experience can be had in the ordinary
mundane life.     On the contrary, the mind will be drawn
more and more to the sensuous desires only.

12. To Bhaskarpanta Chimadakar of J amkhandi, 1900.
     To raise the cry in chorus, ‘Victory to the Guru’ on a
 particular occasion, and to express this by oral or written
 words to another afterwards by recalling the occasion in
 memory should not be considered as enough by a spiritual
 aspirant For, the experience of the actual joy of the occasion
 may not be felt as continuous to the point of time when it is
 described by means of words. Instead, what will happen,
 if there is continual and real devotion to God and con-
 tinual faith in the Guru, is that    the devotee will neces-
 sarily experience the repetition of the same joy which he
 must have had on the first occasion, also on all other occa
 sions whenever the first incident is narrated or described
 orally or by a letter. All the future occasions will be the
 revivals and not merely the shadows or the skeletons of
 the joy and of the event as if.  The event of the past and
 the joy attending upon it ought to be reproduced again and
 again. The event may be a recall, but the joy of it will be
 the same, or more than what it was before.

     If such be the devotion, that is, if the devotion of any
 particular moment of time is not only the summation
                                                           of
 the devotion of all the previous moments but a real
                                                         addi-
 tion to the experience of the past, then there
                                                would emerge
 the real joy of performing a Kirtana, that is
                                                of singing the
  Name of God along with the description
                                         of His glorious
  deeds.   Otherwise, the Kirtana will merely be
                                                 a conglo-
  meration of empty words, empty
                                    events and sounds of musi-
56      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

cal instruments.   The first kind of Kirtana will be able to
carry the whole of audience along the joyful current of .
devotion, while the second, a source of mere entertainment
to them.    The first will be a selfless service to God and
humanity, the second a service to one’s own belly or honour.


13.   To Ramarao Vaidya of Bijapur.

      Niskama bhakti is that which is directed to the exclu-
sive attainment of one desire only, namely, the realization
of God,    Just as a dutiful son thinks that his welfare lies
in being obedient to his father and believes that his father
would do everything for his good, even so, an ardent de-
votee, being loyal to his Guru, will do everything as he has
been instructed to do, and believe that whatever God will
be pleased to do for       him will be of real good to him.   He
will resign to the will of God, and will never insist on gett-
ing his desires fulfilled.    What he will be anxious about is
to watch if his devotion to God is ever on the increase or
not, For, unless it be on the increase there will be no real
love for God and no realization of God worth the name.


14.   To Basappa Katagi and other disciples at Jamkhandi
      February 14, 1901.

      Whatever may be the condition or the        circumstance,
the mind should be joyful. There should not be any other
idea except the name of God in it. Yes, you have written
to me that the shrine of Shri Nimbargi Maharaja has been
desecrated by removing the stones of it by the priests of
the temple on the ill-advice and backing of certain crooked
persons who are against the followers of the saint.       This
action, as you think, has been done in order to bring in
disrepute the name of the Maharaja.         But you need not
worry over this.    For, I believe that instead of lowering
down the importance of the great saint, your keeping quiet
with courage and forgiveness will help to increase it all
the more. Treat these enamies as if they are your friends,
avsd then you will find that success will be yours,
             Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                      57

      tm order to achieve this what we have to do is to
remember the Name of God, without thinking egoistically
about    us that we are ‘so and so’ from the worldly point-
of-view, or that we are the devotees of God and that there-
fore we spend our time and energy in the service to God.
It is egoism which bewilders our intellect and makes us
feel that we are either the tiny individual souls (the jivas)
who are limited by space time, body, senses, mind and intel-
lect, or that we are the devotees of God. Whatever may be
the kind of egoism, material or spiritual, one is, to that ex-
tent, dislodged from the real path of devotion.           The real
devotee will neither be proud of his status or possessions
in the worldly life, nor of his importance or          greatness as
a devotee.   What he will be aware of constantly is the
Name of God. That alone and the unmixed joy of medita-
tion without any tinge of egoism           (material or spiritual)
will make him the real ‘a-karta’ (non-doer) in spite of his
karma of devotion. And when meditation culminates in the
manifestation of God (visual or otherwise) he become non-
different from God.      He is neither the spectator in sleep,
nor the dreamer, nor the doer in the wakeful condition,
since on account of his meditation on the Name of God and
the consequent contemplation on the visions of God, he be-
comes united and merged in God.

      This then is the way to become really happy.            Praise
or censure may be there; but they refer to the body and
mind, with which the real devotee is not at all concerned.
Whether in samsara or in Paramartha (spiritual life) his
consciousness will always have God as its only object and
never his ego or the ‘TI’.

    16.   To Antajipant Risbuda of      Jamkhandi, September 7,
          1902.

          If during meditation one were to experience God as
    Nirguna       (Impersonal)   how will there   arise in him any
    idea about himself that he is a separate individual entity
    or that he is a devotee of God?      He too would become as
    impersonal as God without there being any relational idea
    of his being the father or the son of anyone else.         This
    is the stage at which one can be said to be liberated from
    the notion of jivahood (the soul in bondage) and from that
    of his being the doer of any action. No doubt, such a one
    will still be called'as a devotee (Bhakta) of God, but only
   ‘In the mystical sense that he has the ability to participate
    in the nature of God, and not in the ordinary theistic sense
    of being the worshipper of God from a distance as if. Dur-
    ing this condition of being one with God, he will neither be
    conscious of his age, sex or caste, nor of his being low, sin-
    ful or degraded in any way. His only consciousness will
    be of the Name, the vision etc. of God, and of nothing else.
    10 call him as a Bhakta is to call him a Jnani from whom
    have vanished all Godless ideas. His bhakti or devotion in
    the form of his meditation on the name of God enables
    him to see God and to be ote with him and thus become 2
    liberated jnani, and his unison with God enables him fur-
    ther to feel no interest in. Godless objects which makes him
    2 Virakta.

          All this will be possible if the Name of God as imparted
    by a great realized soul is unfailingly fixed in the throat or
    mind to such an extent that there will be as many effortless
    tepetitions of the Name as there are effortless breaths dur-
    ing twenty four hours namely, 21600.          Will not one who
    undergoes this spiritual discipline be said to be non-diffe-
    tent from Brahman, even though he may appear extern-
    ally to others as being engrossed in the affairs of the world ?



1.) र
              Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                  59

 What can be said about this man? Is he breathing or work-
 ing like other beings, or is he conscious of himself as being
 the ultimate Reality on account of his meditating mentally
 and unceasingly on the Name of God? The answer is
 that, whatever might be the manner in which the.question
 is replied, he is none else but God, with this difference
 however that whereas God is eternally disembodied, the
` bhakta is embodied and does appear to be so to the on-
 lookers, though he is ever beyond the consciousness of his
 body.


 17,   To Mr. Vasudevarao Bidanur of Budhagoan,
       February 6, 1904.

       Why should you mind that you are being censured by
 the people when you are busy in meditation ? Remember
 that this has been the fate of all the devotees since time im-
 memorial. The people in general are ignorant,
                                           and so they
 make such attacks.    Will the frogs in a well come to know
 the expanse of the Sea? A wise man’s piece of advice will
 be a prattle to an untutored shephered. Will a donkey ap-
 preciate the singing of the Celestial beings ?    Even so, one
 who is engrossed in the worldly activities will find faults
 with the a person who is devoted to God. But there is a les-
 son, for the devotee. He should consider both praise and
 censure by the people as being irrelevant from the view-
 point of his devotion otherwise there will       be no sincere
 attachment to one’s own devotional practice. Besides, is not
 the man man who censures a sort of constant watchman of
 the conduct of a devotee? This is an advantage for which
 the devotee should be thankful to him.


 18.   To ‘Narayanarao Godbole and other disciples at Vijapur,
       February 10, 1905,

       Since long we have been denied the pleasure of read-
 ing your letter. You may not have been able to write on
 account of your being very busy in your own affairs.
                                                             Yet
 it is gratifying to note that you have not been totally neglect-
60       Prof. R. 2. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

ful of us. Your gift of sesame and sugar sent to us on the
day of Makar Sankraman, is the sure sign of remembering
us occasionally. This has created the hope in us that not-
withstanding your being extremely busy in Samsara, you
will spare some time to meditate on the Name of God. In
fact, if you begin to meditate daily, regularly and earnestly,
you will yourself discover in course of time that this practice
of spiritual exercise will have the power to destroy the
effects of your worldly karma, as also to remove your false
notion    that   your   worldly   duties   alone   constitute   the
Dharma.     You will come to know in other words, that the
real Dharma consists in realizing the Atman, that is in
visualizing one’s own immaculate form of the Self as de-
fined by Saint Ramadasa. What is popularly and tradition-
ally known as Dharma will then appear as one which has
its reference only to the bodily wants and the material gains,
and which in the absence of the knowledge of Dharma in
the spiritual sense will keep on bewildering the intellect
of man.    On the other hand, the knowledge of the Atman
will enable a man to be alert to look with due interest to
both the sides of Dharma, the worldly and the spiritual.
For, after all, the power of God is equally needed in both
the spheres,     Without that none will be able to move a
step.    Regular meditation and prayerful surrender to God
off and on will therefore enable one to find time for doing
his duty both ways.


20.   To Vishnupant Nimbargikar of Jamkhandi,
      February 16, 1905.

      There is no wonder if one would say that he does not
find time for meditation because he has to do various duties.
This appears natural also for two reasons. One is that man
thinks that he alone is responsible for the performance of
his duties and the other is that he is unable to sce the hid-
den hand of God who is really and ultimately the only
cause of all that takes place in the material and the spiri-
tual spheres. When however, by the continual, earnest and
                Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                     61

    daily practice of meditation and contemplation on the Name
    and Forms of God, one would come to know the presence,
    the power, and the grace of God, he will cease to attach the
    egoistic importance to the will and efforts of man in doing
    anything successfully. He will then come to know that his
     success and prosperity in the material world as well as
    his growing experiences of the various divine manifesta-
     tions with all their beauty, splendour, and glory are nothing
     but the work of God. It depends entirely upon man whether
     he should remain content by supposing that his efforts
     alone, willbe responsible for the success or failure in what
     he does, or come to realize that notwithstanding his efforts,
     which also are ultimately due to God, the success or failure
     depends on the will of God.

        In order to have this deep insight in the nature of
     things, one must in the first place, come in contact with the
     good and the holy, and that, secondly, try his utmost to in-
     crease his attachment, devotion and dependence on God by
     means of regular exercise of meditation on the Name of
     God. To bring in thus the spiritual power in oneself is to
    ~ bring in more    and   more   contentment   and the   bliss   of
     divine presence, in spite of the condition and place where
     one is placéd by God.    Otherwise, he will become blind by
     his egoism and behave with arrogance.        All the same one
     must remember that what passes on ordinarily as bhakti,

     viz. that which is not supported by the grace of Guru, is a
     perishable one.  On the contrary, if after being initiated
     into the spiritual life by a person who has realized the
     Brahman, one resorts to meditation daily and for a long
     time, he is protected by God and he gets more and more
     suitable opportunities to meditate.


     21. To Ramachandrapant Hortikar, March 15, 1905.


         Yes, you have rightly complained that the mind is too
     unsteady to be controlled.     One might say that even the

7    Space can be rolled up, the mountain can be reduced to
62      Prof. की, D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

powder; but the pulls of the mind cannot be stopped. But
remember there is a remedy to accomplish even this ap-
parent impossibility. Let the mind go and wander wher-
ever it likes to go; but do not move your body in the least
and disturb the posture in which you are sitting for medita-
tion. On other occasions also, let your mind entertain any
sort of desire; but let not your body move in the direction
in which your mind may wish you to move.           Do this as
often as the mind tries to tempt you, and continue your
meditation with stubbornness and zeal.      Then, in course of
time, you will find that the mind is tamed and has become
obedient to help you in your effort t6 become morally spot-
less and thereby to achieve your spiritual Good. And when
you will succeed in doing this, you will find that you are
the happiest man both materially and spiritually.


22.   To Melagirappa Nasi and other disciples at    Jamkhandi,
      January 10, 1906,         न       न
      This letter. is written to you with the intention of re~
minding you.that you have not written to us since long.
People have strange notions about the nature of God and
consequently entertain strange notions about the manner in
which they should behave in the world. In the first place,
they have the wrong notions about the incarnations of God.
They believe that God allows himself to be born in this
world and behaves in mysterious and almost unbelievable .
ways; and then in the second place following as if in the
footsteps of some or other incarnation of God, they believe
that the best sort of behavour for a man devoted to God
would-be that which will create the impression in others
that he is possessed by God. Naturally, as the result of
this twofold wrong notion, he would try to astonish the
world by making a‘correct forecast of the future or by
telling accurately about the events of the past. But do not
believe such people, even though they appear as saints and
possess miraculous powers. Do not consider them to be
saints even though they possess the power to stop the
               Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi                  63

movement of the sun in his course in space. For, their
supernatural powers too are as transitory and perishable as
they themselves are. To be born is to possess a perishable
body, and so, whatever is done excepting meditation and
contemplation on God is as perishable as the body is.

        As against this, what ought to be the end of human life
is to attain the disembodied condition of God-realization,
which is beyond birth and death, inasmuch as it consists
in the realization of one’s own immaculate form.          This can
be had by going beyond the ordinary concepts and practices
of punya (merit) and papa (demerit) or what is the same
thing as, going by the path of the really great Punya,
namely, the meditation on the divine Name as imparted
by the Guru, and the contemplation on the consequent
manifested forms of God which are eternally pure and im-
perishable.     In short, not miracle-mongering, but the re-
alization of the Atman is the only ultimate end of human
life,

23. To Vasudevarao Bidnurkar (At Gudageri),
        January 24, 1906.

        Greatly pleased to receive sugar-coated sesame on the
occasion of the Makara-Sankramana day.          I   was    greatly
delighted also to read that you had gone to visit the places
of pilgrimage such as Kashi, Gaya, and Prayaga, and that
you think yourself to be blessed and a man of merit on
this account,


        What the saints, Tukarama and Ramadasa say in this
connection is, however,, different from what you believe.
Tukarama holds the view that one’s own parents are the

true places of pilgrimage. One who serves them worships
God, and so he need not visit Kashi or any other holy
Place. From this point of view it appears that your ser-
vice to your living mother will bring you greater merit
than what your visits to the holy places might bring to you.
Similarly, Ramadasa also tells us in his ‘Verses addressed
 64          Prof. R. D, Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

 to the Mind’, that there is no necessity of going anywhere
 in search of merit, if one gives himself to meditation on the
 Name of God as instructed by the Guru.   For Ramadasa,
 meditation is the highest possible punya which can be
 acquired by man.


       I have no doubt that you must be knowing this all
 already and therefore doing accordingly. Your visit to the
 holy places is an additional merit no doubt.


 24.   To Amburao, October 13, and October 28, 1906.

       I am now convinced that God being exceedingly kind
 to    his    devotees   gets his   plans   and   purposes   executed
 through them. I am therefore confident that you will spare

 coming celebration will be done most appropriately, as will

25. To Dattopant Namadeva of Belgaum, October 29, 1906.

       To engage the mind for fixed periods three times a day
in meditation is to prevent it from remembering or thinking
 about any other thing.


       In your letter however you have written that the
 spiritual teacher should do this for you, as if he is res-
 ponsible for your faults. But this is unfair. Supposing a
 boy who is too engrossed’ in playing cards does not leave
 his game even though he is asked to do so again and agaift
 by his father, will it be proper to say that the father and
-not his son is responsible for the conduct of the latter ?
 Or suppose, t tame parrot in a cage continues to feel that
he is confined even though he is set free or comes back in
the cage because he is fed there nicely, who can make
it fly to enjoy the freédom of the outside world? Can
           Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of- Umadi                 65


anyone say that the fault here lies with the cage or with
the owner of ithe cage ? Even so, if the disciple becomes
indifferent to the instruction of the Guru, fails to meditate
regularly, intensely and with increasing faith and love,
and as a consequence of this fails to experience the grace
and protection of God, can the fault be laid at the doors of
the Guru or of God? Remember the Guru & God with faith
and love, and you will find that they are ever very close to
you and prepared to forgive you in     spite of thousands of
your faults, provided you fall at     their feet and implore
them to pardon you.

26. To Ramarao Hortikar, May, 1907.

   Disinterested devotion and service to God, if practised
with increasing faith and loyalty, will not make a man
puffed up with egoism, even if he is promoted by the grace
of God to a higher rank and status in his official cadre. But
what is required for maintaining the knowledge of God
and the consequent disgust for all other Godless objects is
the fire of devotion, that is, constant meditation on the
Name of God. Without this, there will only be the craving
for the sensuous pleasures of life. It is given to all, either
to choose the -bliss of God-realization by means of medita-
tion, or to remain satisfied by enjoying the short-lived, illu-
sory pleasures of life by turning one’s back against God.

Pass your time in the joyful mood of meditation.
