 of 8 road, He was paid one eighth of a rupee per day as his
 wages but that too was a help to his brother Gopalarao in
 maintaining the family. This job also came to a close soon.
 Baba then began to carry about a maund (20 Kgs) of salt
 from Solapur, a distance of about fortyfive miles, over his
 head and sell it at Jigajivani and other neighbouring vil-
‘lages. On his way he used to eat raw brinjal with salt as his
 meals. As if this was not enough, a new responsibility
 came over him. His sister Jivubai became a widow. She
 and her very young and the only son were at Inchgiri, a
 place six miles away from Jigajivani. Baba went there out
 of compassion. As his sister’s son was still a.minor, Baba
 worked on his behalf as the Revenue Officer. .
    ‘The trial of adversity turned ‘out to be a spiritual op-
 portunity. Nimbargi Maharaja was returning from Hulyal,
 a place near Jamkhandi, where he had gone to pay a visit
 to his daughter. At night he stopped on his way at Inch-
 giri for rest in the very house in which Baba was staying
 with his sister. But   instead of sleeping, he kept on sitt-
 ing and meditating throughout the night.     On seeing this,
 Baba asked him as to why he was not sleeping. ‘No my son,
 T could not sleep on account of insistent coughing’ replied
 the saint. In the morning however, he took his blanket,
 dusted it off, put it on his shoulder saying to Baba, ‘I have
 now finished my work’, Certainly, this must have been an-

 other direct.instalment of grace from the     great saint on
 Baba. Perhaps as the result of this divine grace Baba was
 Married to one Ahilyabai, who in due course gave birth to

 १ daughter. Baba had inherited a piece of land at Inchgiri
 and had himself purchased a small piece there. There was
 nothing wrong, if like any ordinary man Baba too began to
 find interest and joy in his life as a householder. But this
 was not to continue for a long time.
                                        Baba’s only daughter
 died, and not much afterwards his wife also passed away.
_ Baba was left alone with the gloom and the grief of a for-
 3000 man. Those who are born with a silver spoon in their
` mouths and those on whom fortune smiles        without any
96     Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


reason, can afford to be atheists and preach optimism to
the world, But those who are forsaken by the world and
those who have the ill-luck of seeing always the frowning
face of their fate, and so are miserable and disappointed in
life are bound to be pessimistic. As the last alternative
they resort to God and attempt to see if He would favour
them once at least. Baba was a devotee of the deity known
as Datta; and so in his hour       of trial, he    went over to
Ganagapur, a place sacred because of that deity and of a
great saint Nrsimhasaraswati by name who had lived there
for a few years and practised great penance. Baba stayed
there for three days meditating       and reading a    spiritual
book known as ‘Gurucaritra’ with devotion and hope.           On
the third night, he had a dream.      But instead of seeing a
good   omen   indicating that he    would   be    married   again
he saw a sannyasin who gave him a rosary for meditation.
Baba returned to Inchgiri as sad and        gloomy as he was
when he left Inchgiri, probably not being able to under-
stand the meaning of the dream that he would soon meet
a great saint.

     Wonderful it is that God creates the trials for his devo-
tees and solves them Himself in a mysterious way. People
of the world who think too much about their tiny intellect,
speak about any strange or miraculous happening in terms
of chance and accident. Very few can see the hand of God
in all that happens, and in the arrangement of the circum-
stances in which the events take place. Baba was too dis-
appointed to think of any way out of his troubles. And yet,
he was hoping that he would be married again and would
be able to pass a happy life. God wanted to use him as his
instrument in spreading the devotion leading to self-reali-
zation, Consequently on April 1892 as desired by his sister,
he went to Nimbargi to witness a festival in honour of
the deity Bhimraya, which coincided with anniversary fes-
tival of the passing away of Sri Nimbargi Maharaja. There
he met Sri Bhausahib Maharaja, the most prominent of
the disciples of Nimbargi Maharaja. With great hesitation,
              Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri             97


Baba allowed Bhausahib Maharaja to initiate him in the
spiritual fold, for he feared that the initiation would put a
final stop to his most    cherished desire of    enjoying the
householder’s life once again. Besides, being honest to the
core, he did not wish to make his spiritual career a pre-
tence. That      is why he   plainly put forth   the difficult
situation of his mind before his Guru, Bhausahib Maha-
raja.    But when the latter promised him that he would
take upon himself the responsibility of his disciple’s safe
journey on the spiritual path, that is, without allowing any
moral Japse on his part to spoil his spiritual life, Baba
accepted the: Name and returned to Inchgiri repeating it
with great zeal and with his gaze on the tip of his nose.
For three months continuously Baba never ceased to medi-
tate nor did he change his gaze, though he suffered much
from pain and defect in eyesight. “I lost my interest in
Samsara? he used to tell about himself, “and like a mad
man, I sometimes forgot to put the morsel of food into my
mouth, though I used to be hungry. I     become intoxicated
and engrossed in uttering the Name of God to such an
extent”,


    The result was obvious. Day and night, Baba began      to
see a brilliant light before his eyes, but he became blind to
the objects of the world.    He referred the matter to his
Guru, who then convinced him that the light and all other
visual experiences were spiritual in nature and       that he
could have them on account of his constant meditation with
unswerving devotion and faith.



        Section B:   SOME NOTE-WORTHY INCIDENTS

    Baba used to have always an uncompromising attitude
with reference to morality on the one hand, and spiritual
discipline, on the other. Not that he was not aware of the
fact that the vicious and the atheists prosper and that the
Virtuous and the devotionally minded suffer in this world.
But when he was asked as to why such gross injustice
98      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


should happen in the kingdom of God, and as to why the
virtuous should be punished        for even a      slight departure
from the righteous path,        and the vicious      should go un-
punished in spite of all the wrongs they would do, immedi-
ately came the reply from Baba that the rules of conduct
and discipline of a school are applicable only to some one
who attends the school and not to the one who does not.
The    former passes his future life in happiness          and joy,
whereas the latter in misery and pain.

      The great German philosopher Kant solved the pro-
blem by saying that the balance would be set right in a life
after death by God, and so he cherished the hope that the
present suffering would be recompensed by future happi-
ness. Being influenced by the great philosopher, others also
began to believe that the Kantian postulate contained in it
the axiomatic truth. Our saint of Inchgiri was more prag-
matic    than   visionary. He    believed   like   his   Guru and
other great saints, in the cash and present value of things
and not in their credit or would — be value of the future
His empirical observation of the actual         course of events
was as keen and unerring as his insight into spiritual mat-
ters. And so, his judgement that a virtuous life combin-
ed with poverty is far more valuable than a life of pros-
perity based on vice has to be accepted as the cornerstone
of both morality and religion

      A virtuous life for Sri Baba meant a life of continual
practice of virtue without any single exception till death.
For, he feared that a single exception would at any time
undo what has been achieved till then and lead him on the
path of temptation and evil. On one occasion, when there
was a great crowd of spiritual aspirants at Inchgiri, his
own spiritual sister, Shivalingavva by name, who was
nearly as old and as spiritually advanced as he was, hap-
pened to sit for meditation in the room.in which Baba was
already sitting. She was careful to hang a curtain between
her and him, so that he might not be            disturbed in his
            Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri               99

meditation. For some time,‘Baba did not become aware of
her presence as he was deeply engrossed in remembering
the Name of God. But no sooner did he becorne conscious
of the fact that Shivalingavva had taken her seat in his
room, than he got up from his seat and left the room. Just
then, an elderly person seeing Baba going out of the
Math, asked him as to why he had suddenly left the room
and where he intended to go. Baba told him that inas-
much as Shivaligavva had entered his room, he had to go
out in the jungle for meditation. ‘Both of you are very old
and highly advanced in spiritual life.     Why then there
should arise any iota of suspicion of any kind in the mind
of anyone?’, asked the gentleman. ‘Yes’, said Baba, ‘I am
very old, no doubt, and that Shivalingavva is spiritually
great, but she is endowed with the body of a woman, and
I with that of a man. To trust my body, as if it    will ever
be absolutely free from temptation, is a dangerous supposi-
tion. I do not wish to have any experiment about this and
to desert the path of virtue even by way of exception.’


    Baba’s conception of what is morally right went far be-
yond the conception of what is legally right.      Baba was
ignorant of the meaning of lottery and so had to be told
that the.gamble had the capacity of earning thousands of
 rupees, even though the investment was of a rupee only.
 Money earned in this manner was legally justified, and it
 could be used, he was told, for the spiritual cause which
 was dear to him. Resenting the suggestion Baba said very
 bitterly that it   meant   nothing
                                  but the exploitation of
 thousands of persons for the purpose of securing the end
 of one person.     ‘I do not approve that foul means should
 be adopted for securing an end, however laudable it may
 be. If the end to be achieved is good, the means also must
 be good.   Foul means can never be justified on the ground
 that the end would be good. And especially in a lottery,
 how can the happiness of one person be justified when
 it is achieved at the cost of the suffering of the thousands”
    100       Prof. 2. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


            Following in the footsteps of his Master, Sri Bhausahib
    Maharaja of Umadi, Baba too believed that the whole of
    the moral life of     a man consisted of two negative virtues,
    namely, desistance      from usurping another man’s wealth
    and woman. Of course, these seem to be only negative in
    nature, but they are in-fact, the source of all-the so-called
    positive virtues, long list of which can be found even in
    such a sacred book like the Bhagavata or the Bhagavad-
    gita.    For instance if one has no lustful      eye on the wealth
    or women of others, why indeed should he try to earn
    money by foul means:and increase his greed day by day?
    Will not such a man be considered as pure in heart, honest
    and truthful to the core, and friendly and gentle to all?


            This approach ६0 - the problem of virtues is only an
    improvement upon the        original approach of Sri Nimbargi
    Maharaja, according to which virtue consisted in the three-
    fold desistance from usurpation of another man’s wealth,
    lands and women. As landed property is.a kind of wealth,
    it appears that Bhausahib and Amburao must              have sub-
    sumed it under wealth.       In this. context,    one can even go
    back to the very ancient times of the         Upanisads and see
    that for the author of the Isavasyopanisad both lands and
    women must have been subsumed under the single cate-
    gory of wealth.      For, there, one is advised not to cast a
    coveteous glance of a vulture on the wealth of any person.
    That the people of those times should consider women also
    as a kind of wealth, appear to be the outcome of the         sup-
    position that she was considered as a slave of man,-or bet~
    ter still as a chattel which belongs to him. Anyway, the
    root cause of all evil, vice and sin appears to the saints
    as well as to the sages of the past, as being nothing but
    the fraudulent use of another man’s wealth.

        Though not opposed to the view of Sri Nimbargi Maha-
    raja, it appears that both Bhausahib and Amburao held a
    somewhat different view regarding the place and status of
    Karma in spiritual life, and as if as a consequence of this,

न
           Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inckgiri                     101

regarding the relation of virtue with the power of God.
“If my own karma is very powerful in its effects, how can
God be responsible for it? If our actions are virtuous
enough, none of the three great gods Brahma, Visnu and
Mahesa singly or jointly can do any harm fo us, On the
other hand, if the actions are evil, it would be impossible
for the three gods to save us.” This, in the opinion of
Nimbargi Maharaja, amounts to the autonomous view of
morality. And, yet, morality to him was not unrelated to
spiritual life. It is impossible to please God, thought he,
without being thoroughly virtuous, that is, so long as
one has a coveteous eye upon the landed property, the
wealth and the women of others. In the case of Bhau-
sahib and Amburao, morality does not seem to be as al-
together existing   independently of spiritual         life.   One
may practise virtue as much as he likes, but perfection
in it can never be attained without the help of the power
of the grace of God, that is, without oneself beihg spiritual
at the same time.    Neither mere        gun-powder,     nor the
spark of fire by itself will cause an explosion.        Even so,
the ethical and the spiritual must go hand in hand.            Besi-
des being incomplete, the former without the help of the
spirit will only engender egoism, and the latter without
morality will simply be a hideous picture to look at. The
ideal of God-head includes in it perfection of both the
moral and the    spiritual life.   If   man is   to approximate
to that ideal, he must also be gradually more and more
moral and spiritual at the
                        same time. Morality    would
make him more and more worthy for being spiritual in
nature, and his being spiritual would give him additional
 strength to become more and more virtuous.             In short,
instead of there being exclusively and independently two
fields of inquiry, morality and spiritual life are to Bhau-
sahib and Amburao Maharaja two inter-dependent com-
ponents of one and the same life divine.         They were the
obverse and the reverse side of the same coin, whether
‘this position was also exactly held later on by Professor
102     Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


की, D. Ranade         or modified in        some way, we shall see
when we come to discuss his contribution to philosophy,
and religion.      What must be noted at this stage is that’
the most fundamental point common to all the three saints
was that the life in spirit is of supreme importance to man
and as such,       even the autonomy of morality is subservi-
ent to the will of       God, beyond which there is neither any
norm,   nor any mathematical rule to guide us.                  The saint
of Nimbargi knew this very well; but when he said that
the power of the three gods would not be of any avail to
avert the good or bad fruits of Karma, what he intended
to emphasize was that the road to the life divine should be
made smooth and easy by the practice of the moral virtues,
and that the efforts of man should be directly connected
with the grace of the Supreme Being without the inter-
vention    of   the    lesser   deities,   like   Brahma,   Visnu    and
Mahesa, one way or the other. -

      Naturally,      notwithstanding       the   interaction    between
morality and spiritual life, all the saints of this spiritual
school are in agreement in holding that if the connection
with God is once for all well-established, the moral life too
will be secure and free from danger. The next question
which will engage our attention is as to what infallible
path is there which one ought to follow in order to find out
God and to be one with Him. Amburao Maharaja tells
us that the Name imparted by a great saint who can en-
able others to realize God is alone fit to be meditated upon,
and not any other name or even the same name if it hap-
pens to be chosen at random by the devotee himself. For,
the Name imparted by the Guru is impregnated with the
power of the Lord, and hence capable of showing us the
face of the Lord. He tells us that the Name should be
meditated upon along with the incoming and the outgoing
breaths, that is mentally, throughout day and night. This
he calls as the ‘Nishabda Sadhana’, that is meditation with-
out making any articulate sound by the tongue. And this
too, he says, must be done regularly, daily and with faith,
            Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri |              103

determination, love and regard throughout one’s life, with-
out caring for what others might say or ‘think or, for their
appreciation or censure or again without being daunted by
difficulties and obstacles on the way. He further tells us
with absolute clarity that meditation should not be moti-
vated by any worldly desire. It should be done in the spirit
in which a blind man prepares a rope out of the jute. The
blind man is not aware of as to how much jute is given to
him for making a rope, whether the rope which has been
already made is there or has been cut out and removed by
someone, Even so, should one meditate upon God, says
Baba, without caring to know how many years he has to
live and meditate or what has been the effect of his medi-
tation upto a particular    time.     One must     meditate and
meditate till his mind ceases to entertain any desire, till
he forgets that he is a bodily and mental being, and till he
realizes that he is nondifferent from God whom he comes
to see in course of his meditation.


    It should be clear from     all this that God-realization
that is, the contemplation on the vision of God, was for
Baba as for his predecessors and his spiritual brother, Pro-
fessor R. D. Ranade, the highest and the central virtue
for the attainment of which the only means is the
meditation on the divine Name as imparted by the spiritual
 teacher,


              Section C: “SAYINGS OF BABA”


     1.   The aspirants should bid      good-bye    to lust and
 greed, otherwise there would be no meditation,

     2.   There wiil be no vision of God, if one imagines and
‘imagines only.


     3. The divine Name must come from a realized saint,
 who is ever immortal. Hundreds of names which one may
 find in religious books are of no use.
104         Prof, R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


    4, Undaunted by difficulties and calamities, meditate
firmly with courage and love on the Name as instructed
by the Guru, and you will come to know that you alone
are the happiest person in this miserable world.

       5.    God will certainly manifest Himself if His Name
is firmly rooted in the mind.

       6.    God becomes proud of his devotee who remembers
Him always.

       7.    One who meditates can alone know that Brahman
is real and Maya is not.

       8.    To remember the Name of God consciously along
with the breaths is the indication of having faith in the
Guru,

       9.    Meditate silently in an erect sitting posture.

      10.    Look at the illumined vision of God and meditate
silently on His Name even while you are busy in doing
other activities,

      11.    Instead of   preaching   others,   achieve your   own
spiritual good by remembering unceasingly the            Name of
God.

      12.    To see God everywhere is what is meant by real
samadhi.

   13.       To become satisfied by looking at one’s own imma-
culate form constitutes true religion.

      14.    While the physical moon can be seen in the sky
only, the spiritual moon can be seen everywhere.

   15.       Be devoted to God after being able to look at Him.

   16.       The true Brahamcari (celibate) is one whose mind
and intellect are rivetted on Brahman.

   17.       Let the body move, but fix the mind on the Name
of God.
                Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri           105

  18.     The ideal is that you should see the vision of God
wherever you cast your glance.

   19. People will respect you if you always remember
and see the omnipresent Atman. You will even achieve
the power of God in some measure,

   20.    The real kind of immortality is to become united
with God, it has no temporal meaning.


   21. What God does is beyond the control of man, Gains
or losses and life or death depend upon the will of God.
Man should resign to it and only remember Him.

   22.    Success, glory, and fame are the gifts of God.

   23.    To remember God is to become like Him, to forget
the Name is to become the slave of Maya.

   24. One must be prepared to suffer the insults and
censure of the world before expecting the favour of God.

   25.    If one wills and fixes his attention on the Name
of God, he will not put forth the excuses that there occur
aches in the body or that he feels sleepy while meditating.

    26.   Devotion motivated by desires ends in the fulfil-
ment of desires, while unmotivated devotion ends in the
realization of God.

    27.    To    become a slave of Maya is to invite misery
and troubles.

    28.    Any spiritual gain which is not the result of one’s
 own exercise of meditation lasts for a short time.        But
an experience which is the joint effect of one’s own endea-
 vour and of the grace of God lasts forever, and is the
progenitor of other spiritual experiences.

    29.  To insult the vision of God in any        manner is to
 bring the wrath of God on oneself.   `
106         Prof. 5. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


      30.    Do not retract from the path of devotion once you
have resorted to it. Have patience, and it will surely lead
you towards God.



SEcTIoN         D:   BABA’S ATTITUDE TOWARDS MIRACLES


      Baba shared the belief of his Guru that miracles hap-
pen as the result of the great merit of being one with God
and that they are not manufactured by a saint.         A saint
has the capacity to know what is going to happen; and also
to know how the grace of God would transform an im-
possibility into an actuality.    The saint only watches and
does neither interfere in the natural course of events nor in
the miraculous workings of God. He usually does not speak
about it but merely remains content by saying that the
ways of God are inscrutable.       Sometimes he would      pray
to God and open his heart before him, but leave every-
thing to the will of God. This was the attitude of Baba
since he had no selfish end to fulfil.


      At one time Sri Baba was to go to Jalgaon. He expres-
sed a wish to meet one Mr. Mallharrao Joshi of Akola.
Accordingly Ranade wrote a letter to Joshi. However later
on realizing that Baba desired to meet Mr. Joshi as a de-
votee of God and not as a friend, he did not post the letter.
When Baba came to know about this, he appreciated it and
proceeded to Jalgaon.      But lo, Mr. Joshi went to Jalgaon
of his own accord on the information about Baba’s expected
arrival from his friend in      Jamkhandi. Exactly similar to
this was the meeting of Baba and Bhausahib at Nimbargi
on a day which was not their usual fixed day of meeting.
Bhausahib on that occasion had arrived at Nimbargi a
few days earlier. During the reading of spiritual literature
he asked those present whether Baba was likely to come.
At about the same time Baba, who had come to Nimbargi
for a totally different reason, with no knowledge of Bhau-
sahib’s arrival, was being asked by the villagers if he had
              Sri Aniburao Maharaja of Inichgiri            107

come to see Bhausahib, He promptly went to meet Bhau-
sahib

    It was in- April, 1927 that Baba went to Hulugbali and
stayed at Visnupant Parulekar’s. Vishnupant’s wife Tanu-
bai was always afraid of a serpent who used to come to
their house on every fifteenth day of the bright half of the
month. Tanubai used to burn a little camphone before that
serpent and place some milk for it. The. serpent used
to drink the milk and never come again for one month.
“I become much- afraid by seeing the serpent, though he
has done no harm,” said Tanubai to Baba. “If you are
afraid, the serpent will not come again” Baba told the
lady. And since that day, the serpent never visited that
house

        Two more incidents about serpents, Quite unusual it
was that Baba got up from his meditation and took in
one hand a stone and a lamp in the other and hurriedly
went to the room where some visitors were        staying. Then
he went straight to the       bathroom and with the light of
the lamp saw a serpent coiled up in a corner of the bath-
room. Allowing the serpent to go        out through a hole he
blocked the hole with the stone and quietly returned to his
room to resume his meditation. It seems Baba must have
foreseen the danger in his meditation.

        On another occasion, a disciple of Baba had specifically
 come to inform Baba that he was much afraid of the vision
of the   great serpent (Sesa). told him not to be afraid
 of that vision of God as that serpent would never bite, He
further advised him to carry on his meditation with greater
zeal, so that the hood of that serpent which was downwards
 will be turned upwards thus indicating that God was pleas-
ed with him

        There is no evidence to say that Baba attached any
 importance to such happenings though people made much
 ado,    For Baba that was all normal and routine.    To know
108   Prof. R. 2. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


what is passing on in the minds of others, or to know the
past or the future was for him, it seems, as good as see-
ing the present events and things. Once, he was going to
his room for meditation.   A lady who was waiting for seve-
ral hours in order to have an opportunity to get herself
convinced of the reality of the vision she was having, re-
quested a disciple of Baba to inform him that she wished
to speak with him only for a minute.      Without speaking a
word with the lady, Baba took a piece of paper and drew
upon it a figure about the veracity of which the lady wished
to have his opinion and told the disciple that what the
lady wished to ask him was about the figure he had drawn.
He instructed the disciple to show the piece of paper to the
lady and tell her that the vision she was seeing was real
and that she should carry on her meditation.


Section    E:   BABA’S BHAKTI: A FINISHED PRODUCT
                FROM THE VERY BEGINNING

    Bhausahib Maharaja has left the message for all that
Bhakti should be ever on the increase. What he means is
that devotion to God should increase both quantitatively
and qualitatively. Thus it should continue for longer periods
of time, and should more and more be informed with faith.
reverence, love and fear. The devout life of Baba is an illu-
stration of this. His devotion was not so much in the mak-
ing. It was already an accomplished, perfect product, which
could not have been improved except by making its roots
go deeper into the soil of his most fertile faith and love. An
earthen pot does not require any further baking, if it is
already baked. At any time, it gives forth a metallic sound,
if it is struck by the knuckles of the     fingers, or lightly
touched by a wooden rod. Baba did not make the transi-
tion from want of faith to faith or from an indifferent
attitude of mind to that of faith and love. Faith & Love
were there from the very beginning of his spiritual career.
Who else can have the courage of fixing his gaze at the tip
of his nose and meditate on the divine Name continuously
           Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri              109

for nearly three months? Who else can obey his Guru and
do exactly what is against one’s liking?    Who else, even
after the death of his Guru, would not allow      anyone to
wave light before his own photograph if it is placed along
with the photograph of his Guru? Who else would with
great pleasure, drink the   water which was trickling from
the bones of his deceased Guru after they were washed, as
if it was more holy than the water of the river Ganga?
Who else would think and actually feel that even after
death Bhausahib Maharaja was not dead? And who else
would   accept a   word of his   Guru as the word of    God
‘and not of man?    Baba used to tell some of his disciples
that it was because they considered their Guru as man that
they could not have the     spiritual experiences, which he
could have. whereas he himself treated his Guru as one
infusing in him the spirit of God and as being non-diffe-
rent from Him.     Can anyone weep for the death of his
Guru for twelve years as Baba did?     His passion for medi-
tation was so engrossing that on one occasion, when he was
awakened from his meditative ecstasy for taking his meals,
he asked with surprise, “Have I not taken my meals? I
think I have” “No, you have not. Please come, people are
waiting for you,” he had to be told.   On another occasion,
when his meditation was only half-finished, he had to join
to others for meals. He took a morsel of food in his hand
but could not put it into his mouth. He washed his hands
and went into his room to finish his meditation. Later on
when asked as to why he did not eat anything, he said
that the food then appeared to him as something not fit for
human consumption


    Baba was equally enthusiastic and anxious about the
 meditation of others and    felt very delighted if   anyone
 spoke about his spiritual experience to him.   It was in the
 year 1906 that a person who had just been initiated by
 Baba was asked by him to go outside Inchgiri and not get
“up from meditation till he got some spiritual experience.
         110     Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


         Accordingly that person went outside Inchgiri and began

         usual to have collective meditation.

               Till   noon     the     disciple     did     not    return.   Baba
         told” the     other    disciples      to   dine,    but      himself did
         not take     his meals       saying   that he      was not    hungry   in
         the least.   When someone asked if he should go and call
         the disciple who had gone out for meditation,                  Baba said

         ence. Till evening the disciple did not return. All the
         people became anxious about him. Baba alone was undis-

         Bhajana the disciple came and bowed down at the feet of
         Baba with a smile. Baba asked him about the experience
         which he had, and when he became convinced of the truth
         of it, he said to the assembly, “I had said before Maharaja
         that I would not remain in the matha, if this disciple did
         not get any experience.” People then came to know that
         Baba had not        taken his    lunch     because that gentleman
         also was without any food. Rare indeed is a saint who cares
         so much for the spiritual good of his disciples !



               Another similar incident happened at Rabkavi,                 near
     Jamkhandi.        To a disciple who complained that he had no
     experience,       Baba said,       “Sit in my room for meditation
     The disciple did accordingly.                Baba finished his evening
     meditation of three hours, and took his light meals also.
     Then he commenced the reading of the Dasabodha.                         Just
     then the disciple came out of the room. Seeing him, Baba
     asked him if it was true that stars were falling and flashes
     of light resembling the lightning were going “up, though
     the moon was yet to rise.” With tears of gratitude and joy
     the disciple said, “Yes, sir, by your grace, I have this ex-
     perience.”                   1

       Section     F: SOME OUTSTANDING SPIRITUAL
                    EXPERIENCES OF BABA

    Experience of the nature of the spiritual Truth became
the sole aim of Baba’s life. For the first three months after
he was initiated, he had, as we have already seen, abundant
experiences of light and colours, though he did not under-
stand what they all meant. He himself never told his ex-
periences to anyone except to those who deserved to know
them, probably because the undeserving people might
either disbelieve them, or ridicule them which would
amount to the insult of God. We give below some of the
most important experiences of Baba based on the authority
of Professor Ranade and Laxmanabhatji Nimbargi remem-
bering all the while that experience does not simply mean
the experiences of light, colour, sound, smell, and touch,
but also includes dreams which come true in the waking
life of the saint on account of the appearance of the Guru
in the dream.

      On the     authority of Professor   Ranade,   Mr. Ramanna
Kulkarni tells us*      that Baba had the experience of seeing
his    own immaculate form, the Atma-darsana, in the year
1901-1902. It was perhaps at about the same time that Baba
used    to see the vision of the Sun during his meditation.
Ranade explained that Baba used to sit in his room cover-
ing his face with a thick piece of cloth over his head so
that he could witness the brilliance of the vision of the sun
all the more in that artificial darkness. Which of the three
experiences is real, while talking with Shivalingavva Baba
asked her, the Particle-like vision of God that is seen in
the beginning, or the vision of the Atman           which comes
later on or the eventual experience of formless’'God. From
this it becomes clear that Baba had the experience of the
formless, Nirguna Brahman also. The fact that Baba was
Seeing his own immaculate form is also evident from the


“Jeevan darsan’ R. P. Kulkarni, 1966, Athani, Page 90
112    Prof. R. D.   Ranade .and His Spiritual Lineage


way he used to interpret a portion of Saint Tukarama’s`
Abhanga ‘my feet are on, your head,’ in such a way that
the apparent absurdity of meaning was removed, and the
real mystical meaning     was clearly   brought out.   Baba’s
interpretation, probably based on his own experience, was
that Tukarama saw his own immaculate form on the stone
idol of Vitthala.                                        `

      An interesting conversation between Baba and Bhim-
rao, his sister’s grand son, shows us clearly that Baba could
see the vision of Bhausahib Maharaja in the waking life,
even after the death of Bhausahib.       Bhimrao had taken
away the Tabla (drums) from the matha of Baba:to the
shrine of Bhausahib. “Why did you take away the Tabla?”
Baba asked Bhimrao. “I took it away because the Bhajana
was going on before the samadhi of Bhausahib.” “Is that
Samadhi of greater importance than the photo of Bhau-
sahib which we worship here?” asked Baba. Bhimrao re-
plied, ‘Maharaja is there where his samadhi is, how can he
be here?’ “What. is he not here? See, Maharaja is standing
right there.” Baba said to the boy calmly.

    Similarly, once on the birth anniversary day of the
Lord Krsna Baba said to Laxmana Bhatji, “Today one may
like to have an idol made of earth for the purpose of wor-
shipping Lord Krsna.” After finishing his morning medi-
tation Baba found Bhataji busy in preparing an idol out of
clay. So he asked him, “What are you doing, Bhataji?”
“Preparing the idol of Krsna, as you had asked me to do”
was the reply of Laxmanabhataji. “Why should I require an
idol, when I have seen the Lord in the form of a grown up
child? I thought some of the ladies perhaps may require
an idol for worshipping.”                 ।

   Baba too like Arjuna had the experience of seeing gods
and goddesses, including the three great divinities of
Brahma, Visnu and Sankara, as well as of the beautiful
heavenly damsels such as Rambha and Urvashi. Baba was
generally so much engrossed in meditation on the Name,
               Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri          113


and in the observation of the various sublime visions that
he used to lose the consciousness of his body and sing
merrily clapping his hands.

    A Brahmin used to worship the idol of Hanuman in a
temple beyond the brook. One day when Baba bowed down
before the idol, the Brahmin asked Baba, “How is it that
you are bowing down before this idol today?”        Baba re-
plied, “Do not misunderstand me, please, I am not bowing
down before your idol, I am saluting the God whom I see
over the head of your idol.” Had not Baba seen the vision
of God wherever he cast his glance, he would not have
remained calm and composed after he was forced to stop
visiting and bowing down at the samadhi of his Guru due
to certain circumstances.     He then   used to look at the
summit of the temple from outside, and return to his room
with satisfaction and joy.


    Once, instead of returning to the matha from his place
of meditation, he     went in the opposite direction towards
the village of Girgaum. “I became so much attracted by
the vision in front of me,” Baba used to tell, “that I lost
the sense of direction.      I became aware of going in the
wrong direction only when I found that I had           almost
reached Girgaum. This is why I came home at about three
in the afternoon, two hours later than usual.” Commenting
on the ritual of the Sandhya, Baba once remarked,      “Why
do you look in several directions while       performing your
sandhya and bow down with folded hands? Do you see the
various deities of Fire, Varuna, Indra etc. and then fold
 your hands?      You should see them first   and then salute
 them.   Otherwise, the deities will not know whom you are
 saluting.   Your folding of hands also will have meaning
 only after you recognize a particular deity.”     Baba also
 used to do as the Bhataji was doing, but the piece of ad-
 vice he gave to Laxamanabhataji is enough to make us
 understand that Baba used to salute the deities after seeing
 them first.
114       Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


      Hardly did any newspaper come to Inchgiri; and hardly
did Baba read anyone. Yet, strange to say that he asked
Professor Ranade one day if a great battle was being fought
somewhere on the face of the earth.        The    Professor was
surprised-to hear this, for, as a matter of fact, the first
world-war was then going on with alternate successes and
defeats on either side. ‘Yes,      a war is going on. But how
do you know this?” asked Ranade.        Baba said in reply, “I
saw the Atman fighting on this side one moment and fight-
ing on the other side the next, and killing several men on
each side.”    It is indeed wonderful that Baba should visu-
alize such a scene of international importance on the cos-
mic curtain,


      One day while he was deeply engrossed in meditation
in a place a mile away from the matha, he heard the sound
of a gallopping horse.    Looking around as he did not see
any, he resumed his meditation.       Again did    he   hear the
same sound.     This time he got up and looked in all direc-
tions. On not seeing any horse, he again resumed his medi-
tation,    But immediately after, he heard the words, “Why,
will you not get up?      Should    I run you     over with the
horse?” Baba thought to himself “Maharaja must have ar-
rived in the matha.” He hurriedly’ went and saw that
Bhausahib Maharaja had really come. Seeing Baba bowing
down before him, Bhausahib said to him, “You do not seem
to easily understand the subtle language of signs, must you
be run over by the horse?”

     To an aspirant, Baba said that he should see him the
next morning for being initiated. But when the man went
to him the next morning, Baba told him apologetically, “Oh,
I had heard the Name to be given to you, but I have for-
gotten it.    Please come tomorrow.”     The divine      Names
which were to be imparted to the seekers of spiritual know-
ledge, Baba used either to hear, or to see written before
him. Never did he impart a Name to anyone unless he
first heard it or saw it written before him, It is said that
            Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri                      115

he asked Mrs, Ranade to sit near the Samadhi of Bhau-
sahib, saying to her that Bhausahib himself would impart
the Name to her. Mrs. Ranade did as she was told, and she
heard the Name which she was to meditate upon. As soon
as she came out of the shrine, Baba uttered the ‘very name
and asked if it was the same as she had heard. To a
totally unknown man from Gwalior who“had come to
Baba for being initiated, because he had had 20 experience
though he was meditating on the Name given by his Guru,
Baba told that there was no need of a fresh initiation, but
that the man should add one letter to the Name he had re-
ceived from his Guru. Although Baba knew nothing about
the stranger’s Guru nor about the Name-he pointed out the
letter to be added.    During his last illness due to dropsy,
Baba had lost the consciousness of his body as well as that
of the outside world. Yet, to a disciple of his, he told the
exact number of the letters of the Name he had imparted
several months earlier. Then he uttered a Name and ‘asked
if that was the Name imparted earlier. When the disciple
replied in the negative, Baba.advised him to meditate either
on one of them or on both of them. This shows clearly that
Baba did not make any distinction between different names
that different disciples might have received from a realized
saint, for all the names were of one God only and so were
equally efficacious.   Of course, the hearing of new names,
especially belonging to different religions is      a mystery, and
the mystery becomes all        the     more inscrutable till one
understands that all the names are the names of the one
unnamable God.    When even the first Name which a              saint
meditates upon is itself a mystery, there should be no won-
der if it gives rise to other mysteries of the same kind.

    It   makes   no    sense   to    hold   the   view   that   there
can be only one saint or saviour for the entire world, or
one Name or Mantra for the purpose of meditation. For to

do so will make God finite and limited. Naturally, the de-
votee also will be sectarian, bigotted, fanatic and intoler-
ant, The mantra if it is too long and difficult to be pro-
116    Prof. R. 0. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


nounced, will soon bring fatigue, disappointment, lethargy
and sleep. Adding to this mess, music and musical instru-
ments, mikes and    loud-speakers,    decorations and     electric
lights, and gatherings   of   effiminate   men   and    garrulous
women, would no doubt, make a temporary            and outward
show of devotion but     create soon in     its place a     great
vacuum and confusion which will have no end. We must,
therefore, remember that real devotion is the most natural
and living bond between the devotee and God through the
intermediary of the Guru.   That natural bond takes the
form of meditation on the Name and contemplation on the
forms of God.    If there be no short Name which can be
easily pronounced    loudly    or   mentally and   remembered
with love and reverence,      and if there is no       living and
powerful realized Guru who would enable the disciple to
realize God directly and immediately there can be no real
devotion.   The exercise of devotion — whether meditation
or contemplation — can be carried on so silently and ment-
ally that the presence of one more person may be felt as a
crowd and of any more, a nuisance.


  Secrion G: BABA REMAINED TRUTHFUL TO THE
                              LAST


      What Baba spoke at the time of his leaving the body
is the summary, so to say, of his entire life. “All my
desires are burnt; I shall pass away uttering the divine
Name ‘Narayana’” were his last words. Throughout his life
since the day of initiation by his Guru, his only passion
was to become one with God. His desire for enjoying
married life and pleasures in life was put an end to by his
master. And his desire for earning wealth had withered
away by his poverty and helplessness even in the prime of
life. Whatever he earned by the sweat of his brow was
                                                     to
his, but it was so meagre that he had no other way but
remain contented with his lot. Neither for his own main-
tenance nor for any spiritual purpose did he covet any
             Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri                        117


money. He never believed that means are justified by the
ends, however noble the latter may be. Even the spread of
Paramartha by means of money obtained by winning of
lotteries was utterly condemned by him.            When the Raja
of Jamkhandi, on the occasion of the birth of a son, had
sent Baba a present of one hundred rupees and a beautiful
rich garment, Baba said to the Raja’s personal assistant
bringing the gift, “What shall we do with such rich pre-
sents?” He added five rupees to the present and returned
it   with   the   words   ‘It    should   be   considered     as     the
Prasada from the Maharaja.’ It was in 1932, that a disciple
of his sent to Baba a fine Italian woollen rug               with all
affection and sincerity.        Baba returned it     saying that he
was not in need of it but if the disciple wished, he could
go to Inchgiri and spend the money to feed those who medi-
tate. We are told that the disciple still remembers with gra-
titude that it was by the grace of Baba that he was ap-
pointed a teacher ia college.           He also remembers          with
regret that a year    later     that   appointment    was   suddenly
terminated, probably as the        result of his     abandoning the
idea of going to Inchgiri after having decided to go there
and having a vehicle waiting outside for him.               Baba un-
aware of these events         wrote to him     “God behaves with
men as they deserve.”      When he went to Sangli, one year
after he was initiated to pay his respects to Baba the latter
asked him to meditate three times a day, an hour in the
morning, one in the afternoon           and one 71     the evening.
Later on, when Baba inquired if he was meditating accord-
ingly, he told him with a puff of pride in him that he had no
time to meditate in the afternoon, as he had to attend the
college. “In that case, then. meditate for two hours in the
motning and two hours in the evening” said Baba.                   TEvid-
ently it became clear to the disciple that he had made the
mistake of arguing with Baba. Baba told him however
that his work in the college will be excellent if he medi-
tated foc two hours in the morning. This came out liter-
118    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


ally true during the next thirtyfive years of the disciple’s
career as a teacher


      To his disciples who invited him to come to his house,
he would tell, “You shall have to behave as I wish so long
as I stay with you.” And to others who went to the Matha,
he said that they shall have to behave according to his
wishes, so long as they remained in the Matha. In both
the cases, not the will of the disciples but the will of Baba
was    to prevail. This is quite consistent,   we think, with
Baba’s behaviour with respect to his Guru.       Baba obeyed
his Guru in all     matters, whatever might have been his
personal inclinations; for he believed that the chief quali-
fication of a disciple is to obey his Guru in every way.
Besides, if he were to behave as his disciples wished him
to behave, it would have been an impossible task for him,
as there were too many disciples.


Section H      BABA’s GUILELESS HOSTILITY TURNED
  INTO UNQUESTIONING FAITH AND SURRENDER
                       TO HIS GURU

   As described earlier, after the death of his wife Baba
was so much obsessed by the idea of marrying again that
excepting as 2 70168115 of fulfilling his   desire, Baba   was
hostile to the spiritual life as such. That is why he showed
his total reluctance’ to meet and be initiated by Sri Bhau-
sahib Maharaja who was waiting for him. This hesitation
on the part of Baba explains why perforce was he taken
by a strong man before Sri Bhausahib who then first
tempted him to have a pinch of tobacco, and then induced
him to be initiated in altogether a new life. How could
however Baba be made to renounce that which he wished
to have first and foremost in life? The urge for not only
a legal and a moral married life was to be sacrificed but
a total ban was to be put on his instinct of sex. Baba
plainly told Bhausahib that the very two conditions of
spiritual life namely, of not having a desire for the wealth
           Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgirt                    119


and woman not belonging to him, might not be fulfilled by
him. Bhausahib told him that he need not worry               about
that inasmuch as the responsibility of his leading a moral
life lay on him meaning Bhausahib.            Baba then had 70
other alternative but to submit to the will of Bhausahib.
Accordingly, he was initiated on      Chaitra Sudi Purnima,
sake 1814 (1892. A.D.) Since that very moment Baba’s in-
domitable will coupled with unflinching faith in his Guru
became as if a great challenge to his desire of getting him-
self married a second time.


   But now he was not alone to fight the battle. The great
spiritual strength and the grace    of   Bhausahib Maharaja
was on his side. With his gaze fixed on the tip of the nose
and the divine Name on his tongue or along with his breaths
he began his twofold journey back to Jagjivani on foot as
well as uphill on the spiritual path. For continuous three
months he did not change the direction of his gaze, nor
did he forget to meditate silently on the Name, Though
he suffered severe from trouble of his eyes he did not
mind it. Occasionally he became so absorbed in meditation
that he forgot his   hunger   and   thirst;    he   would   take a
morsel of food in his hand and keep it so for a long time
without trying to put it into his mouth, His elder brother
Gopalrao and his sister, Jivabai thought that he had gone
mad on account of his not being married. They tried to
console him.



   We have already described how on account of unceas-
ing meditation for three months with his gaze fixed on
the tip of his nose, Baba began seeing nothing but the
Waves of light. Baba who did not know anything about the
nature of spiritual experience, complained to Sri Bhausahib

that he was unable to see anything and that he had gone
blind. We have also seen how Bhausahib convinced Baba
that the light he saw was the experience of God, that is,
of the Atman or the Brahman,
{20    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


      On receiving   this assurance from Bhausahib that what
he was seeing was the experience of God, there was no
limit to Baba’s joy. Bhausahib also told him that nothing
could eclipse the vision of the Lord, but that the vision could
eclipse everything else.   Being aware however as to why
Amburao thought himself to have gone blind,       Bhausahib
told him to meditate since then for fixed periods of time,
that is, for six hours in the morning and three hours in the
evening. According to this fresh order of his Guru, Ambu-
rao began to meditate for nine hours a day till his death
in 1933 without a single exception.


      Baba’s meditation    was so regular and punctual and
he did it with so determined a will that his experiences
began to be more and more vivid and his faith in his Guru
became absolute, All his desires for worldly pleasures began
to wither away; they were burnt in the fire of his medita-
tion. His love for God and for His Name became so absorb-
ing that he could not tolerate any disturbance or engage-
ment during the hours of his      meditation.   Once at   Jam.
khandi, a disciple decided to get.Baba photographed and
so he called in a barber and a photographer. He then got
Baba out of his meditation for a shave and a photograph.
Baba became extremely annoyed, came out of the room and
prostrated before the barber. The barber immediately left
the place. Seeing how the barber was treated by Baba,
the photographer also went away and Baba resumed_his
meditation. It is indeed very difficult to judge whether his
passion for meditation was greater than his faithful obedi-
ence to his Guru or vice versa. On another occasion when
he had already covered the distance of twentyone miles
from Vijapur to Inchgiri on foot walking behind Bhau-
sahib who was on a horse — back, he had the patience
to overlook the tormenting hunger, and go in for medita-
tion for three hours at the behest of Bhausahib.

   On one occasion, when Baba was fiftyfive, Bhausahib
Maharaja came to Inchgiri quite unexpectedly, and asked
             Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri                121


 Baba if all was well with him and if all of his anxieties
 had been taken care of. “I am now old and so there re-
 mains no cause for any anxiety now” replied Baba. “Good”
  said Bhausahib, “My responsibility is now over” and went
` into his room. To Baba it was a restless night. He realized
  that he had committed a great mistake in saying to Maha-
 raja that there should be no worry any longer. In the early
 morning next day Bhausahib rode away for Umadi. With
 remorse and sorrow he struggled in his mind to ease him-
 self, but it was all in vain.   Even at the age of 55 he ran
 after the horse for about three miles, Bhausahib casually
 looked behind and seeing Baba running towards him, he
 stopped the horse and got       down.   “Why   have you come
 panting   after   me in   such a   manner, Amburao?”     asked
 Bhausahib. “Forgive me sir,” saying so Baba fell prostrate
` at his master’s feet, and said to him in a piteous but solemn
 voice, ‘I shall   not leave these holy feet, until I get an as-
 surance from you that you will protect me till the end of
 my life.’ “As Bhausahib was all love for Baba, he did say,
 “Don’t repeat it, I shall protect you till the end of your
 life.” In reply to this Baba said, “Your word is law unto
 me and since this day my body and mind are completely
 given to you.”

     Baba’s words were not a lip-service only. He behaved
 as he said. When once down with fever, he thought 1८
 should have a holiday to his daily routine of rigorous medi-
 tation.   So, he walked back and forth in front of Bhau-
 Sahib’s room in order to draw his attention.       “Why have
 you come, Amburaya,” inquired the master. “I am suffer-
 ing from severe cold and fever” said Baba. Bhausahib in-
 terrupted Baba saying,” “Then go and sit for daily medita-
  tion.” Without a murmur Baba went into his room and
 began his meditation. Soon he perspired profusely, and the
  temperature came to normal. Baba used to narrate this
 ieident saying that his Guru’s word could remove the
 fever and enable him to complete his three hours’ medita-         ,
 tion as well.
, 122    Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


        Once while passing through      Tikota, a village       near
 Vijapur Bhausahib      said to Baba,    “The samadhi of your
 Guru, Savalsang      Maharaja is here in        Tikota.   Go there,
 offer a coconut and wave the light before the samadhi by
 burning a little camphor.” At this Baba told   Bhausahib
 frankly, “I have no other Guru except you, and I need not.
 do all that you tell me to do.” “I say, you should do as I
 wish. Will you then do it or not?” said Bhausahib. “Cer-
 tainly, I will,” so saying Baba did all that his Guru had told
 him to do. Similarly at Jath also, he obeyed his master much
 againt his wish and offered a coconut in the matha of
 Shivalingavva (also a disciple of Bhausahib). Similarly
 again in Vijapur, Baba reluctantly took his seat in a tonga
 alongside his Guru at the order of the latter. Out of res-
 pect for Bhausahib,    Baba sat    shrivelled    up   in a corner.
 Seeing him seated in such      a   clumsy manner          Maharaja
 asked him to get down from the tonga,             Instantly Baba
 jumped      from the tonga   and began to       run after    it.   In
 all these instances of his behaviour there was not visible
 any trace of his hostile nature. The change from unwill-
 ingness to obedience was so sudden and smooth, that it
 was as if no change at all.    His apparent hostility and re-
 luctance were so thin that it required the mere indication
 of his Guru’s wish to break it. Probably, he became aware
 of his prior reluctance after he had obeyed his master.

        It is this inherent contradiction of his nature which
 ean be said to be the inverted image of his soul, Inter-
 nally he was as soft as butter; externally, his skin was as
 soft as silk excepting the skin of his back which he had
 deliberately exposed to the sun during the six hours of
 daily meditation for a long continuous period of more than
 forty years. To his Guru, he was all humility and obedi-
 ence; to others, though he was most kind and compassionate,
 his uncompromising truthfulness and unpretentiousness took
 sometimes the extreme form of unyielding assertion. As a
 consequence, he appeared to be indifferent to the feelings
 of others. At Athani he happened to put up with Annarao
                  Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri                 123 .

    , Deshpande who being a Vaisnava had a strong prejudice
     against the spiritual path chosen by Baba. But as a res-
     pectable house-holder he requested Baba to dine at his
     place. Baba told him that he took his         meals and drank
  water only in the houses of those who were disciples of
  Bhausahib, and so requested Annarao to excuse him, Anna-
  rao then repeatedly requested Baba to accept some milk and
, fruits. But Baba declined to have that also, telling Anna-
     rao that if was his rule not to take anything at the hands of
     those who were not initiated, and as such he should be ex-
     cused,    Baba, however, did his meditation of three hours
     at Annarao’s house. Perhaps, as a result of this, a few years
     later, Annarao got himself initiated by       Professor Ranade
_    and got a building built at       Nimbal    Ashram,     known.as
     ‘Dhyana-mandir.’

          ‘One residing in a city would have thought by looking
     at Baba’s attire that he was one of those illiterate persons
_ who are blind followers of traditional beliefs, customs and
    ‘superstitions.   Not only was he totally different from all
     such ignorant people, but also there could have been hard-
     ly such a thoughtful, critical, and open-minded person like
    . Baba.    He was in every detail of the affairs of life, sym-
     pathetic, considerate and full of common sense.          “I weep
     and mourn over the death of my Guru by night and not by
     day, for if I myself begin to weep,”        said he to Professor
     Ranade, “who will console these bereaved souls who come
     to   me    with -their   hearts   striken   with   grief?’   When
_    Tumours began      to spread   that even after Bhausahib      was
     cremated, he talked with someone, ate and drank with an-
     other, was seen at the house of someone,           Baba criticised
     all this as nothing but idle talk. All such rumours might
     have been useful to false propagandists and to aspiring
     false Gurus, but Baba brushed them aside with scorn and
     Yidicule. “How can the body. of my Guru which was turn-
     ed into ashes     ‘reappear at any time?” was         his straight
     question to all. As there began to appear more and more
      Gurus in the matha and as there arose bickerings and: quar-
124     Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


rels as to who was superior to whom, Baba silently moved
away from the scene and was content to celebrate the anni-
versary days of his Guru in his own small room.           To a
young boy who immediately after he was initiated said
that he had the vision of Lakshminarayana, Baba‘ told that
it was quite alright, but that he should meditate more and
more.   To others, however he explained that what the boy
needed was encouragement, but that so far as the vision
of Lakshminarayana was concerned it was nothing but a
memory-image of what the boy had seen in a picture pre-
viously and had    heard it from others that the picture was
of Lakshminarayana. For, to have such a vision would re-
quire meditation on the Name of God for a very long time,
and that it should have its beginning in the vision of subtle
self-effulgent   form of God as small as a particle of dust.

    A truth which is verifiable was alone acceptable to
Baba. Even while Bhausahib was alive, he had told Baba
to initiate the aspirants and increase the devotion. Baba
 was too shy to do so, so long as his Guru was living. But
even after his death, when people were clamouring for be-
ing initiated, when several elderly persons requested Baba
to initiate the seekers, Baba did not give his consent. He
wished to receive the order from his Guru, either in a
dream or in meditation. Accordingly, Bhausahib appeared
twice in his meditation and asked him to spread the cause
of spiritual life. He had to obey the order. However in
order to have a sign or proof that his act of initiation would
bear the fruit of God-realization, he made his first experi-
ment on a man named Vishnupant Parulekar, who had no
faith in spiritual life but who was very truthful and
honest. It seems Baba must have thought that          faith or
no faith, just as medicine must be effective, even so, the
Name which he was going to impart to Vishnupant must
bear fruit if it had power in it. Besides, truthfulness is not
a small virtue. When Vishnupant came out of the shrine
of Bhausahib ‘screamirig that he was frightened by the ex-
perience of lightning and thunder   as he was meditating
              Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri                   125

 when as a matter of fact there was no sign of lightning
or thunder in the sky, Baba was convinced that the Name
imparted by him to Vishnupant was potent and not hollow.
Thus did he verify the truth even in the spiritual life.

   Baba was most adament in sticking to what he con-
sidered to be the truth and to what his master “had told
him to do. We the men of practical day to day utility may
not agree with him and may like to compromise the truth
with what is useful-to us. The attitude of Baba was un-
compromising. A certain lady had prepared a woollen cap
for Baba out of great affection and reverence for him.
Baba too liked the cap, but asked her as to what he should
pay her as the price of the cap. In vain did she try to ex-
plain to Baba that the question of price did not arise at
all, since she had prepared it for him out of affection. “But
you must have paid for the wool and so you should have
no objection in accepting that much money from me,” said
Baba.   But    the   lady   appeared   to   be   disappointed.   So
Baba give his old cap in exchange and then accepted the
new one.




   Section I:    GIST OF THE REMINISCENCES AND
        LETTERS OF SRI AMBURAO MAHARAJA

    On being inquired as to what the propriety was in
asking the aspirant before he was initiated to say on oath
that he would not entertain any desire for other’s wealth
and women, Baba replied that it was a moral binding only.
“What can the teacher do if the disciple breaks his oath?
Will he file a suit against him? The promise that the disci-
ple makes on oath is made to God, and so God will decide
as to what should be done in case the            oath is broken.”
“This moral binding too,” Baba added further, “is meant
for those who are willing to be bound by it. The discipline
of the school is meant for those who enrol themselves as
students and not for the others who are wandering
                                                  outside
the school.”
126     Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


      A certain   man received   a letter of   congratulations
from Baba on       his being
                         blessed with  the birth of a
daughter. The man however wrote in reply to Baba that
the birth of a daughter was not a matter of joy and congra-
tulations, since the burden and responsibility of the father
were only increased. At about the same time, another per-
son wrote to Baba with joy that his daughter had passed
the Matriculation examination. Baba told the congregation
that one does not understand that God will undertake the
responsibility of nourishing the daughter, while the other
forgets that his matriculated daughter will leave him soon
and go to reside in her husband’s house.       After all, man
becomes either unnecessarily anxious or joyful because he
identifies himself with the imaginary     anxieties or joyful
occasions, and takes pride in doing so.


      To do something out of enthusiasm or out of the’ vain
desire of being praised by others, was never encouraged
by Baba. A poor man who escorted Baba from Inchgiri to
Satara invited him and his party of about twenty persons
for dinner and said with humility that he would not mind
the expenses. Baba told him plainly that it was not at all
necessary for the spiritual life and one should do only that
which he could do with ease. “If you feel that you should
have ‘Prasad’ distributed, bring some sugar worth about a
quarter of a rupee and after offering it to God distribute it
and partake a little of it. That is your ‘Prasada.’ And sup-
pose you spend a lot of money for the dinner of us all, will
not people call you a fool, and me an unthinking Guru?”

      On seeing people: bowing down before some elderly
and spiritually advanced sadhakas, Baba told the sadha-
kas that they should consider themselvesfit to receive salu-
tations only when they would lose awareness of the identity
of the person. saluting them. Thus they should not know
whether the person saluting was a male or a female. Baba
explained this further by saying, “This condition of the
mind and the senses will arise only when the person accept-
           Sri Amburao Maharaja of Inchgiri              127

ing the salutations sees the vision of God even before and
in the presence of the approaching person.” Without this
state of mind, to allow others to salute is to commit a sin
from the spiritual point of view. A person with a constant
vision of God before him will know that it is the God and
not. him who is being saluted.

    How fine would it be, thought Baba, if men would
only.give up wrong notions, wrong thinking, and the crea-
tion of false philosophies! Someone asked him as to what
harm was there in being a non-vegetarian when, while one
eats anything   he must be    swallowing   minute invisible
germs, Baba silenced him by      asking   “Why do you    not
drink the water from the gutter?” Similarly, he made the
distinction between one who remembers the Name of God
while doing his works, and one who does his works while
remembering the Name of God. To the former, the work
is more important than the Name; to the latter, the Name
is more important than the works.    So, the difference bet-
ween the two modes of behaviour is that in the first case,
the works will be binding a man by their fruits; in the
second case they will be dissolved along with their fruits
by the power of the divine Name. Man will be accustomed
to do such kind of activity only when      he cultivates the _
habit of saying to himself, “My business in life is to remem-
ber the Name of God; otherwise I am simply a corpse hav-
ing nothing to do with works which yield pleasures and
joys of the world.”


    Regarding the nature and the function of the Guru,
Baba had very clear ideas.   “If one can attain the moral
perfection by his own efforts, why then is the Sadguru re-
quired at all?” asked he. None can be morally perfect with-
out devotion and the grace of the Guru or God. It is enough
if man turns his back upon the wealth and women of others,
and then approaches the Guru for spiritual enlightement
and for the acquisition of other virtues. And who after
 all is the Guru?   It is not the body but the Atman or the
128    Prof. R. 7. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


God in him who is the Guru. In fact, it is not also the body
of the disciple who is disciple, but the Atman in him. The
Atman thus becomes the Guru as well as the disciple,
though the bodies of both of them are essential for the
purpose of initiation. The Guru can impart the Name of
God because he has a body. But when this function of the
Atman or the Guru is over, .there remains for the disciple
the duty of meditating on the Atman and discovering for
himself that he is nothing but the Atman, and not at all the
body, the senses,  the mind, the intellect and egoism


      Baba once told a disciple of his that from the view-
point of the saint there is no difference between a stranger
and a relative or a friend.     The saint has his eye on the
quality of the heart of the person who approaches him and
10६ on his body, whether it is of a friend or of a stranger.
It is therefore immaterial for the saint whether the per-
son speaks well or ill about him.     If the person is a sin-
cere seeker of truth,    the saint guides him and brings him
on the spiritual path.    The saint does so because he bears
compassion for the fallen and the sinful.   It is in this sense
that the saint is considered as greater than God, who with-
out the intermediary of the saint has the sense of justice
only. God becomes compassionate because of the interven-
tion of the saint, who is always forgiving and merciful.
Therefore, it behoves a disciple to consider every word that
comes from the mouth of a spiritual teacher as if it is a
word of God. Verily, there is no difference between God
and the saint.

    Baba’s conviction that the divine Name is the essence
of spiritual life was so deep-rooted in his mind that on one
occdsion at Jejuri near Pune, when he was suffering from
high fever, he requested the doctor attending upon him to
clear up his congested nose so that he would be able to
meditate without any difficulty along with the incoming
and the outgoing breaths.      As for his fever, he told the
doctor that he was unconcerned      whether the    fever was
            Sri Amburio Maharaja of Inchgiri                   129

there or not. His only concern was that he should be able
to meditate so long as he lived. Very often he used to say
that there is nothing so real      as the Name of God.        The
present writer remembers it very well, how even during
his terminal illness of dropsy when he had lost almost all
consciousness of his surroundings, Baba used to tell him
to hold fast the Name, and not to mind anything else.           It
is indeed very remarkable that he never complained about
his painful condition or any other discomfort, but spoke
always about the supreme value of meditation, “Do not use
a mosquito-curtain, though I am using it; for, I do not
sleep, but you will.   Let the mosquitoes keep you awake
and enable you to meditate” he told the present writer.


    One should never think of what becomes of his medi-
tation, or of the. quantitative    measure   of it.    He should
meditate till his last breath.   Some people speak about the
supramental condition as the goal of meditation. Baba used
to ask, “What will you do, supposing you attain this condi-
tion? Do you wish to stop your meditation?         In that case
you can stop it even without attaining that condition.” The
Supramental condition is not a way of escape from doing

meditation, but is the restful condition of joy and peace
which can be had only by the process of continual concen-
trated meditation.   It is such a kind of meditation which
will be the constant support      of the feeling of     Vairagya,
meaning disgust for all worldly objects and the progenitor
of real and various spiritual experiences. A fit of Vairagya
which might spring up at the cremation or the burial ground
will not last beyond a short time because it is not backed
up by meditation. On the other hand, one who meditates
daily and for a considerable length of time at fixed hours

regularly and punctually will be     endowed with Vairagya,
on account of the sheer power of the       Name,      as it is im-
parted by the Guru. This real Vairagya will be felt by the
meditator as very effective in driving away all other
                                                      God-
Jess ideas which cross and recross the mind with great
tenacity and force. After all these ideas arise out of
                                                       imagi-
130     Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


nation, which moves round our desires. The Name of God
comes directly from God through the medium of the Guru
and being' devoid of imagination is full of divine power
and so is able to crush and destroy both the imagination
and the desires. In 8 sense, the desires are burnt in the fire
of meditation, and not simply suppressed and pushed back
in the antechaniber of the mind.


      Unlike the. orthodox. devotionless activists Baba was
too rational to believe that the realization of     God is not
possible in the present age as it was in the ancient time
for-the great sages.   Everything 1.6. God, nature and man,
being the   same, Baba could    not   understand    why   there
should not be uniformity so far as the realization of God
was concerned.    An idle notion he thought, can have no
support of reason, much less of direct experience.        It is
like believing that fire or water has got a different nature
today from what it was once upon a time.           In fact, the
nature of God-realization, the description of which we find
in the works of the ancient sages is believed to be true
today, because the same identical experiences can he had
even today. And supposing that God cannot be realized in
the present time, then there is no use in reading the scrip-
tures as if they are authoritative. They have no value for
man except the pleasure they might yield like that of read-
ing romantic novels. It is enough for us, Baba used to say
that we have the experience of God exactly as our spiritual
teachers had, and so, we have nothing to do with the
opinions of arm-chaired idlers and pretenders.
