                Shri Gurudeva

Prof.    R.     D.       RANADE       of     NIMBAL

        Birth        :   July   3rd 1886

        Death :          June   6th 1957
                      CHAPTER IV

              THE SUMMIT AT NIMBAL
                    (Prof. R. 20. Ranade)
                                                 च




          Section A: A BRIEF LIFE-SKETCH

    Hailing from Jamkhandi, a village in the Devagadh
taluk of the Ratnagiri district in the Konkan, the Ranades
came to settle in the beginning of the 19th century in
Ramadurg, a small state about 250 miles south of Poona.
As back as 1740, the original ancestor, named, Hari, who
was greatly devoted to Rama had built a temple in honour
of the deity. His great grandson, and the great grandfather
of R. D, Ranade also known as Hari, being equally devoted
to a great saint known as Chidambara Swami of Murgod in
the district of Belgaum, had built a temple in honour of his
spiritual teacher. One ‘Hari’ was devoted to God, and the
other ‘Hari’ to the Guru as to God. Ranade’s mother too was
a pious and devoted lady who could in no small measure,
mould the life of her son. This explains how following the
law of heredity, Professor Ranade came to realize God
through his devotion to his Guru namely, Bhausahib Maha-
raja of Umadi. He might not have built a temple in stone,
but realizing first that his own body was a temple of God,
he made hundreds of his followers to realize God.         This
constitutes the great and the unique achievement of the life
of the Professor. In a way, he was both the creature of
heredity and the creator and pioneer of a great spiritual
movement which is universal, because it is rational, devo-
tional, and mystical.

    Nevertheless, none should say that Ranade was, as is
wrongly supposed   about   the incarnations   of God,   a full-
fledged mystic even at the start of his spiritual career. He
must have got all the potentiality of being so, but he was
like any other great saint a mystic in the making; for, in
supposing this lies the hope that any one can follow in
132      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


the footsteps of a great saint, and make himself approxi-
mate to the ideal, in whatever degree it might be possible
for him to do so.   We have therefore to address ourselves
the question as to what it was that made Ranade spiritually
great.   No doubt it is difficult to fathom the life-history of
the Professor and to find out correctly and precisely the
motives which shaped his character and conduct in both his
mundane and spiritual aspects of life. One thing is certain
about him, that his outer and inner lives were ever con-
sistent with each other; they were the obverse and the
reverse of each other; rather, the counterparts of each other,
there is nothing hidden in the one or the other, Perhaps,
this much difference might be there; one is implicit and
jnarticulate,   and the other explicit and articulate.    That
is why for a close, sincere observer it was never difficult
to find his heart on his palm.     To others who lacked   this
insight Ranade might have appeared as having had a double
mind. With an autobiographical touch, as if, he himself says
about the imaginary Professor in his article, ‘The centre of
the   Universe’, that “he manifested his precious soul to
those fortunate few who, after a long apprenticeship had
come to win his confidence.”      To those who judged him
from what he appeared to be, he was more or less a lunatic,
and it seems that they might have been encouraged by
him in believing so.    Though, in course of time, such be-
lievers were completely ruined.

      To the question then how to account for his extraordi-
nary greatness — his matchless intellectual heights, emo-
tional depths of humanism, sympathy and love, and keen
sense of duty and conscientiousness — we think we can
give the reply that it is all due to the presence in him of
four great virtues, two of which can be said to be innate
with him, but all of which were developed by him in the
course of his life. They are (i) Correct intellectual grasp
of the situation; (ii) Indomitable courage to face the diffi-
culties and dangers; (iii) unshakable faith in his spiritual
Teacher; and (iv) the power acquired by meditation on the
                 The Summit at Nimbal                         133

Name of God. From his early childhood superb was his in-
quisitiveness. By seeing the blue eyes of a horse he asked
his mother whether that animal was born in the Konkan
strip of the western part of the country or on the.table-land,
since he had heard that men living in the two regions had
their eyes either resembling those of a cat, or blue or dark
in colour. Keen was his observation, and resolute his will.
Even at five, when he had gone with his mother to visit a
great saint at Jamkhandi, he could observe that the saint
was looking at the sky while he was foretelling his mother
that the child would be a great saint. Hearing a casual
remark of his teacher that the English hand-writing of
Ranade was not good, the very next morning Ranade was
found busy at the copy-book in order to improve his hand-
writing; and the result was that within a few months’ time
Ranade’s hand-writing was considered as the model for all
other students of his school. The same about the knowledge
of the Sanskrit language.
                       In a village like Jamkhandi, 34
miles away from the Railway station, where there was no
facility of any kind to learn a language thoroughly, nor any
help coming from any body, Ranade depended on his own
intelligence and efforts and secured the most coveted first
Jagannath Shankarseth     scholarship   at   the     Matriculation
Examination of the Bombay University in 1902. And, when
a learned Shastri asked him as to how far did he have the
knowledge of Sanskrit, he placed a Sanskrit dictionary be-
fore the Shastri and told him to ask him anything about
any word contained in the dictionary. Unabated lasted this
zest for acquiring knowledge not only during his career
as a student   but   also throughout his     life.   At the   M.A.
Examination of the Bombay University in 1914, when he
stood first and got the Chancellor’s Medal, one of the exa-
miners is reported to have written on his answerbook that
the “examinee knows more than the examiner”.        One feels
that Carlyle’s dictum, ‘A hero is a hero at all points’ is pre-
cisely true in the case of Professor Ranade. For, whatever
may be the subject to which he applied his intellect, he
134     770. R. 73. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


excelled and was never second to anybody. His knowledge
of Greek, for instance, was so deep and engrossing that his
articles about Parmenides, Heracleitus, Aristotle etc, not
only won the appreciation of the then great Greek scholar,
Professor Bain of the Deccan College, but found their way
10 England when the Professor left for home. He left the
whole of his library of Greek and Latin books for Pro-
fessor Ranade, but took Ranade’s few articles with him.

      His intellect again proved to be a powerful instru-
ment for him afterwards in solving a great philosophical
problem before him, namely, as to how it is that the non-
dualistic philosophy of Sri Sankaracarya could be com-
patible with devotion to a Personal God.    During his visit
to Banaras he happened to listen in the Matha of Sankara-
carya the song of devotion, namely, ‘Be devoted to the Lord
Govinda — a lesson in Grammar, when the death is at
hand, will not save you.”    This gave him the impetus to
make a thorough study of Indian Philosophy and of Mysti-
cism as taught by the saints of India. His intellect it was
which came to his help when he was confronted with tempt-
ing situations in life. With the help of the affectionate
(Professor Woodhouse, and of the theosophist leader in
the country, Dr. Anne Besant who was greatly impressed
by him when he visited her in Banaras, he could have
risen to any position in the world-wide theosophical move-
ment at that time. But there was, on the other side, the
hope of realizing God by means of silent meditation on His
Name, as instructed by his Guru, Bhausahib Maharaja of
Umadi. With intellect, will and courage, he could set aside
all the temptations which Theosophy offered him then.
Some note-worthy points about Prof. Ranade:
     1. Ranade used to send ten rupees every year for the
festival of Sri Chidamberdixit, in view of the fact that his
great grandfather was devoted to him.

   2, Ranade’s mother also was a disciple of a saint,
namely Sri Sambhuling Swami. After one thousand repeti-
                   The Summit at Nimbal            ,               135

tions of the name she used to mark a finger print on a wall.
When Ranade was four years old his mother took him to
Sambhulinga Swami who then predicted that Ranade would
be a great saint, Ranade could remember even in his old
age that the saint was then looking at the sky continuously.

     3. Ranade was born on 3rd July, 1886 and was named
as   Ramachandra     in     memory     of   the   grace     of    Uma
Rameshwar.

    4. At Jamkhandi, Ranade while he was yet in his
teenage, used to visit the temple of Hanuman everyday,
and while going to the school, he used to repeat a stotra of
Hanuman,

     5.   Once in 1899, he was saved from being drowned by
his friend Kallu Bhokare and Mr. Bhokare was responsi-
ble for his initiation by Shri Bhausahib Maharaja at Jam-
khandi on the occasion of Vaikuntha chaturdasi and he be-
gan to meditate on the name of God by means of a rosary
and as a result of this it is said that he could get Jagan-
natha Shankaraseth Scholarship, 1902.

     This increased his faith in the efficacy of name as well
as in the worth of his Guru, and his meditation was on
the increase day by day. His devotion increased so much
that he became as if a prince in the worldly life as well
as in the spiritual life.

     6.   As his spiritual exercise increased in intensity, he
began to feel the burden of his orthodox rituals.

     7.   Ranade believed that meditation on the name of -
God in any posture and in any condition would necessarily
be fruitful since every such name was to fall in the bag held

     8.   Bhausahib appeared in his dream four times.             Once
open at all times by God.

 he wanted to garland Ranade, next he wanted to apply
 marks of sandle paste      on   his   forehead   and     third   time
 Maharaja asked him to read the ‘Dasabodha’ and for the
136     = Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


 fourth time he asked him to stand before God as a servant
 of God with a stick in hand.


      On all these occasions Ranade refused to do as Bhau-
 sahib wished.     Afterwards he writes about this:    ‘All my
faults have led me   nearer to God. My very sins have
been incentives to spiritual life.’ He passed his M.A., in 1908
winning the Bhaudaji prize of Sanskrit; yet he never allow-
ed his meditation to suffer.

      9.    He used to get signs about the future events. His
father was extremely ill. In his dream he received a tele-
gram in which       the place from where the telegram was
written was the land of the dead, and the contents of the
telegram were “I have arrived here safely” and the tele-
gram was signed in the name of his father. Exactly at the
time of receiving the telegram his father        had died, at
Jamkhandi while Professor Ranade was in Poona.

      10.   Before heginning to do anything he used to pray
to God because he believed that the real doer of all things
is God. Once Bhausahib Maharaja being pleased with
Ranade for reading ‘Dasabodha’ said to Nagappa, the grand-
son of Nimbargi Maharaja. “Ranade would spread the name
of your grandfather beyond the seven seas. I asked him to
read the ‘Dasabodha’ on a particular day, to remove his
egoism.”


   Section       B: RANADE AS A MAN OF THE WORLD

      It is as difficult to differentiate between the worldly
and the non-worldly or the spiritual lives of a great saint,
as it is to differentiate between the two sides of an equation
or between the two halves of a spherical substance. Rather
we should say that like a river which runs through its
two banks or like the life itself which is bounded on two
sides by birth and death, the secular and the spiritual are
not much different from each other in the case of a soul
who has become one with the Divine. Therefore, we should
                     The Summit at Nimbal                     137

be careful in distinguishing between the secular and the
Spiritual aspects of the life of a saint. Has the water of a
river two tastes, one at this bank and the other at that bank?
Is not the consciousness of being alive one and the same
from the cradle to the grave? Why then should we try to
find out the difference between the saint’s behaviour with
his fellow beings and his behaviour with other saints and
God? At the best, we can say that there are two view-
points from which we can study the behaviour of a saint.
How does he appear from the human point of view, and
how does    he   appear    from   the   divine   point   of view?
Whether we should judge him as a man of this world or as
a full-fledged devotee of God? Whatever may be the man-
ner in which he is evaluated, let us not forget that we are
trying to know a saint, and not a man who knows nothing
about saintliness.    The saint is still   a man and as men we
want to know him. Yet, even at the start of our inquiry we
have to assume that he is a man endowed already with the
saintly character, that there is in him no mere worldly wis-
dom but a mixture of it with spiritual insight.          To speak
about him will necessarily be to speak about his social and
moral conduct as informed by the contact and grace of God.


    With Ranade, therefore, as he himself says in “The
Bhagavad Gita as the Philosophy of God-realization” devo-
tion to God is the central virtue from which flow all other
moral and social virtues as are described in a treatise on
Ethics.   Even the lists of virtues which are mentioned in
the Bhagavad Gita are nothing but the corollaries from a
theorem of God-realization. As an iron ball derives the
quality of burning due to its contact with fire, even so a
‘saint acquires the divine qualities on account of his contact
with God. Just as God is so becomes a perfected saint.As
God is said by Aristotle to be contemplating upon Himself,
the great and the foremost quality of a saint is that.he con-
templates always on his own immaculate form, which is
called as the Atman. The next virtue which is necessary
to maintain the experience of the Atman is to meditate on
138    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


the divine Name with love, regard and concentration. Along
with this, he creates a congenial atmosphere of devotion
for himself and for others in an assembly of sadhakas.
Whenever he finds sinners and defaulters, he takes com-
passion on them and pardoning them, teaches them the
way of the Lord without being disturbed by the ill-treat-
ment and insults hurled at him by the ignorant people of
the   world.   With his   indomitable   courage   and patience
throughout the miseries and dangers of life he finds the
springs of joy, peace and ecstasy. Like.God he too extends
his helpful hand of grace to save the humanity from being
drowned in ignorance and misery.        He has no foes to des-
troy. He behaves:as the friend and the servant of all. He
has no relatives, only those who are devotees of God are
his relatives. There being nothing else but God before him,
he is always fearless and joyful. He sees God in all and
all in God, so much so that all the duals such as pleasure
and pain, honour and dishonour are to him different aspects
or manifestations of God.

     His whole life turns upon two hinges namely, morality
on the one hand and spiritual discipline on the other. Tem-
ptations of wealth, prosperity, progeny, women and power
do not touch him at all. Being pure like fire, he considers
the |pleasures of life  as the vomit of a dog. He lives
in the world alone like a rhinoceros and minds his own
business of being devoted to God. It is through the saint
that God brings salvation to man; it is on account of him
that God who ig just becomes merciful and forgiving; it is
through him that Kingdom of God is established on this
earth, Ranade was such a saint. We may call him as the
representative or an incarnation of God. Though he was
fighting with his own life-long disease from 1909 till the
end of his life in    1957, he cared and    struggled for the
highest good of all those who approached him with humi-
lity and surrender.
    He never spoke ill about any one. Not that he was
slow to understand the defects in others, but what he
                 The Summit at Nimbal                     139

always tried to do was to find out some good point in
others.   A pleader’s hand-writing was extremely bad,
Ranade said that it was legible still. A student of his had
no ambition, Ranade said about him that he was contented.
A teacher of philosophy was quite prosaic, Ranade said
about him that the teacher could become one with the
subject he taught. He never allowed prejudice or presup-
position to have an upper hand on him. That is why he
could easily forget the past of other men if it was bad and
not mind the uncertain future, but care for the present
which could be moulded in the way in which he would like
it to be. On this very account he was unpredictable and
very often wrongly judged by people as “being a man of
moods and whims.”
    As nothing was-higher than God to him, none was
allowed to disturb him at the time of his meditation. Es-
pecially, if one were to inform him immediately after his
bath even about the arrival of a very important person in
the country, he would become red with emotion and say,
“Why do you not cut my throat? Can there be anything or
any person higher than God?”      There was a high official,
Superintendent of the R.M.S., who told Professor Ranade
that sometimes he did not like even to attend his office,
‘when he was rapt in meditation and divine consciousness,
Ranade heard it but did not reply. Thinking that Ranade
had not listened to what he said, the gentleman repeated
what he had said. Ranade again did not utter a word in
reply but asked the gentleman: “O, what are you saying?”
Being encouraged as if, the officer again told the same story.
To this Ranade replied with all seriousness, “If that be
the state of your mind during meditation, do not care for
the office. Let anything happen when you are engaged in
paying the highest allegiance.to God.” The officer. had no
reply, and there never came any occasion of his remaining
absent from his office.

    He had to ‘deal with all sorts of persons, high and low,
rich and poor, learned and illiterate, arrogant and humble,
140     Prof. R. 0. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

men and women, young and old, devoted and unbelievers,
but he lost no opportunity to remove from them all wrong
notions, superstitions, traditional false beliefs and illusions
and hallucinations. A young man Tamhankar by name suf-
fered from itches all over his hody; he happened to read
about a medicine written by Bhausahib Maharaja. He did
exactly as was written and became free from the disease.
Ranade said to someone, “As Maharaja was not a physician,
the well-being cannot be attributed to the medicine, but
that it should be attributed to the power which Maharaja
had.”   When a reputed astrologer inquired of Ranade if
he had his horoscope with him, Ranade asked with a sort of
irony in his speech after the departure of the astrologer,
if the Atman too has a horoscope of his birth. When a learn-
ed Professor from         Ahmedabad drank a_       glass of water
through his nostrils, Ranade asked why is then the mouth
given   by    God?   When some       doctors    from Bijapur and
Sholapur recommended him to use Enema to remove his
chronic   constipation,    he   asked if it was not    unnatural?
To    principal 8.   प,   Dandekar    who   told   him   that the
Abhanga      which   goes in the name of       St. Tukarama, viz.
that which mentions the existence of a golden Pippal tree
in front of the house of St. Jnanadeva, is an interpolation,
Ranade sternly told the principal not to say so, inasmuch
as that was a fact of experience. To a question again from
Principal Dandekar, as to how many hours of sleep did
Ranade enjoy every night he replied with the counter
question if the night was meant for sleeping and not for
meditation? To a devout student of his who was spending
six to seven hours daily in reading ‘Saptasati’ for the
purpose of improving his financial condition, Ranade sent
a word asking him “to stop it immediately” saying further
that “this (meaning meditation on the Name of God im-
parted by the Guru) includes that (the upasana of the
goddess Durga ).” The student did as he was advised and
soon came to know through a vision that the power of the
goddess was nothing before that of the Guru and his finan-
cial condition also improved.
                              The Summit at Nimbal                           141

     _  Tt cannot however be said that because Professor
    ‘Ranade ‘wished the’ spiritual Good of all who came to him,
    € had the same attitude of mind 10 them all. He never
    confused the worldly and the spiritual points of view. For,
    as his Guru Bhausahib of Umadi used always to make the
    difference between the two, Ranade too, following in the
    footsteps of his Master, used to differentiate between man
    and man from the practical point of view. Spiritually con-
    sidered, he held in greater respect even a barber and a cook
    than a prince and a Professor. Sometimes, both in Nimbal
    and Allahabad, he was seen spending even a couple of
    hours with the, barber, because the latter was singing a
    few devotional songs,           whereas after having talked with a
    ‘university Professor or with a high official in the Govern-
    ment for a few minutes he would immediately retire to
    his room for meditation.            But so far as the seats in a meet-
    ing or at the dining table were concerned,               he would allot
    them first to the officials, then to the learned persons and
    ‘then to those who were dear to him from the spiritual point
    of view. For he knew very well that what counts as im-
    portant in the practical everyday life is the political power
_   first and then learning and last of all the devotion to God.

           It was no doubt unfortunate
                                   that many of the so-called
     advanced and elderly sadhakas did not understand the
    secret of this behaviour. A learned Professor went to the
    extent      of   saying      that    Ranade    too     was    a    man     of
     the    world       and    so    they - became       prone    to    believe
    ‘that     spatial    and     physical     contiguity    was       the    sign
     of being very closely attached to him. When, for instance,
    the letters of Sri Bhausahib Maharaja were to be read and
    ‘discussed in an afternoon meeting in Nimbal,                I do remem-
     ber that on three occasions at least Ranade told me to sit
     in a place which was just opposite to him with the remark
     ‘that appropriate things should be done at appropriate
    ‘times.    But strange to say, the very next day, I found that
     the seat allotted to me was already occupied by someone
     ‘else who was fond of hovering round: the Professor. I was
142    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

too shy to request the usurper to give me my seat, and
Professor Ranade too somehow tolerated the injustice in
order not to disturb the devotional atmosphere. A learned
Professor allowed himself to believe that willy nilly Ranade
did to some extent come under the influence of men who
were politically great or great on account of their wealth,
reputation or social status. But nothing can be more ab-
surd than to think in this manner. The learned Professor
mistook   courtesy for flattery, probably he was looking to
his own projected self.    Indomitable was the spirit of in-
dependence born out of utter desirelessness of Professor
Ranade.   Had there been the least desire of fame           or re-
putation in Ranade’s mind he would not have severed his
connection with the Theosophical Society, nor declined the
offer of the Lokamanya B. G. Tilak to take the entire con-
trol of the Kesari and the Maratha Newspapers in Poona,
nor refused to have the credit of starting the Gujarat Uni-
versity and of becoming its First Vice-Chancellor, nor re-
fused to accept the visiting Professorship of Oxford, nor
had remained absent at the Fergusson College to witness
his own   oilpainted   portrait   unveiled   by   Dr. S.   Radha-
krisnan, the then President of India. Let it be remembered
once for all that his only desire throughout his
most troubled life was to become united with God.
Indeed, not to understand that the attitude towards
anything is the measure of the worth of that thing,
is the real cause of apathy not only to that thing but also
to everything else that flows from that thing. If the attitu-
de of devotion to God is the Chief and the central thing in
life, then to have it and to preserve its value everything
else must be subordinated and surrendered to it. In the ab-
sence of this attitude to God, it is natural that one should
be unable to know as to which thing should be subordinat-
ed to which other. Ranade was firm in his belief that who-
ever comes to Nimbal comes at least with a provisional
faith in God, and that therefore it was Ranade’s prime duty
to look to his comfort and well-being so that the visitor’s
faith would increase and he would begin to tread on the
                    The Summit at Nimbal                          148

path of devotion. Not understanding this basic principle
of devotional life, some of the so-called stalwarts in the
spiritual life thought of modelling the pattern of life in the
Nimbal Ashram on the pattern obtaining somewhere else.
Ranade gave them a long rope to finalise the scheme and
then to submit it before him for sanction. For two full
years those men of wealth and high status in society work-
ed very hard and chalked out a scheme in all its details
regarding what should be the daily cost of lodging and
boarding and of all other facilities of conveyance, reading
room, bath, toilet etc. I was the only person who opposed
the scheme totallv. but I was from their view-point a non-
entity and at the best a supporter of the poor in society
who, as a   stalwart among the great sadhakas used to
express with an   abuse on his longue, came to Nimbal
only to dine. I being the usual visitor from Belgaum with
my wife and children had to eat the humble pie. At last
came before Ranade the framers of the scheme.                Ranade
gave a patient hearing to all that they said in praise of the
scheme.   And then Ranade said to them all “Let me ask
you only one question, and if I will be satisfied by your
reply, I shall immediately put my signature signifying my
‘approval of it.    Please let me know whether from today
no poor man        can    have   ‘Paramartha’    at   Nimbal.”   That
torpedoed the scheme no doubt; but I wondered if that
opened the eyes of the supporters of the scheme. Perhaps
not. For had it been otherwise 8 poor rikshawala from
Poona, Joshi by name, would have also been invited to
take his meals at the Dhyana Mandir. None was kind
enough to do 80.         I too was helpless for on account of my
many restrictions on my           diet I used to have      from the
kitchen only one or two pieces of bread.              However Pro-
fessor Ranade was looking to all that was going on from
a window of his house which was as equidistant from the
Dhyana Mandir as the old house where myself and Joshi
 were   standing.        Forthwith   Professor   Ranade    went    to
Dhyana Mandir and told the devotees that they had for-
144.      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


gotten to call Mr. Joshi for the dinner. Immediately ran
a number of advanced sadhakas to call Mr. Joshi. What I
think about the whole of the affair is that while to Pro-
fessor Ranade devotion to God (including. devotion of
others) was all in all, to others, the dinner was more an
item in a picnic get-together than a common meal in the
presence of God or the Guru.        -

    I wish that this vital point of attitude (known as
‘Bhava’) should thoroughly be ,understood in its full con-
notation.    There   was a   man,   ‘Shettyappa’   by   name who
took care of the horse of Sri Bhausahib Maharaja. It was
in 1924 that while I was in Nimbal he also came and stayed
in the old house of Professor Ranade for a few days. Day
and night he was doing nothing else but meditation. Ranade
had severed his connection with D. E. Society and his fin-
ancial condition was straightened.        His steno, Mr. Shankar-
rao Dharmadhikari was living with his family in the ad-
joining two rooms of the same house.           With Ranade there
was his cousin sister, Yamunakka only; with all affection
and devotion she was serving            Professor Ranade day and
night in all possible ways. But she was a little blunt and
unduly outspoken.       Often in the hearing of         Professor
Ranade, she used to complain about Shettyappa that he was
only a parasite and unnecessarily hanging upon Rambhau;
he did not do any work but took his full meals and sat or
slept. Ranade did not say anything to his sister for some
time; but when he       overheard the complaint         again and
again, he said to his sister, “Do you know that he is busy
in accomplishing the highest Good which might be possible
for man. He has served my Guru by taking every care of his
horse, It is by the grace of the Guru that in spite of his be-
ing totally illiterate, he reads and understands the Dasa-
bodha. Please do not abuse him, but heed that if I get even
a small piece of bread to eat, I shall first give half of it to
him.” I think no comment is necessary to bring out the im-
plication of what Ranade said to his sister. Verily the attitu-
de of love and regard for Guru and to God must be express-
                     The Summit at Nimbal                     145

ed in this manner only. In vain are devotion and knowledge
if one fails to express them in such activity as would show
that he is the servant of all those who are devoted to their
Guru and to God.

     - In this context it would not be out of place to mention
the salient features of his behaviour.

1.    Ranade’s nature of Forgiveness:

    Professor Ranade’s quality of forgiveness was remark-
able. To err is human, but for Professor Ranade to for-
give was divine. Forgiveness combines both.the qualities
of courage and patience, though patience is the expression
of courage.    A two-fold motive seems to us, however, in-
volved in the patience shown by Professor Ranade.             One
was to increase the capacity to bear quietly the insults of
others for the sake of truth and God. The other was to give
an opportunity to others to repent and come on the path of
God.     People were slow to understand this.        Once a uni-
versity Professor wrote to him that          unless he gets some
spiritual experience he would advertise         that what Ranade
professes is unreal. Ranade thought of writing to him but
later on said that his reply would not be in the interest of
that Professor and so he dropped the idea of writing to him.
 To a poor Brahmin from Belgaum who could haxfdly support
_ himself and his family, Ranade was first hesitating whether
 to impart the Name or not. For this, he gave the explana-
 tion that to initiate a person on the path of spirit is not a
 joke, for before doing so the Guru has to take upon him-
 self the burden of all the sins, misery and poverty of the
 person who comes to him for being initiated. Notwithstand-
 ing -this,   he   initiated   the   Brahmin.   Immediately   the
 Brahmin’s condition improved during the four months of
 the rainy season when he was called 98. 8 priest for various
 religious purposes.     Ranade expressed’ it once that in his
 capacity of Guru he had to bear patiently the sins, miseries
 and -the diseases of others.        People were impatient but he
 was a tower of patience. Like Jesus, he too atoned for the
146    = Prof. R. 0. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

sins of others, suffered for others and also lived for the
sake of others. On one occasion he allowed a close relative
of his to die in peace; on another occasion when his own
son died he said, ‘God has given and God has taken him
away’; on a third occasion when his second son died, he
said, I am not without any credit with God, but let God’s
Will be done,’ and we may even cite as the fourth occasion
when he was quite fit to initiate others, he waited for 16
years till the death of his spiritual brother, Sri Amburac
Maharaja. All these illustrations show that Ranade could
face all the dangers and trials in his life with courage
and patience and would at the           same time forgive the
faults and blemishes of others.       Like Nimbargi Maharaja
he used to think that the growth of spiritual strength de-
pends on the capacity to bear with patience all that comes
as a challenge to life.

2.    Never Divulged the secrets of others:
      One   of   the   prominent   characteristics   of   Professor
Ranade was that he never divulged the secrets of other per-
sons. If was not required to tell him that a particular thing
is to be sealed as secret. Whatever was communicated to
him was as a sealed deposit in his heart. Naturally, people
used to cotifide in him without any reservation anything
whether it was good or bad.

      Another characteristic of Professor Ranade was that he
had a uhiqtie way of speaking which was clear and harm-
less, 011 जाह occasion he said to one Mr. D. D. Kale, one of
his students, as if by way of compliment that he had the vir-
tue of remaining content with whatever he had got, and as a
jest he said to him, ‘Just look at Vinayak, he is very ambi-
tious.’ After some time Kale said to his frietid, ‘Have you
observed the way in which he has told me that I have no
ambition? Instead of being cross with him I have enjoyed
the very soft way in which he had told me that I have   10
ambition.’ On another occasion, when he had come out if
the court-yard of his house to give a send off to 4 friend
                    The Summit at Nimbal                        147


of his who happened to be a High Court Judge, I became
a little jealous and was thinking about the costly car of
the judge when suddenly Professor Ranade turned to-
wards me and putting his hand upon my back, he said ‘that
you too could have become a judge and could ‘have had a
car like that, but what of that? You would have been ab-
sorbed in wine and women.’ I could immediately see the
implied meaning of his observation that where there are
wealth and prosperity one is likely to invite some attendant
evil also, and where there are poverty and contentment,
one may be accomplished with the virtuous life.

3. The Unflinching faith and devotion of
   Professor Ranade

   Any one of the innumerable incidents in the life of
Professor    Ranade   would have shattered        the   faith   and
devotion of a man of the world, however great he may be.

    (1)     It is a matter of common experience that one
may look gloomy and sorrowful, if he loses his job. Ranade
did not mind resigning his post of a Professor at the Fer-
gusson    College   and   severing   his   connection   with    the
Deccan Education Society in June, 1924.         For three and a
half years, he was without any job, but his devotion and
faith in God increased abundantly. There came many 7107४
guests at his house but he remained undaunted, and he
used to work ceaselessly in reading and writing.

    (2)     His mother and wife died, but he remained stead-
fast, saying to God, “You and I shall now live together.”

    (3)     He came to know that his dear son, Venkatesh,
died at Kolhapur, when he was at the Miraj Railway Junc-
tion and was proceeding to Jamkhandi to preside over the
gathering at the High School there.          Instead of going to
Kolhapur, he went to Jamkhandi and gave the presiding
lecture without a tinge of sorrow on his face,

    (4)     It appeared to others,    that   he was     dependent
upon several persons for one thing or the other.           But it
148    = Prof. के, D..Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


was not so.     He was dependent on God only. He said to his
sister one day in Allahabad, who had served him for a num-
ber of years, and to a disciple of his, who was also serving
him in his own humble way, that they might go away from
him, if they were tired, for God was with him.


   .   (5)   When he lost his second son in Allahabad, he said,
“God had given him and God has taken him away.”


       (6)   Devotion   to   God, being the only all-absorbing
occupation of his life, he purchased a car solely for the
purpose of going to the bank of Yamuna or to some solitary
place, in order to meditate there.       It was only with this
object in view, as also with the view of avoiding unneces-
sary social visits that he used to go évery everting to Bargarh
Railway Station at a distance of about 50 miles frori Allaha-
bad,   spend the night there and come in the morning at
about 10.


   , (7), That there was nothing higher than devotion 10
God to Professor Ranade can be seen by his total uncon
cern with anyone of the dignitaries of the world as well as
from his internal affection for those who loved God.      It is
not without his undaunted courage that he included in 11:8
great book “Pathway to God in Kannada Literature”, a
song from his cook Bhagavantappa. It was not tincommon
for him that he spent even an hour with his barber who
used to sing a devotional song or two while shaving his
beard. When on one occasion his close attendant, Mr.
Jagannath Lele, told him immediately after he came out of
the bathroom that a certain prominent man had come to
meet him, Ranade said to him in anguish “Why don’t you
cut my throat instead of telling me that someone has come
to meet me, when you know that I have no other business
now but to meditate.” To a superintendent of R.M.S. who
repeatedly told Ranade that he was rather not keen to
attend his office when he was meditating on God, Ranade
said with all emphasis, “Don’t go to the office if that be
                  The Summit at Nimbal                   149


the condition of your mind, for you will then be showing
your allegiance to the highest God.”

    (8)    It was in 1922 that he had gone over to Mysore
as a guest of His Highness, the Maharaja of Mysore, where
there was gold and silver everywhere. Next morning he
said to Jagannath quoting a verse from       Ramadasa, “If
there is no devotion to Rama shown in this palace, then I
have nothing to do here; let us go back to Nimbal,” and
immediately he did as he said.

    (9)    Once he burst into tears at the thought that the
innumerable souls in the world were wasting their lives
without caring to know the nature of God.      ‘What would
happen to these poor creatures,’ he said with a sorrowful
note and burst into tears again and again.   His passion for
love of God was not for himself alone; he wished that
others also should be devoted to God.

    (10)    What shall we gain by looking outside at the
nature if like Descartes we go on doubting or indulge in a
poetic fancy like Wordsworth?       What shall we   gain by
looking inside if we are to find a void? What shall we gain
by a logical equivalence between the outside nature and the
internal ego of man, if we do not look upwards and sub-
sume both the nature and the soul as non-different from

God?

    (11)    If you wish to increase your faith and devotion
to God, go and meditate on a funeral ground; then you
will come to know the hollowness of the samsara and the
fullness of God everywhere.     Ranade first acted and then
advised afterwards.   It would be only on the background
of God as the only Reality, that you will find everything
else as unreal, illusory, or short-lived.

  (12)     Who else, who has not become one with God, on
account of his faith and devotion, can go outside his house

and devote two hours in silent meditation on the last
day of his life on this earth, and initiate an aspiring soul
150      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

into spiritual life? Who else except Professor Ranade could
foretell his death to his wife and yet do all the routine acti-
vities, as if death is far away, and make provision for
all recurring expenses of the day to day requirements as
also for his own funeral expenses and clear off the debts,
if any? Who else could say that the     T.B. germs in his
blood were very fortunate, inasmuch as they were destined
to be saved along with him? And who else can guide all
those, who believe in him, even after his death, if he had
lived only a biological life?

       (13)   Spinoza is considered as a God-intoxicated philo-
sopher. No doubt, his definition of God as the only stb-
stance is excellent, He says that God or the Substance is in
itself and can be conceived by itself.     But if this is true.
no human being can know God or can become God, and
then in that case ‘God-intoxication’ will lose all its meari-
ing.   Look, however to the concept of God-intoxication as
Professor Ranade understood it.      This will be clear by his
prayer to his Guru, Sri Bhausahib Maharaja of Umadi. He
says that his eyes should ever be filled by the vision of
God, that his body should vibrate with the meditation on
the Name of God, that his intellect should remain steadfast
in the contemplation of God, and that all his physical and
mental activities should be turned towards the God head
only and not aim at finding pleasures and happiness in
the sensible world. God-realization to Professor Ranade
was the whole of life as ceritred in God, and as circum-
scribed by God alone. To him, there was none and nothing
higher than God or higher than his Guru who had realized
God. Naturally, God’s abode was to him his Guru’s abode;
Pandharpur was available to him at Inchgiri. Ranade
would bécome at times so absorbed in the meditation arid
contemplation of God, or so absorbed in telling and listeri-
ing to the incidents in the life of his Guru and co-devotees,
that he very often did not know whether it was day or
hight. All this constituted for him what is known as God-
intoxication which yielded for him the greatest joy.
                    The Summit at Nimbal -                     151


     (14) That Professor Ranade wept without being
noticed by anyone else and by night for months and
months on account of the death of his Guru is an indication
of his deep and unwavering love for the spiritual life
which his Guru exemplified fully.       He asked Jagannath
Lele to get the well at Nimbal dug after seeing in his
dream at Allahabad eighteen years after the death of his
Guru, Bhausahib Maharaja that he was digging the well,
shows his indomitable faith in his Guru as representing the
divine power.

    (15)    Ordinarily, it is almost impossible that a rejected
candidate should be appointed as the Head of the univer-
sity department as the best candidate by the same mem-
bers of the committee; but this has happened.            At this,
Professor Ranade only remarked,        ‘Nothing is impossible
for God; He can do      and   undo   things   as He   likes”    A
miracle though, it is the result of his faith in his Guru as
well as his devotion to God.

    (16)    His faith and devotion were so absorbing that
they resulted in complete surrender to God.       As a -result
of this, Ranade did not do anything which was not sanc-
tioned by    God.   Once he was to    go to   Sangali,    and as
usual everybody expected him to be very busy in prepar-
ing for his journey at least half an hour before the depar-
ture of the train. But he was calm and quiet; while every-
one else who was to go with him was ready with bag and
baggage. Someone asked him as to why there was no pre-
paration and hurry about his going.     Ranade told him that
he has not got the sanction of God and perhaps we may
not go to Sangali at all.   Within a few minutes, there came
a telegram from the-Rajasahib of Sangali stating that for
some urgent work he was leaving Sangali and so, the Pro-
fessor should postpone his visit.

    On another occasion when he was to come to Bom-
bay, his attendant did not find the carriage of the train at
Allahabad in which Professor Ranade’s berth was reserv-
152     Prof. की, 2. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

ed.  The attendant moved up and down and saw the whole
train, but again came and told the Professor that he could
not find anywhere the berth which he was to occupy. ‘That
cannot be,’ replied Professor Ranade, ‘you look carefully
in the third bogie from the engine, and there I have got
my seat.     This frain is nothing but the copy of the ideal
train in which I am seeing my berth.’ The attendant went
to the bogie as directed and returned with the information
that his seat was there.


      On another occasion still, when a student of his was
to go from Belgaum to Jodhpur, he sent two trusted friends
of his to Miraj junction saying to them, ‘Give this Angara
(holy ashes) to him, he needs protection.’       This will also
explain how sugar which was blackish in colour was ap-
proved by him as      very   nice   in   preference   to   another
variety which was very good from the point of view of an
ordinary man.     The blackish variety was nice because he
saw the vision of God on it, whereas on the other variety
he did not see the vision. The approval of God was essen-
tial for every    act of his.   Every article   of food,    dress,
etc., he would keep before the photograph of his Guru and
then use it. It was not simply a matter of ritual but of
complete surrender, which to him did not follow his acti-
vity but it either always preceded it or was sirmnultane-
ous with it.


      (17)   That his devotion and surrender were divine
virtues, co-extensive with his very life, can be seen from
his very anxious inquiry regarding any place in which
devotional bhajans and meditation were carried on regular-
ly. He used to ask the very names of the persons who
assembled in those places and used to become very glad by
knowing that new and new centres of devotion were grow-
ing in number and were attracting new and new aspirants
for spiritual life. This means, in other words, that his
zest for devotional meditation and surrender was riot merely
confiried to himself but went beyond him to other souls
                  The Summit at Nimbal                        153


near or far away. Devotion and surrender must always be
on the increase not simply in the life of an individual,
but in the social life of humanity at large. That was the
ideal before him. In short, Bombay, Ahmedabad and
Jodhpur were as near and important to him as Athani,
Bijapur and Sholapur.

     Section C: GOD THE BEGINNING AND THE

               END OF HIS PHILOSOPHY

     Professor R. D. Ranade was not a mere speculative
philosopher, though he did make use of speculation as an
handmaid to his intuitive knowledge of God. He construct-
ed a philosophy which was both the expression and justi-
fication of his mystical realization, though it may be that
on this very account, a casual reader of his writings might
think that the Professor’s thought was the father to what
he   became   afterwards,   namely,   a   God-realizer   of   the
highest magnitude. If we wish to understand him thorough-
ly, we must    reverse the position and say that his having
realized God was the father of his entire philosophy of
spiritual knowledge.   Like Descartes, he did not look first
into himself, and that too with a certain amount of doubt
and distrust; nor did he first look outside him like Words-
worth and other Nature poets or scientists like Aristotle
or Newton in order to       become aware of a transcendent
Deity who alone was responsible for all the creation, in-
cluding, Man and Nature. Ranade looked, or was rather
advised to look, upwards first, so that on finding God not
only above the clouds but also as being present exactly
as before, within and without himself. he came to the con-
clusion that his own being as well as the entire Nature
outside was nothing but God alone,        His pantheism, there-
fore, with stress on theos, was not then the result of his
own making, but was rather forced upon him as the result of
his life-long, silent and devout meditation on the Name of
God, in accordance with the instruction of his spiritual
teacher. God thus became to him the central problem in
154      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


metaphysics, which when solved helped him to solve the
other two problems of matter and Mind.


      Though, ultimately, God can be said to be the begin-
ning and the end of his philosophy, he did not make any
attempt like Spinoza to start with a definition of God or
substance, so that the definitions of all other entities would
follow from it, as a corollary follows from a theorem. Nor
did he make like Locke any empirical inventory of all
the ideas which the human mind can think of and then
said that the idea of God was the chiefest among them.
Nor did he think like Kant to examine the telescope of
mind and its power to visualize the stars in the heaven,
so that he would be able to declare that so much of the
creation of God was knowable to the human mind.             He
wanted to make an experiment regarding all the knowable
and the    unknowable aspects of Reality, without taking
anything for    granted   except a   provisional faith   in the
words of his Guru. Nothing was written on the ‘tabula
rasa’ of his mind, by way of presupposition or prejudice.
There was neither any pet theory of his own to be sup-
ported and substantiated, nor any      hypothesis of his own
which he wished to test. What he wished to verify in his
own experience was the truth of the various statements
of his spiritual teacher and of his spiritual brothers as well
as of the works which he had read in the several literatures
of the country and of the outside world. Can God be per-
ceived? Can his power and glory and other attributes be
achieved by the finite soul? Can man be like God or non-
different or identical with Him to some extent at least?
These and such others were the problems he wished to
solve for himself before evolving a philosophy of religion
in order to carry his own conviction to others.

      To achieve this, he invented a peculiar method of his
own. Perhaps, it might be said to be an improvement of the
method used by certain writers of the history of philosophy
in the West, notable among whom are A. K. Rogers and
                  The Summit at Nimbal                          155


Mary Calkins. But what these authors have intended to do
is to make their philosophers tell their philosophy directly
to the readers, instead of telling about them in an indirect
manner. Professor Ranade also has made the sages and
the saints tell their convictions about truth and reality
directly to the readers; but it seems that he does not allow
the latter to form any opinion as they like except the one
of which he himself was convinced as the result of his doing
long, long toil in the field. He wishes to communicate to his
readers the entire account of what he had read and of what
he had learnt from his Masters and of what he had himself
experienced. His was a twofold task of making a ‘survey’ of
all that he had read, heard or seen, as an astute historian
would do, as well as of making a new ‘Construction’ out of
it, as the very title of his most famous work ‘Constructive
Survey of Upanisadic Philosophy’, clearly suggests. He has
used the device, no doubt, of hiding himself behind the sages
and the saints, in order that it should appear that what-
ever he has written was         already there as the precious
treasure of all those who had gone before him, and that he
himself was only a mouthpiece of them.             This is no doubt
excellent, so far as the annihilation of egoism and the ex-
pression of humility and surrender to God are concerned.
An intelligent reader of Ranade’s works will however dis-
cover that there is something more than this. He will read
between the lines and find out the presence of Ranade there.
This is just like sensing the smell of a flower, but being
aware at the same time that the smell resides in the flower
and not outside it.    Judging from this point of view, it will
not be wrong to say that all the considered opinions and
quotations   of all   the   sages   and   saints   which   Professor
Ranade has arranged in his works are not primarily intend-
ed to display the wisdom of them all, but to support his
own conclusions       about spiritual life to which he arrived
through meditation and contemplation on the Name and
the visions of   God, respectively,
156    = Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


    Real love would only begin with the sight of God.
Before that, generally speaking, there will only be mechani-
cal repetition of the name of God. But the name also can
be uttered with a sort of love even when there is no vision
of God.    In that case this preliminary love would only
mean the remembering of God’s name mentally, silently,
intensely and for considerable length of time. This includes
also a faith in the efficacy of the name as it is given by the
Guru.

      The credit of loving God after one has the vision of
Him does not go much to the devotee as to God for, it is
the beauty of God that attracts the devotee and compels
him to be attached to God.

    Professor Ranade’s doctrine of the Asymptotic Ap-
proach to God or what can also be called as the theory
of Approximation to God-head is not so much a via-media
between two schools of thought which hold respectively
that none can know God, and that God can be known in
his entirety, as a synthesis of the thesis that God can be
known entirely and its anti-thesis that God cannot be so
known by any human being.

    The question whether one sees the face of God first or
sees His feet first should be solved by reference to the
experience of great saints only. Those who recommend
manasa Puja advise us to visualize mentally the feet first,
and the face and the head afterwards. The reverse how-
ever is the position in mystical experience. There it is
said that one sees the face first and feet afterwards.

    That the feet can be touched is a further point in the
experience of a saint. They are not merely seen like the
face in a mirror but are also touched by him when he
places his head upon them. Now the visualization of the
feet may occur in the top-most portion of the brain or even
on the plain ground and it is just possible that one can see
and touch them simultaneously in either of the two places.
                   The Summit at Nimbal                      157


That the face is seen in both the cases is taken for granted
so that the divine Person is visualized from head to feet
and a fraction of that person, namely the feet, is touched as
Saint Kabira has said .


 Secrion 0: VARYING STANDARDS OF MEASURING
         THE GREATNESS OF PROF. R. D. RANADE

   We shall begin with the most superficial standard
employed by some of the fanatics and then rise higher
and higher to the most impersonal valuation of the saint
in him. Making a show that they intend to describe the
saint of Nimbal, they have only shown their own greatness
and the greatness of the land they occupy. According to this
view Ranade is great not because of his own merit but on
account of   the   land in   which   he was   born,   that is in
Jamkhandi, which is a place in Karnataka. But this is an
anachronism.   When Ranade was born, Jamkhandi was in
Maharashtra in the district of Belgaum and not in Kar-
nataka in the district of Bijapur. A true student of history
would like to say that Ranade was born as Maharashtriya
though he had done a great service to Karnataka. And sup-
posing that he was born in Karnataka,         were not there
other beings or creatures born on the same day on which
Ranade was born? Even a cow might have given birth to
a calf, but what of that as Shakespeare says.         The great-
ness ought not be judged by birth or by the land in which
a person is born but it should be judged by the achievement
that he has made in his life.The great generous hero of
the Mahabharata, Karna, says about himself that his birth
was due to his fate, but that his valour was his own.      Simi-
larly,   Jamkhandi might be great because Ranade was great
and not that Ranade is great because of Jamkhandi, much
less because of Karnataka.     Where were these fanatics of
Karnataka when Professor Ranade was in great financial
difficulties due to his breaking away from the D. E. Society
for nearly three years and half, from June 1924 to December
158      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


19272    Ranade     achieved    his   greatness   at    Nimbal   and
Allahabad, and manifested it throughout India and abroad
by his lectures, books and through his ardent and sincere
moral and spiritual disciples.

      There   are   others   who   rise   above   the   stupid   per-
sons mentioned above.        They take note only of his scholar-
ship and intellectual acumen.         His securing of the Jagan-
nath Sankarseth Scholarship at the Matriculation Exami-
nation   and other laurels at other examinations has very
little to do with his real greatness. Had he taken the same
impression of greatness       which   other   people    have   taken,
then he would not have melted the gold medal which he
got? His intellectual greatness was only a glitter and spark
of the inherent greatness of his spiritual being. Alas, how
can the ordinary people understand this?           Then there are
others who take note of his being a very learned Professor
in the Deccan Education Society and a Vice-chancellor of
Allahabad University.        But this also is a low estimate of
Professor Ranade. For, there have been professors and
professors, and vice-chancellors and vice-chancellors and
what value they have at present, every body knows, Even
an Assistant Junior lecturer in a university is now-a-days
called as a Professor. A person who is drawn from among
the teachers of a higher secondary school and placed in
the chair of a professor in the university is called a Profes-
sor. Professor Ranade used to say that he had only seen
two professors during his life time. One was Principal Bain
of the Deccan College, Poona, and the other Rudolf Otto,
the author of the famous Book, The Idea of the Holy, and
all others were called as Professors ottly by sufferance. A
Professor, according to his view, ought to be head and
shoulders above all, and must have contributed largely to
the existing stock of knowledge. Accordingly, A. E. Taylor,
was a professor even though he was a mere lecturer in the
university of Edinburgh. Quiller Coach was a professor,
though he used to engage his classes for six hours in a
month. The late Vice-chancellor, Dr. Ganganatha Jha of
                   The Summit at Nimbal                    159

the University of Allahabad, appointed Dr. Ranade as Pro-
fessor of Philosophy on reading his monumental work,
Constructive Survey of Upanisadic Philosophy, though
Professor Ranade was out of employment nearly for three
and half years, after he had severed his connection with
the Deccan Education Society. Professor Ranade accepted
ihe appointment on the condition that he was free to go to
Nimbal and come to Allahabad at any time. Dr. Jha wrote
to him that his name on the rolls of the university teachers
was an honour to the university. The real professor stands
by himself whether he is in or      outside   any university.
Dr. Ranade was such a professor.




   There are others who have risen above the petty intel-
lectual standard of measuring the greatness of a man.
They say that Ranade is great because he has no presupposi-
tions of his own or any prejudices against anybody.      This
he could do because he felt the necessity of bringing the
knowledge of the philosophy as close as possible to the
spiritual knowledge of his Guru, Bhausahib Maharaja. As
a proof of what they thought, they give the evidence that
Professor Ranade ‘was    equally   conversant   with   various
languages, and that whatever was best there in any part of
the world he made use of it in order to justify what his .
spiritual teacher Bhausahib taught about God. This typical
measurement of Ranade’s greatness in terms of his attempt
to bring about the marriage between intellectual conviction
and spiritual knowledge can be given by some of his students
who had learnt at his feet either in the Fergusson College,
Poona or Willingdon College, Sangli, his book on Thomas
Carlyle, his monograms on Eleatic philosophy and Heraclei-
tus as well as his various articles in various magazines such
as the one which was started with the help of his friend,
Professor Widgery of Baroda, and the college magazines,
160        Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

      Section A: RANADE’S SEARCH FOR REALITY

      Ranade’s intellect was as penetrating as was his dis-
cerning     eye,   and his judgement as          unerring     and precise
as that of a veteran surgeon,             His method of study was
unique.      He used to collect a number of books on a              parti-
cular      topic which he wished         to study.      He would then
sit   in   an   arm-chair   and read      the    introduction     and   the
index, and would then refer to the relevant portions con-
nected      with   the   topic   irrespective of     the   fact   whether
they agreed or went against his opinion.                He was always
willing to accept a good point from anywhere and reject
the defective point as well.          He would immediately call
his steno and dictate to him what he felt about all such
points     and make his constructive            commefits upon them.
He would not move from his chair till he had finished all
the books which he had collected for the purpose.

      But between what he discerned and what he judged
he used to give          ample    room   for     deep   and    prolonged
thought, so that none should accuse him of being a hasty
judge. He put his finger exactly on the central topic of
inquiry and would soon form his own opinion first in a
general Way; but he would wait and carefully see what
other men had to say about the topic under inquiry, weigh
the relative value of each one of the opinions sympathe-
tically but critically, turn the topic this way and that
look to its several aspects, modify and eulogize the ver-
dicts of other stalwarts in the field without any hesitation,
and straightway put forth his own opinion for people to
appreciate or criticise it.       His method of study was thus
both analytic and synthetic. The motive of analysing the
material was not to destroy but to make a careful survey,
just as the motive of synthesizing was mot to create 8
patch-work but to produce altogether 4 new and lasting
piece of spiritual] literature. In short, it was a ‘cdrtistruc-
tive survey’ of all that had gone before him, and the
construction of a unique something which was the prero-
gative of Professor Ranade and of norte else.
                       The Summit at Nimbal                            161

    Keeping this in mind let us also ourselves make the
survey of all the philosophical material in the East and
the West as Ranade has done it, and come to understand
what the nature of Reality is according to him, Let us
first consider the key-points in Western philosophy, not
so much in comparison with their counter-parts in the
Eastern philosophy, but in respect of their value so far
as the mystica] philosophy of Professor Ranade is con-
cerned. Are Parmenides and Zeno correct in saying that
there is no motion, or is Heracleitus correct in saying
that perpetual change is the nature of the Reality ? No
purpose will be served by saying that the Upanishad also
Speaks of the Brahman as having motion and at the same
time as having no motion (Tat ejati tat na ejati). We have
to solve the problem whether motion
                                  is real or not, In
its own way, it is good no doubt to understand that the
identification    of    the   real   and    rational    as   stated    by
Parmenides and as worked out by Hegel corresponds to
that of the Sat and cit in the philosophy of Sankaracharya,
or to know that H. Bergson’s philosophy of Elan Vital is
the     revival of the    Heracleitian      change;    but   this is    no
solution of the problem whether change is real or not.


        Parmenides’     assertion    that    Being      is     changeless
appears as a dogma. The credit goes to Zeno who does
not take the truth of the dogma on trust, but explains it
by means of his World-famous riddles that the concept
of motion is an illusion.      ‘The flying arrow is at rest’; ‘The
swiftest hare can never overtake 2 (1070156 in a race, if
 the latter is given an advance at the start’ have been his
 challenging statements which have bewildered the intel-
 lect    of -the’ greatest    mathematicians          during    the    last
 twenty centuries,


        Tt is Ranade who has pointed out the hollowness of
 Zeno’s argument. Motion, says Ranade, is a spatio-temporal
 relation, and so can never be explained in terms of
                                                     space
 Only or in terms of time only. Since the days of Einstein’s
162     Prof. R, 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


principle   of Relativity, time is considered as
                                               the fourth
dimension of Space, and unless it is taken into considera-
tion, motion can never be explained. The time-old three
dimensions of length, breadth and thickness of space
would describe only a static wooden frame, or at best
be reduced to a single linear dimension, which for the
sake of convenience, might be called as-distance between
two points, This will not however explain the perform-
ance of a    fly which actually travels: from one point ‘to
the other, Even     so,   the measurement of   time   as past,
piesent and future will describe the period during which
the fly was resting at a particular place. The transition
from one point in Space to another will not be explained
by the mere flow of temporal sequence. Motion is the
graph described by an object between two distances or
co-ordinates issuing from some known point of origin. As

the two co-ordinates are space and time, motion of an
object becomes resultant of the two      forces acting upon
the object, When this would happen, then alone it can be
determined as to with what speed the hare and the tor-
toise make    their progress in   the race. Zeno shows his
skill of the sleight of hands by first mentioning ‘ime’
only with reference to the hare, and then by mentioning
‘space or distance’ with reference io the tortcise. He does
not mention space and time together then with reference
to the hare and the tortoise. The result is that in fact,
there is no comparison between the relative motions of
the two creatures, One absolute term is pitted against
another absolute term.                          a


      In the second place, Zeno commits the mistake of
equating two dis-similar things. The steps of the tortoise
describe a continuous series of points on the surface of
the ground while the agile feet of the hare avoid the
dull continuity and take jumps over the co-ordinate of
space. It is on account of the ignoring of this fundamental
difference between the units of the two kinds of motion,
that the iortoise appears to be always ahead cf the hare.
                  - The Summit at Nimbal                                     163

   Our main interest lies in knowing what Ranade has
to say regarding the question whether-motion or change
is real or not, and not so much in knowing how he has
refuted Zeno’s argument against motion. We wish                               to
know ‘his definite opinion        regarding the Upanishadic
statement, ‘The Brahman moves, the Brahman moves
not.” We wish to know whether like Sankaracharya he
looks upon change as an illusion or whether it is the
necessary Characteristic of Reality. Taking the clue from
St. Tulsidasa he appears to us as having reconciled the
apparent contradiction between change and no change.
Tulsidasa tells us to observe that which intervenes bet-
ween the place of the vision of the Real and the Perceiver.
As the vision of the real or God cannot be intercepted
by anything which is not real it can safely be assumed
from what-Tulsidasa tells us that what has intervened
in the middle is also the real. This, in other words as
Prof, Ranade appears to us as inclined to believe, means
that there is the continuity of the real at any point in its
procession    from   One place   to    another.          In      other    words
Ranade wishes to point out to us that the gap between
the vision of the Real and the Perceiver is                       filled in by
pervasive nature of the        Real.    It       is    this      pervasiveness
which speaks of the immanence of God in the perceptible
world.   At   no point,   therefore,        within       the      phenomenal
world can it be pointed out that the reality has undergone

a change, though apparently            there          may     occur      infinite
changes in the world. It is this substantive nature of the
real that solves incidentally another ethico-spiritual prob-
lem namely that there occurs actionlessness in action. The
real remains the same whether the saint is active or not,
whether he walks or sits silently. At any point in its

journey of crores of miles from the sun to the earth, the
ray of light has got       a   substantive            existence,      and    not
Simply an adjectival one.        Though it belongs to the Sun
 and   though   without    the   Sun        it    has       no    independent
 existence of its own, it acts         as    a representative of the
 Sun and points out the sun only. As with Plotirus, 80
{64     Prof. ह. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


with Ranade the theory of Emanation involves pervasive.
ness,   In ordinary parlance, we may call the ray of light
or the fragrance of a flower as having only an adjectival
existence Since the quality of light or that of the fragrance
depends upon the existence of the sun or the flower. But,
in reality, the ray means the sun, and the fragrance means
the specific flower, The         Nyaya-Vaisesika          categories    of
Dravya     (substance),   Guna      (quality),     Karma (action),
Samanya      (universal),      Visesa    (the     differential) and
Abhava (absence or negation) are intellectual devices to
make    us aware of the difference, for instance,                between
a quality and an action; but in reality, they are the modes
in which the substance behaves,           Devadatta         is kind or
not;
  he is sitting or walking and so on.                 Excepting the
substance,   all the   other     categories     are   a   part    in   the
Spinozistic sense of the procession of the King. It is not
that place only where the king is that it is called as a
procession; from the first drum-beater to the last drum-
beater, the entire march is known as the procession. Evert
so, the categories constitute the procession of the sub-
stance. What does not change is the substance; what
changes are the categories, And as to the God-intoxicated
Spinoza, so to Ranade, the only substance is what exists
in itself and which can be conceived by itself.

      Ranade did accept the philosophical division between
two kinds of reality which Plato, Aristotle, Bradley and
Sankarachatya recognized           as   being    inevitable.       Plato
made the distinction between the sensible world and the
world of ideas;     Aristotle,     between      matter and         form:
Bradley between appearance and Reality, and Shankara-
charya, between the Vyavaharika and the Paramarthika
reality. But Ranade did not stop at the speculative
philosophy of any one of them. His experience went
beyond speculation. The shadow or the copy of the sen-
sible world did not exist for him when he made the
transition from the idea to reality. Matter did not exist
when he was face to face with the immaculate Form of
                            The Suminit at Nimbal                               165

        God, --Appearance disappeared in the self-same Absolute;
        and the -place of the so-called’ practical worldly life was
        taken by life spiritual. Indeed, he spoke about the two
        worlds..and about-the ‘characteristics of them not only
        to those ,who were intellectually interested in philosophy
        but also to those who were seekers: of truth; but the
        motive is:altogether different.    To the former he spoke in
        the capacity of a teacher of -philosophy, to the latter he
        spoke as a co-traveller in the spiritual field and as a guide.
        The sensible world, of names and forms was not to him
        a mere reminder of the Ideal-world; it was necessary as
        a fact for all the. ethical and spiritual progress of man.
        lt is not enough simply to know that the sensible world
        isa fleeting show of evenis in           space   and    time;     it.    is
        necessary to Yise above it and see the           sun     of     Reality
        behind‘it.   Formless matter was to him, 28 to-Aristotle an
        abstraction only;     and like Aristotle he régarded Matter-
        less Form as Real.        Let there be appearances of dreams
        and of illusions,   of silver and serpent but they would be
        nowhete inthe wakeful condition or when the shell and
        the ‘rope are seen.    Even so, let there be the worldly life
        and the pleasures and the sorrows of life;             they will be
        nowhere when there would          afise ‘the bliss and peace of
        God-realization,    Hence; the two-world theory, whether of
        Plato,   Aristotle, Bradley or Sankara was only an intellec-
        tual half-way house in the so-journ of Ranade to the
        ultimate’ haven ‘of. Reality, ‘Hence, also intellect was to
        him “a useful: instrument in-holding communication with
        other intellectuals-and in building a philosophy and a
        psychology ‘of religion which however, in its turn, owed
        all its-worth to-his-own personal experience of God

          , One, might,be tempted to draw. a comparison between
        the flight. of the philosopher of Plato into-the world
                                                               of
        Reality in. order to contemplate upon the
                                                     Idea and his
        return     to the-world    -of sense m   order to   distribute the
_       benefit:of his contemplation among: the .morta
                                                       ls of the
    `   world,-and Ranade’s being absorbed in deep, medita
