166      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


and    his    coming    out   of it   in    order   to    raise humanity
to divinity, Thus, one might try to show that for Ranade
also, the two-world theory was a fact of experience.
Indeed, so far so good. But, it should not be forgotten
that his amphibious life-was only an appearance. He
lived always in one world, and that was the world of
Reality.      His bodily existence was in the world of sense
 o doubt;      but his spiritual existence was in the world of
the Spirit,    From one point of view, there being only one
experience of God and nothing else for Ranade, there was -
no    difference between God           being    immanent          and    God
being transcendent,        There was before hirh the same con-
stant vision of God, with his eyes shut or open.                        From
another      point of view, however, God            was for him both
immanent        and    transcendent,       When he       was   among the
people and busy in doing his duties, God was immanent
for him; but when he was deeply absorbed in the medita-
tion of the divine Name or in the contemplation of the
divine     forms   God was      transcendent        for    him,    that   is
beyond his body, mind, senses and all other belongings.
Once again, we see that the two words, immanence and
transcendence, which he used with reference to God were
relevant ‘for him in his capacity as a teacher of philosophy,
but in reality what he meant was that God is immano-
trancendent at the same time. If God were merely imma-
nent and not transcendent, it would perhaps mean that
He is a mental creation, or at the best an after-image of
an idol seen in the outside world; and if He were transcen-
dent and not immanent, it might mean that one is having
an illusion or at the best a hallucination. In both the
cases, there would be only delusion and no vision of God
Hence, the criterion of a real vision of God is that the
vision must be absolutely the same whether He is seen
internally: when the eyes are shut, or externally, when
the eyes are Wide open,         In other words, there would be
no distinction between God as ‘Antaryamin’ or God as
‘Bahiryamin’, though to see Him internally and externally
                     The Summit at Nimbal                                 167

as the same will give us the double assurance that we
have the Reality and not appearance.

   Considered thus, we should have no hesitation| in
saying that Ranade lived only the life in God, notwith-
standing     his dwelling in         the   midst      of   men.    This     is
analogous to the saying of the people that a river has two
banks: it may also be supported by the boatsman who
plies his boat several times everyday from one bank to
the other, Ask the stream of the river, or a fish living
in the stream and it will reply that it knows not the
banks at all; that it knows not that it is one, it only
knows that it has to meet the ocean and that it has simply
the joy of constantly pouring its waters into the ocean
and in forgetting its own tiny individuality of name and
form, The people in general and philosophers too, thought
that   Ranade     was     living     an    ordinary     common life        of
Vyavahara or Pravrtti (activities) along with them; others
who were spiritually-minded thought that he was leading
the life of Paramartha or Nivrtti (non-activity).                  Neither
of them knew that his was the life of action in actionless-
ness   and   of   actionlessness      in    action.     And   if   Ranade
himself could have been consulted on ihe point, he would
have said that he knew that he was only joyfully medita-
ting on the Name of            God    and     contemplating        on     His    ”
various beautiful Forms, but that he knew not                      whether
that constituted activity          or non-activity.        What    all    this
amounted to was that like the water of the river which
was giving nourishment to the trees and plants on both
the banks, Ranade’s meditation              and    contemplation         wére
responsible for     not   only     sustaining     his   seeming    activity
and inactivity, but also the moral and the spiritual lives
of others who came in contact with him.                    He knew that
both his works and rest were dependent on the will of
God, that the waters of‘ his spiritual life were running
very freely through all the pores of his worldly life, so
much so that instead of two parts of life, one Vyavaharik
and the other Paramarthik, he was constantly living one

                           é
168      Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

single   life divine.        Not Ranade,    but God was living in
and through him, at any time and in whatever he did.
As the Upanishad would say, sip the water in which salt
has been’ completely dissolved from the top, it will
taste salty; sip it from the middle, it will taste salty; and
sip it again from the bottom of the vessel, it will have no
other taste except being salty. Even so, the saint in Ranade
would like to say that for him there is the bliss of God in
the beginning, in the middle and at the end of any activity
or rest.’ `             शि


` ˆ ˆए]18५ however
                 is the nature of this bliss? Is it the
same
   as hurnan happiness which alternates with or is
sometimes Mixed with pain or Sorrow, or is identical with
God or different from Him? Is God, in other words, bliss
or blissful? Does man enjoy ihe bliss in his ecstatic
condition, or does he become identical with it and there-
fore identical with God?             Such are the various questions
which we must know as Ranade has solved them, and
not as he has made known to us as the solutions of others
in his various works,

      If anyone wishes to scale the Himalayan heights of

Bhagirathi, Jamuna and Alakananda take their rise not

 as the above-mentioned rivers gave up their solitary and

 What he meant by this was that only the fittest among

  to him -that he alone deserved            to receive the precious

  an aristocrat by birth and            learning;    Professor    Ranade
  became a servant of humanity in spite of his intellectual

of silently enjoying
                  the infinite bliss of meditation on

beautiful; even so, as'the sublimé rnay not remain sublime

mind tod Will soon gain its freedom to’fly to
                                              other objects
The’ beauty of* the       snow-capped         mountains ‘and of the

                                                ity of the
vast expanse of the flooded Ganga or of the Niagra Falls
 vill fade in    course       of   time.”   Even   Supposing    that     the
172    = Prof. R. 0. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


beauty and the sublimity are not phenomenal in charac-
ter, but are the attributes of the visions of God, the
sensibility Of the perceiver may not remain constant.
Besides, the object of the spiritual ‘experience which is
beautiful and sublime may remain perpetually as the
‘Other’ and so may not be assimilated with the perceiver
of that object.          There will be the cleavage between the
seer   and the seen, the relative            dependence of the seer
upon the seen or vice            versa.   One   might      say     that   the
consciousness       of    the perceiver is   lifted   up    by the sight

of the beautiful, just          as   a poet may say that a lotus
01088008
      0 the sight of the moon, or that there occurs a
tide in the sea on account of the influence of the moon.
This उंड not however the whole of the truth.                      The lotus
withers: there is an           ebb-tide in the    sea;      even     so   the
lifting up of the consciousness has              as its counter-part,
the creature-consciousness or the feeling              of    ‘dependence
which      arises   on account of the seeing of             the    sublime.
‘I am nothing but dust and ashes before thee, 0 Lord.’
said Abraham,            There is going on the        unceasing       decay
and the unceasing repair in the cells of an organism. It
is this double process which is constantly going on and
which is technically known as metabolism which alone is
true, and so neither anabolism which lifts up the conscious-
ness for the time being, nor katabolism which casts it down
are finally real. Naturally, therefore, neither the beauti-
ful nor the sublime have the capacity to bestow the
lasting joy and peace upon a devotee of God, though he
would: certainly be nearer God if               he be fortunate           to
experience that God is- both beautiful and sublime. As
beauty and sublimity aré the necessary inevitable aspecis
of God, or as anabolism and katabolism are the construc-
tive and the destructive elements of metabolism, even
 ० the joyfulness due to divine ‘presence and humility
before Him are’ at once the necessary qualifications of the
dévotee of 5598. The metabolism of the cells sustains an
organism, even so devotion to God sustains the realiza-
tion of God.’       On the one hand, it increases the joy and
                     The Summit at Nimbal                                  173


peace due to participation and assimilation of the soul
with God, and on the other it destroys the vanity and
arrogance of being the possessors / 61 all godless things
Devotion, in other words, is to Ranade as. to_Jnanadeva
the key to and the guarantee for acquiring the kingdom
of God


      We are certain that it would be absolutely uceless to
make an inventory of the spiritual experience of a saint
in particular or of all saints in general.              But we make a
reference to some of the spiritual experiences of Professor
R. 0. Ranade with the view of bringing ont the fact that
there is the vital connection between the experience and
the objective contro] which the saints get as a result of
the    experience,     We     have        also    the       authority          of
Mr. Kakasahib Karkhanisa of Bijapur who happened to
ke an @ye-witness and a direct and authoritative reporter
of some of the experiences           of    Professor        Ranade.        For
instance,    when the famous     Dr. N. ©. Sirdesai of Pune
told Professor Ranade that his-mother would expire on a
particular date, Professor Ranade told’ the Doctor in
advance that she will not die on that date. Later on when
he was asked as to how he could contradict the opinion
of the expert doctor, Ranade replied that                   he had seen
on    that   day   innumerable   beautiful’ eyes             everywhere.
This obviously implies that Ranade                had the connection
With the     experience of God in some form or the                    other,
which endowed-the devotee with the power                          ot having
objective control over natural phenomena.                              १   ५

      Another instance can be given regarding how                              an
imminent danger became avoidable by the grace of God,
When     the   Rajakars   had   burnt: the        station      at    Nimbal
which is at a distance hardly of half a furlong from the
Professor’s, ashrama,       Ranade    told       the    inmates      of    the
 ashrama that they should meditate on the name of God.
 As it was being done, the Rajakars went away from
 Nimbal without giving any attention                   to   the     ashrama.

Many 07 the so-called miracles which occurred
                                              during the
life-time of Professor Ranade, can be explained, as being
the result of Ranade’s meditation on the name of God
and contemplation on His form. A disciple of his tcld us
that he was saved from death because he was asked by
Professor Ranade to sleep one night in his rosm. The
next Morning Professor Ranade explained to some of his
other disciples that the last night was a danger to the life
of that disciple.

      Mr. Karkhanisa tells us that from Sravana to Magha,
that is, nearly for six months, Ranade could not take cr
digest even a little of milk. Just one day before the anni-
versary day of his Guru Shri Bhausahib Maharaja, he had
become so weak that with great difficulty and with the
support of the stick he could take a few steps in order
to go to his room. But on the very next day he was seen
moving here and there and seeing that the people who
were dining there were      comfortable    and   that .al]   the
arrangements had been properly done. Every one of the
persons who had assembled there in order to celebrate the
punyatithi of Shri Bhausahib ‘Maharaja felt surprise at
the vast difference between Ranade’s inactivity of the
previous ‘night and his great activities of the folicwing
day. At last he was asked to explain the matter, as to
how’ this miracle could happen. Ranade said that it was
all due to the power of God whom he was seeing all the
while.

         Secrion D: PROFESSOR RANADE’S VIEW
         ABOUT THE RELATION OF MORALITY
                 TO THE SPIRITUAL LIFE

      No Writer on Ethics, not even the Kantian or the
Bradleyan type, had given any adequate reason for the
question, ‘Why should I be moral?’        To say with Kant
that the ‘Can’ implies the ‘Ought’ or the Bradleyan con-
cept of morality that it occupies the      whole sphere of
life including religious consciousness, appears as dogmatic
                          The Summit at Nimbal                              175

solutions only, Professor Ranade alone has asserted for
the first time the emphatic,-clear cut, unambigous ‘solu-
tion .of the problem by saying that-there would-be no
spiritual life without the strength               and- backbone of the
morality.- He says with all the force that                   the     spiritual
life without morality is a hideous picture to look-at and
the immoral life, in other: words is .a hideous spectacle
and a-blot on the spiritual life.* Some one might say that
this is         a dogmatic reply of the Professor, but this ig not
80.    In view of the explanation which it seems he had in
his mind, a social life with all the necessities,                   adequate
justice, and. legal and political relations is 8 -life-which is
a     mutual and reciprocal             adjustment    of   several     living
human beings,..It rarely goes                deeper   and Jooks       to    the
intention: and motives of man, A: moral life deals primarily
with motives and              intentions and secondarily            with    the
results of actions            which    are neither illegal nor against
the interest of the State.               An immoral life is the sub-
version         of all the rules of conduct          and   so   creates     an
unbridgeable gulf between the body and mind, while the
spiritual life primarily deals with'the spirit in man. The
object and aim of it is the realization of the spirit and
God, and what Professor Ranade means is that no reali-
zation.of God is possible unless there is pari passu deve-
lopment of the moral fibre in man. For him the secular
and     the       spiritual    are    not   antithetical   terms,     but   he
affirms that the secular life will have its firm foundation
on the ethical conduct as well as the spiritual foree beyond

it. Spiritual life in his opinion moves on the two hinges
Of the moral life.and the well-disciplined life of medita-
-tion and contemplation,              as instructed by     the Guru.


       The unflinching devotion of Professor Ranade was not
Simply directed towards God.   1६ had its roots in his rela-
 tion with the society as well-as in his relation with the

 §00d and the holy. In other words, his whole spiritual

 ~         GOD-REALIZATION IS A SOURCE OF
                            ALL VIRTUES                        -

       It is    very    difficult   to    point out     which “pf the
several qualities such as            courage,    patience,   faith and
devotion, was the chief ingredient of Professcr Ranade
conviction that God would always protect his devotees
There would be as many answers to this question as there
are the qualities.      We make two observations in this con-
nection, One is that all these qualities of Professor Ranade
were just..different forms of one and the same supreme
quality, namely, his experience of God-realization The
other observation is that all these qualities made the
experience of God also as a permanent feature of his life
His courage was exemplified on several occasicns, His
constant association with his life-long friend, which was
in fact his life long enemy only, his illness due to Tuber-
culosis, could not have been possible              without assuming
that He was as brave or perhaps more than a veteran
warrior in the field of battle.            His undaunted       8558 tion

105 sister, who had served him for a number of years,
that he did not require anyone to serve him and that
She could leave him at any time, is an instance of his
courage.    His determination to visit his   Guru at Inchgiri
after he was discharged from the famous Sassoon Hospital
at Poona is another instance of his courege. His bidding
goodbye to the Deccan       Education   Society in   1924,   his
reply to Sri Aurobindo that time alone will show who is
Yight and     who   is wrong so far as the interpretation’ of
Heraclietus is concerned, his bold refisal      to   2८८. ihe
Vice-chancellorship of the University of Gujarat notwith-
‘Standing the temptation of rupees two lacs, and his clear-

he would go to Nimbal as many times as he would ‘feel
it necessary on account of his ill-health before accepting
the post of the Head of the Department: of Philcsophy,
are some of the other instances which exhibit his courage.

    His patience which was also nothing but the form of
his courage, can be seen exemplified when he deliberately
and consciously avoided to initiate the aspiring souls for
‘a period of 16 years, though his spiritual brother Shri
 Amburao Maharaja had told him in plain words in 1920
 that he had become fit to initiate the seekers into the
Spiritual fold,

    His indomitable will took the form of faith which not
only saved him but also others in time of trouble, for he
thought ‘that the divine name as imparted by the Guru,
does possess the power of saving a man from all sins and
talamities,    It was this unique, unbreakable faith of his.
which was for him a substitute for- the oath which was
usually administered by the Guru to the disciple,      namely
 that there would be neither any adultery nor any usurpa-
tion of another man’s money or land,          If the Name is
powerful, what is the specific advantege of taking an
 vath?     This courageous step of faith he could not have
 taken unless he wag backed up by his unflinching devo-
 tion and his imperishable experience of God-realization

SPIRITUAL BIOGRAPHY OF PROFESSOR RANADE
  ` The historical biography of                                  an       individual          or   of a
nation is located                in space            and     dated in             time.      The bio-
graphy of a very great saint remains ever beyond space
and      time.        History has              a    temporal sequence; the spirit
knows not the difference between the past and the future
It lives in the eternal present;                             and so,           he who becomes
one with the spirit has in fact very little external history
excepting that which is concerned with the date and the
‘location of his birth and death, with other ordinary events
of his life, such as educational career, marriage, off-springs
and withthe cares, the anxieties
                              and the                                                 difficultics in
life.    Therefore to consider Ranade as only a man who
was born in            Jamkhandi and in the Province of Karnataka,
and to mention that he was a great scholar, a professor
and      a       Vice-chancellor           is        only        0, describe             a    wooden
frame without taking any. note of his everlasting, univer-
sal presence in the hearts of all who are devoted to God.

        It is however extremely difficult to separate what
Ranade- thought about the spiritual life from what othe
great and ancient. sages before                              him          as     well        as    great
Saints all         over      the      world          had thought akout that life,
since his very method of expounding his view was to hide
them behind the views of others. However we need not
despair. For this very method is the clue to find him out
from his hiding place. He has expounded the ‘philosophical
views and the mystical experiences of several other sages
and saints in India only to point out his own direct
experience of God-head and the philosophy he erected on
that very broad bed-rock of God-realization, What others
had said before him is the exact picture of what पील
                                                                              .




                     The Summit at Nimbal                                   179

wishes to say. He has made others to speak for him. And
whenever there would           occur different versions or differ-
ent view-points, his         own version      and    his own point           of
view prevails on all occasions.             The spiritual biographies
of other saints constitute           his own auto-biogiaphy. We
shall see this illustrated in what follows though for want
of space we Shall not be able to explain fully how it is
80.



      Ranade puts        forth his    own     view        akout    what     an
Avatara’ (incarnation of God)    means, by separating it
from all other traditidnal views. Who indeed will be able .
ever to convince others that the highest God cumes to
birth for the sake of protecting his devotees and of des-
troying their enemies?          One may believe this or not as
he likes, or as he is taught to believe. An ‘Avatara’ (‘tr.’
with    ‘ava’ as    prefix    means    in    Sanskrit       ‘to    descend’),
according to Ranade, is the vision of God which descends
from above and manifests before him in some definite
Form, without there being any physical body made up of
flesh and bones.         This is exactly the description of the
Atman as‘we find in the Upanishad.                     One        might    say
that Ranade        serves     iwo purposes        by      this    detinition,
Firstly, he points       out that what he           has    experienced is
Supported by the Upanishadic                statement, and secondly,
that the immaculate Alman             (which has the form of the
body of the individual denoted by the words ‘aham’ and
‘tvam’) is identical with the Brahman or God,                             (Jivo
Brahmaiva na        aparah) That is         why     his    definition aims
at     realizing   the      Atman     as    distinguished          from     all
others which have their aim in having prosperity in this
world, combined with long hfe, health, power, progeny
and fame, or in enjoying pleasures in the heaven, by
Pleasing a particular deity by means of the performance
of sacrifices, or the giving          of charity, etc.            There are
Schools, he knew, which believe in the intellectual under-
Standing of the nature of God, like “that of the Vedanta

 and the Samkhya, schools of asceticism and yogic powers,
180         Prof. के, 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


as well as several varieties of dualism and pluralism. His
is    the   Schoo]    of mystical,       humanistic,        eudaemonism       in
which activity            and   quietism,    devotion and unitive joy,
meditation          and    contemplation       are       involved   simultane-
ously.      It is the school in which the disciple can become
the Guru on account of his twofold discipline, the moral
and the spiritual, and comes to know that both he as
well as the Guru            are    identical   with the        Atman or the
Brahman; it is the schoo] which teaches that the ultimate
category       of    existence      is   ecstatic    joy     and    peace;   the
school which teaches that there is one God, one humanity,
and the only one way of devotion by which one becomes
dissolved in the Absolute like a river into the sea


        In the school of Ranade from St. Kadasiddha to him-
self,    it is God who is thought of                as    equally. present in
the heaven and on the earth as also in the intervening
space between them                and the beycnd and in all the ele-
ments of Akasa,           air, fire and water, who reveals Himself,
either directly or through the intermediary of the spiritual
teacher to all those who surrender with faith,                      love, fear
and regard.         This revelation is in the form of transmission
of the glory and power of God according to the capacity
of the disciple or the son Who is adopted -or initiated by.
the spiritual teacher. Naturally the disciples would differ
in degree and not in kind. One will have’ that: much
water from the river Ganga as he has his vessel, small
or large. With the constant addition of devctional fuel
fire in the form of vision of God also wi'l grow and
become-constant: with the continual life-long exercise of
meditation on the divine Name as imparted by the Guru
the illuminational and the unilive knowledge and power
of God comes to reside in the meditator just as the burn-
ing power of the fire comes in the iron which is put in it
Therefore, according to the school of Ranade, as also fom
the Upanishadic view-point or from the viewlpoint of
           in India, to know God is to Lecome God. This
great saints
however. is the. epistemological rendering of the meta-
                         The Summit at Nimbal                                181


physical truth that the soul is already one with God. He
15 said to become God when he comes ic know his own
self and when he sheds the various upadhis or the limita-
tions of body,:senses, mind, intellect and                  egoism, which
have somehow clung to him on account of the forgatful-
ness -that he      was     already     the    Brahman,       and       not   the
‘shadow of things with which he had identified himself.
The spate      which       is    confined    in a jar or a rcom is not
different from the one, eternal space.                It is already non-
different from it, but appears to be different on account
 of the walls of the jar and the room.                The moment they
are ‘broken,       the    apparent     difference     between          the   two
spaces vanishes and there remains one space (akasa only).
 The artilicial barriers that create the feeling of duality
 and plurality: disappear and              there    comes    to    light     the
 original, undivided and the indivisible unity of the reality
 of the’ Brahman,         That unity is like the meeting of fire
 with fire, or of water with water.                 And it is this unity
 of the soul with         God      which     Ranade    achieved         by   the
 devotional process of meditation on the Name of God.


   `   First’ of   all, the      cancelled    the    distinction       between
 Sravana and Manana,              What he heard physically from his
 Guru, namely the Name of God, he used to hear it by
 uttering it loudly       and hearing the same by his physical
 ears,"He then       used       the physiological method          of   uttering

 the Name       either      with the       ingoing    and    the outgoing
 breaths, semi-mental ox with the throbbing of the nerves
 in the throat, and then purely with the mental process of
 distinctly and clearly uttering and simultaneously hearing
 Of it by the mind only.           In other words, what he did was
 to divide the mental power in attending to two objects.

     1 meditation is all-in-all, one would like         to       know
 What exactly is Professor Ranade’s idea regarding the
- Mutual relations    of Name,    Form    (under it come the
 various spiritual experiences namely the tac‘ual, visual.
 auditory, gustatory and the olfactory) and mental disposi-
` Gon or devotional Attitude (Nama, Rupa and Bhava).                A
188    Prof. ह. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


mere   intellectualist     might   say   —   and   no   doubt         quite
correctly   also   —     that whereas    Rupa is important meta-
physically, .Nama is important            epistemologically           while
Bhava is the result of Nama and              Rupa taken tovether
Ranade  would say that Nama is greater than Rupa
because unless one meditates there will be no manifesta-
tion of Rupa, and unless there is the             revelation of the
visions, sounds, touch and presence of God, there would
be no emergence of Bhava, that is, of the feeling of love,
reverence, fear, awe and dependence            for the       most sub-
lime and beautiful face of God.          And what after all would
be our metaphysical view of Reality except that which
is obseryanle by our epistemological telescope? And how
can   there be     any    emotional   reactions    unless what          we
observe through the telescope strikes our intuitive eye as
something uniquely grand, sublime, beautiful and inspir-

if there is no emotional warmth, no devotional zest and
fervour, there cannot, be meditation on the Name nor any
growth in our illuminational participation in the nature
ofthe divine life.                                           ।


  ` The combination of these three aspects of Nama, Rupa
and Bhava corresponds to that of three aspects of the
Vedantic conception, namely, sat, cit and ananda or to
that’ of the devotional school of the saints in - general
namely, Bhakti Jnana and Vairagya.            Indeed    it       is   very
difficult to say which aspect of the Vedantic triad or that
of the Bhakti school is prior to which. The existential
static’ aspect of the Brahman will be of no value unless
we take into consideration the dynamic aspect of the
spirit which makes its movement from the individual soul
(Jiva) to the Brahman and vice versa, and its subsequent
enjoyment of the eternal bliss (Ananda). Why should one
stop at Ananda, even though we have the. Sruti statement
‘Anandam Brahma’? After. all, ananda also is a state of the
mind,a wave on the ocean of the Brahman There is another
                     The Summit at Nimbal                     189


Sruti statement which goes beyond this; it is, “Shantam
Brahma Upasita,” meaning thereby “one should meditate
upon the Brahman as Peace incarnate”.         As the peace of
the supramental condition goes beyond bliss of the mental
condition, Ranade was very often seen as a living golden
staiue of perfect calmness radiating the same, like the
lustre of a diamond or the moon, on all who were fortu-
nate to see him,      Even so, it was very difficult     to   say
whether he was rapt in deep meditation on the Name of
God (Bhakti) or joyfully one with the vision which might
have been present before him (Jnana) or in the condition
of absolute desirelessness or disgust for all Godless things

(Vairagya), Who can say which of the three things in the
cyclic order is prior to which?      Therefore, our only con-
clusion regarding Ranade’s spiritual life is that he was at
once three in one, Bhakti, Jnana and Vairagya, or Sat.
Cit and Ananda, or better still, the peaceful undisturbed
condition of the Brahman itself, in which case, to worship
him was te worship the Brahman,


    This direct and immediate vision of the immaculate
form of himself from foot to head end the consciousness
that that Form was both       the   inner   and    the   external
(Antaryamin and the Bahiryamin) Atman or Brahman in
  whom he could perceive the existence of all beings and
. who was seen to be existing in all beings, constituted for
him, the real and the ultimate Guru          andthe       eternal
 Source of all.    That was the   extraordinary Punya     (merit)
 as undersionod by Nimbargi Maharaja which he could see
 always kefore him, and not the merit which the ordinary
 People think they can acquire by        worshipping the un-
 Atman.    It     meant perpetual freedom    and    immortality.
 From that Punya he could tap the energy he wanted to
 Sustain his life, and not from a ‘piece or two of bread or
 a cup of tea or water. That is why he could say about
 himself in Allahabad when about fifty people had come
 ‘to his house to celebrate some spiritual function in 1945,
190     ०. हे. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


“People   say I havenot eaten anything during the last
cight days; do they know that I have not taken a drop
of water during these days?” We must remember how-
ever that he was not lying in bed, though he was not
quite well. He was carrying on all his routine duties of
attending the    university,   giving   lectures,   reading    and
going   out for meditation on the bank of Jamuna,             Just
as God-realization or    the seeing     of the Atman by the
Atman was for Ranade the highest and the only real way
of worshipping the Atman, even so that was for him the
final act of perfecting the moral endeavour of man. For
without such God-realization, even the so-called moral!
excellence of a great soul     might lead him to indulge in
boastful egoism which is the      deadliest    enemy    of    man.
Without love for God anger may not be sublimated into
compassion and sympathy; it might be transformed into
hatred and enmity. How can then arise, as it arose in
Rznade, the disinterested love for all those who love God,
as if they are the nearest relatives, and how can one
cease to be a relative   of those     who do   not love God?
There will then    be friendship based on the motives of
profit or pleasure. and never on principle.         And if there
be-no- meditation on the Name of God or contemplation
on His form, will theretbe any other way of arresting the
wayward mind from’running into the vicious modés of
life?   And, if the mind and after it the body is allowed
to behave-as it likes, why should one talk at all about
self-control, self-sacrifice, meditation, contemplation and
about the attitude of love to God and to the Guru? So,
the only hope of achieving all these will be to begin the
meditative life under the guidance of the Guru and not
to lock into the past, however bad it may be. Sit for
meditation at a fixed hour and for a fixed period of time;
kegin with the quantitative measure first, and then in
course of time, with the emergence of the attitude of love
for God there would emerge, says Ranade, the qualitative
aspect of meditation, First, do the meditation on the
Name in the spirit of ‘Duty for duty’s sake’, and thon
                    The Summit at Nimbal                              191


automatically with the emergence of the beautiful ‘visions
of God, there would arise the compelling attraction and
love fér God.   Those who would advise us to love                   God
without seeing Him will not understand what Ranade
and other great saints tell us. As a matter of fact it is
not sufficient to see God: it is necessary to know that God
also sees us When we look at him. As Ranade puts it,
“mutual inspection is necessary for              mutual love.” One
must know that he loves God and that God too loves him
in return, In spiritual life at least this kind of reciprecal
love is necessary, and unless this becomes a fact of spiri-
tual life, there will be no kind of equipoise (Samatva),
neither physical nor mEntal nor spiritual.


   Thus, when Self-consciousness           (with       S as    a capital
letter indicating thereby the consciousness of the Atman
or Brahman) would become the highest Good of life, when
in other words, theie would exist nothing else but the
tlissful Brahmanic¢ consciousness as the onlv content of
Reality, there would then be no meaning left, according
to Professor Ranade,    even   to   the   words,       ‘Thou’,   ‘I’ and
the ‘Al’ in ‘Thou art that’, ‘I’ am the Brahman’ and ‘All
this is Brahman’,    For the first two       statements,         though
grammatically   correct,   would    thoroughly          be    wrong    if
notwithstanding the Brahmanic consciousness which alone
is their content,   they   are interpreted as expressing the
existence of a Separate ‘thow or an ‘I’ from the existence
of the Brahman,      The two sentences           are   known in       the
Vedanta as the “Mahavakyas” or ‘great statements’ be-
cause they express the existence of a single entity, namely.
the Brahman, and therefore imply the destruction of the
entire content of the two words, ‘thou’ and ‘I’.             Thus inter-
preted, the two words not only            lose    their      substantive
meaning but the adjectival meaning          also,      They are com-

Fletely lost or dissolved in the          one existence of            the
Brahman, just as rivers lose their nama and rupa in the
Ocean or the dream is lost in the wakeful state.               If ‘thou’
 and ‘ would or must retain their separate individuality
192       Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


in some form or other, the reality of the Brahman would,
to that extent, be limited by something other than itself,
and then there would not have been these two Mahavakyas
at all.    The Sankara      Advaita would           to that   extent   be
modified into the Ramanujayan pattern of Advaita, which
in fact ought to be known          not    as    ‘Visistadvaita’,       but
‘Visista Dvaita’,



      Similarly,    the   statement,   ‘All    is    Brahman’ is not
intended to convey the idea that an ‘All exists side by
side with the Brahman, or that the ‘All’ exists first and
that the Brahman is immanent in all. The statement is
not the expression of a variety of Pantheism with stress
on Pan (= all). If we are to become aware of the exist-
ence of the ‘all’ first and then of the Brahman, we shall
have only a Wordsworthian type of pantheism where all
the objects of nature such as a rainbow, a lily flower. or
a blade of grass, have a prior and independent existence,
but which subsequently are able to point out to us that
they are all “informed by the spirit of God.” The Advaita
of Vedanta, and much more so, the Advaita experience of
Reality wishes to establish a pantheism where the stress
is on the word ‘Theos’ and not at all on the ‘pan’. In this
variety of pantheism which Ranade like other great saints
wants us to understand is that what is real from the
beginning to the end is the ‘Theos’ or the Brahman, the
homogeneous existence of which is neither dependent on
ihe Nature, nor on the concept of the jivahood which is
expressed for the sake of convenience or social inter-
communication by the words ‘thou’ and ‘I’, Not only this,
but from-the point of view of the consciousness of the
pure, blissful Atman, even the words ‘immanent’ and
‘transcendent’ lose their meaning. The moment one asks
the questions, Immanent in what? T.anscending what ?’
ore has to pesit first the independent existence of a world
or that of a jiva in order to make the question sensible
enough for being understood.       But in that case, the aware-
ness of a separate and independent world or a jiva will
                    The Summit at Nimbal                         193

necessarily   put   limitation   on the      ecstatic,   indivisible
consciousness of being nothing         but   Brahman, and be a
cause of 21] further divisions, differences and distinctions
which are the lot of all common men in the world.                 No
doubt people and some philosophers do speak that God i8
both immanent and transcendent, but that is the language
of a theist and not of a mystic. And unless theism rasses
into mysticism, none can understand what Renade or any
other great saint stands for. Instead of spiritual life, there
will then be only verbal knowledge,            mere      intellectual
informers, not men of insight.


    Now, if the world remains as a garment of God, as
Green suggested, or the body and the jivahood remain as
vestures of the Atman, they have to be finally climinated
tor the sake of the bliss and peace of the Brahman.               In
that case, Ranade is justilied when he says that for spii-
tual aspirants neither the solutions of cosmological             and
cosmogonical questions     nor   of eschatological inqmry        will

bring any good, though they are of some philcsophical
value and interesting also to handle. It our whole con-
cern is about what we can achieve spiritually within the
Short mortal life granted to us and when we are debarred
from knowing with certainty what we were betcre birth
or what shall we be after death, all our arguments about
the existence of God or about the survival of the soul
beyond death can give us only intellectual pleasure, as
Bertrand Russell says in his first book on philosophy in
1912.   Whether the earth came out of water or air or fire
ot whether it will return to any one of these elements
has nothing to do with the destiny of man. The ccsmolo-
Bical argument is nothing        but   anthropomorphism         writ
large, God is like a potter; and then finding it difficult
to say what that material was out of which God produced
 he universe, we are. compelled to say that God is both
the efficient and the material cause. To speak in favour
of the teleological argument is to place the teleos beyond
‘the ken of God. The only argument worth the
                                                   name is
the ontological argument for, an aspirant passes from the
194       Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


idea of God, namely the Name, to the reality of His Forms.
Even so, the belief in the transmigration of the soul or
in the threefold karma theory has no other utility except
as    an incentive for leading    a good moral life.   Beycnd
this there is no substantial value attached to these views,
There is for the devotee of God, says St. Tukarama.,
‘neither any Sancita     (siore-house of the effects of karma
in the previous lives) nor any Kriyamana (the effects cf
actions    in. the future)   nor, the Prarabdha   (that karma
which has begun its course.”       His karma as a whole be-
comes burnt as soon as he is initiated in the spiritual life
by a great realized sou]


      We think that Ranade has once for all dispelled the
wrong notion that the saint is afraid of facing the world
and therefore chooses to fly away from it. It is entirely
wrong to say that he is a coward, a pessimist and is habi-
tuated to look to the dark side of the worldly life.     Like
Jnanadeva and Tukarama, the two great saints of India,
Ranade too had suffered much and had 10 pass through
great temptations and ordeals of life.     He lived the life
of a householder, as a man of the world doing the duties
of a scholar, a professor and a vice-chancellor, and he has
shown by his own example that there cannot be any con-
tradiction between the doing of duties and the perform-
ance of spiritual exercise. The doctrine of ‘Videha mukti’
(liberation after death) meant for him “Liberation with-
out the consciousness of the body” (‘Vi? — without the
consicousness: ‘deha’ — body). What then if the body exists
or does not exist ? or if the world exists or 0765 not exist?
So long as the body exists, one should achieve liberation
by being non-different from God: and so. for Ranade
‘Videha mukti? meant nothing but ‘“Jiven-mukti’. (Libera-
tion while living) that is liberation without the conscious-
ness of the body being real. And as mind, intellect
egoism and the several other conditions are states like
wakefulness.    sleen and dream and connected with the
body, liberation for Ranade meant the severance of all
these sheaths or appendages from the innermost reality of
                       The Summit at Nimbal                            195

the Atman, This does not mean the destruction of them
all,   It only means the consciousness of the Atman only
in spite of the presence of them all. So, let the world
remain; let the body, mind etc. remain; the saint’s spiritual
life is not affected by them in the least.             Ranade does not
however Seem to be content by achieving his own libera-
tion; he is equally interested in the ‘Parivara-mukti’, that
is liberation of {1092 who are associated with him as his
parivara    (associates), That is why, neither the medita-
tional    liberation       (Ratana mukti     of   3211170),     nor    the
libcration of gods living in the vicinity of the supreme
Being (Nikata-mukti) nor the liberation in the form of
ceing like God (Sarupya-mukti of Ramanuja) satisfied
him. He believed in the supremacy of that kind of libera-
tion     which is    the     combination    of    self-surrender       and
identity with       God,    and    so he   can    be    compared with
Jnanadeva, Mirabai or Sankaracharya, or with the ideal
saint of the Bhagavad Gita who derives bliss by being
lodged in the Atman, that is one’s own immaculate Form.


       It Seems to be a puzzle however to decide whether
Ranade’s God was Personal or Impersonal                       (Saguna or
Nirguna), especially in view of his firm kelief that a
physical manifestation of God is impossible to be believed
philosophically or Scientifically. While 10 Kabira God is
‘nirguna’ inasmuch as He is spotless or immaculate, and
is full of light and sound, to Tulsidasa Rama as ‘saguna’,
is Superior to ‘nirguna’, though not contradictory to it.
Rama according to Tulsidasa, aroused, the various emotions
of dread, valour. filial affection, sex, peace, bliss and an
indescribable       emotion       respectively    in    the    minds    of
demons, warriors, parents, women, philosophers, saints
and Sita. No doubt the description of Rama as ‘Saguna’
in Tulsidasa is richer in meaning than the ‘Niranjana’ of

Kabira. ‘Nirguna’ might be the source of ‘saguna’, but
Says Tulsidasa, it is the devotion in the form                   of the
niterance of Name that leads one to the attainment of
nirguna.     On the other hand, Kabira believes that the
 nirguna’ includes in it the ‘saguna’. For instance, he says
196       Prof. ह. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


that the bodily form of a saint is a fully representative
personal     (saguna)    and    physical replica of     the    Godhead.
Ranade solves the impasse by going beyond एन saguna
and   nirguna by his      conception
                              of Gunatita, which does
neither mean the absence of qualities, nor the presence
of them, Tulsidasa also confesses to his ignorance as to
whether God should be called saguna or nirguna, meaning
thereby that His rea] nature is beyond the gunas of Maya.
Ranade however inclines to accept the view of Jnanadeva
who holds that the saint and God serve as mirrors to each
another, which forget their difference in seeing each
other’s    reflection.   This    is   nothing     but   equating      the
saguna with the nirguna aspect of God.              The infinite God
or the Absolute appears in the form of the saint, while
the saint appears as       God,       There is reciprocity between
Saguna and nirguna,        the Bimba and the Pratibimba, or
the Nijarupa or the Svarupa and the Nirakara.                  This we
can see further illustrated both during the lifetime of a
perfect, realized saint as well ag after his passing away
The Upanishad       (Isa. 5) describes the saint as being seen
ata       distance and everywhere even while he is sitting or
lying down in a particular place. That is, if it 15 possible
to see an embCdied (saguna) saint as disembodied but
as having a bodily form (nirguna) even while he is living,
it is not impossible for his being seen in a disembodied,
immaculate form after the fall of his body, inasmuch as
the personal] changes into the impersonal, or the saguna
aspect of God which was visible while the saint lived is
changed into the nirguna after his passing away. The
saint Mirrors the Absolute no doubt while he is living but
not wholly, but after his passing away there is nothing
to prevent his spirit from being merged into the Absolute
spirit.    The Personal (saguna)           gives place to the imper-
sonal (nirguna). This is like the merging of the space in
a jar with the outside          infinite     space after the    jar   {5
broken. Produce a jar, the space appears as if it is divided;
break the jar, the infinite space is known to be as
indivisible as before, God becomes saguna for the sake
                    The Summit at Nimbal                          197

of his devotee, but when the devotee becomes one with
God, he realizes that God is as nirguna as he was before.
The Caturbhuja-rupa of Krsna and for that matter, all
the experiences of manifested forms,    qualities, colours
and sounds etc. come, as we are told in the Bhagavad Gita,
from the original, one infinite nirguna          nature of       God.
And yet, the whole of the spiritual life must move like a
shuttle-cock backwards and forwards between the saguna
and the nirguna aspects of God,


    But when a saint becomes so God-intoxicated as to
believe that he is beyond all considerations of body, mind,
senses, intellect and egoism, when in other words, as
Ranade puts it, the saint like Ramadasa considers Rama
to be hig dasa (servant) or when God becomes the torch-
bearer and the guide walking before            the    devotee,   and
holding an umbrella over       him or    as going      kehind     his
devotee in order to apply the dust of his feet to his fore-
head, it Should     be   no   wonder    that    the   saint   would
ejaculate in ecstasy, just as Tukarama did, and say that
he was present when aeons ago the great Sukracarya did
his penance on the Meru mountain.         Once again, like the
shuttle-cock, the saint would move not only like the sage,
Narada, from one world to another but also go into the
hoary past or in the utterly uncertain future and do all
Sorts of miraculous deeds.     Will he then cut his tongue
from below in order to sip the oozing nectar falling in
the throat from above?        He will drink it to his heart’s
content by simply repeating the Name of God, and enable
others to drink it by transmitting his spiritual power into
them.   Himself being sinless, he will make others also
sinless by enabling them to become united with God. The
keys of the kingdom of God will be in his hand, and none
will then prevent even if the greatest of the sinners is
allowed by him to enter into it.       With him as the inter-
mediary,   God will shower his grace upon the sinner even

if he be in hell,   He will persuade God to eat with him
to speak and to walk with him.          Sleep, idleness and all
एकलः vices and sins will then run away from him;                  he
198     Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

will even enjoy the bliss of the super-wakeful state of
unison With God in all the three conditions of life, namely,
wakefulness of the world and the so-called sleeping and
dreaming states, nay even in the fourth Vedantic state
known-as Turya,      Being perpetually God-cenitred in       the
midst of actions, he will ever.be free like God from the
effects of actions, Yet, so long as he has a body, he will
never covet the wealth     of others nor have a lustful eye
on any woman.      Morality, a certain amount of asceticism

and renunciation, and disgust for all god-less things will
be the back-bone of his spiritual life, for he knows that a
life even beyond the sattva-guna can never       be attained
and maintained     without being always on     the   level    of
sattva guna, and by not allowing the sattva to be over-
come by rajas and tamas.      He knows very well that the
way Of God’s love is the way of fire, that he who throws
himself in it will be saved and that he who keeps himself
away will be burnt to death.



      In such a state of the highest, spiritual ascent, what
will remain for ever is only the homogeneous life divine,
neither God nor the devotee, just as according to
Jnanadeva, what will remain for ever is the knowledge
and not the two terms on either side of it, namely the
jnata (knower) and the jneya (the known object). They
will be dissolved in the great ocean of self-consciousness,
and lose their nature and names as God and as devotee.
They are merely two relative words which cannot be
known unless the tie of love which binds them is known,
and lived. The devotee is a devotee because he partici-
pates in the nature of God, and God is God because he
cannot live without his devotee. Or, as Ramadasa puts if.
a ‘Bhakta’ (devotee) is that who is not ‘Vibhakta’
(different) from God; and God is that who is identically
the same inside or outside the body .of the devotee, that
is whether he looks at Him with his eyes shut or open.
There occurs in other words “a perfect parity between
the interna] and externa] perception.”

        Let us now Observe in a more detailed manner the
    characteristics of an ideal saint as well as some prominent
    features of the spiritual life as they were observable in
    Professor Ranade :

         (i)   The first and the foremost character of an ideal
    saint which Ranade exhibited at any time to an external
    observer was that he had always joy on his face.                         With
    all the physical ailments and pain throughout his body,
    he was never gloomy or dejected, Lassitude, weariness
    and dissatisfaction found no place in his being. Yet tke
    joy he used to enjoy was not an experience of a happy-
    go-lucky man; it was the Brahmanic joy which he had
    earned after great and long toil throughout many years
    of his life.     Through thick and-thin he used to keep the
    flame of God-realization blazing on account of his medi-
    tation and contemplation on the -Name and                    the visions
    of God constantly,

        (ii)      Along with joy there was fearlessness which also
    was the result of       his   being     united   with       the     blissful
    Brahman,       He remained selt-poised in the midst of all his
    activities,    because he had only to look inside or outside.
    to see the blissful and fearless face of God, which consti-
    tuted for him the “Sahaja Samadhi”, and which therefore
    was not at all dependent on the control of breath (Prana-
    nirodha) and was on that account different from physio-
    logical samadhi, known          as ‘Kumbhaka’, whether it was
    internal on account of the hoarding of air in the lungs cr
    external due to the keeping of the           air outside           the bedy
    {the ‘Antah-kumbhaka’ or the ‘Bahir-kumbhaka’). In the
`   words of       Sankaracharya,    that   might    be       called    as    the
    “‘kevala kumbhaka’ during which the saint can be said
    to have built for himself a supportless cottage between
    the navel and the top of the brain (ketween the Muladhara
    Cakra and the Sahasrara), Here in this ‘kevala kumbhaka’
    the breathing stops without any efforts, naturally and
    automatically as the result of intently gazing at the form

    of God, or on account of the uncommon ability of the saint
200      Prof. ह. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


in being aware of the Atman, in every utterance of the
name of GOd, and in seeing the divine form before seeing
any Other object, as intervening between              the sight and
that object,


      (111)     He was so generous as to      allow    his       spiritual
experience to gravitate to his disciples.           Not only did he
drink the mellifluous juice of God’s name himself, but he
made others also able to drink it, as also able t> visualize
the   unseen forms and        the unheard     and unstruck sound
divine,


      (iv)      For a major portion of his time, Ranade used to
live in the super-conscious state known as the ‘Unmani’, `
which is not, in his opinion a fourth           (turiya) added on
to the        already existing three conditions of wakefulness,
sleep and dream and          therefore   on   par   with them but
Which is at the back of all the three states and which
when it exists there exist not the three states as different
from one another,


     (v) Naturally, as a consequence of this, his soul,
which must have been like all other souls ‘Pent up inside
a tenement of bones’ began to swim and dive like a fish
in the sea of joy, or to sing unhindered like an Upanishadic
Seer and thereby worship his own immaculate form. Then
his affection knew no bounds. Whether he descended to
the very gravel of the earth. or transcended the limits of
the earthly horizon he was always one with the Atmanic
                                                             ॥
bliss and peace,


      (vi)     This   spiral method of ascension or descension,
like the great sage Narada was adopted by him, we think.
with the double motive of raising the humanity to the
level of divinity and of bringing down the divinity or
establishing the kingdom of God on this very earth. That
was his rea] democratic ideal of equality of all men
before God; for he feared that all talk about equality.
fraternity and freedom without’ God was only a pretence
                      The Summit at Nimbal                                  201

to hide selfishness, jealousy and tyranny. That is why, he
‘was modest in spiritual        ambition.      Neither       he     was    too.
Selfish to think that ‘Eka-mukti’ (salvation of him alone
who meditates) was the goal of                spiritual      life    nor    too
poetic and pretentious to believe that ‘Sarva-mukti’                       (sal-
vation for one and all the Souls) was the                   goal.    As     the
golden mean between these two extremes, he believed in
the possibility of achieving ‘Parivara-mukti’ as commend-
ed by Saint Kabira,         He did invite all his assotiates to
come into the boat that was to carry him                      to the other
tank of the dreadful river of Maya.


    (vii)    One of the most difficult things for Ranade was
to convince the people to equate the Guru with God. The
Upanishad is ambiguous on the point.                   It simpiy states
that one who wishes to have prosperity (let us assume
material as well        as spiritual)     should worship ithe omni-
scient    Being.    The   problem       to    solve   is,    who      is   that
omniscient     Being,    God   or   the      Guru?    We          think: that
Ranade solved        this after the pattern of              St.     Ramadasa
who says that ‘one becomes bereft of all his defects by
seeing Rama in the form of his own innermost, immacu-
late Atman.’       Ranade holds that a mystic alone can know
how to make “Self-consciousness enter sabda” (the name
of God), This means that every utterance of the Name
ought to be attended by Rupa, or that Name (sabda) and
Rupa ought to be almost             simultaneously          present.       This
‘will happen only in the case of a perfect saint who sees
the face of God the moment he utters the Name of God;
and by face of God, we now mean the immaculate face
of the saint himself.        This is how,       instead of           bringing
himself in the picture,        Ranada’ indicated that the Guru
is God.     He was however very careful to give the warning
not to ‘confuse the Guru with the body of the Guru,                         but
would clearly point out to some chosen and worthy dis-
ciple of his that the vision of God was the Guru. This
meahs,    in other words,      that the self     (Atman or Brahman
or God) who enters into the sabda               (or into the meditator
on the sabda) is the Guru.          This is analogous to the saying
202    Prof. कि .   D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage

of Jnanadeva who has expressed his opinion in the very
first verse of hig ‘Jnanesvari’,
                             that God himself has be-
come the deity of Ganesa, What this ought to mean for
the spiritual aspirants is that Ganesa can do the work as
God would do; or that the embodied Guru can do the
work of the disembodied God, just as perceptible fire or
iron ball which is made red hot by fire does the work of
the imperceptible fire. Guru and God can thus be thought
of as interchangeable so far as the spiritual upliftment
of the humanity is concerned,       We may however call God
as the Guru of Gurus, inasmuch as Guru cannot be Guru
without the realization of God.


      (ण)    The Guru however is more than a saint. though
koth might be perfect so far as the realization of God is
concerned,    The Guru is the       king   among   saints.   Our
virtuous life may flower in the company of the good and
the haly; it shall bear fruit only when one receives the
grace of the Guru, as of God. The Guru is the farmer who
Sows the seed, the saint is like a gardener who maintains
the garden. The Guru is the mother who feeds the child
on milk of her breasts, the saint a nurse or a baby-sitter
who takes care of the baby, but is unable to feed it
on her breast.      A saint can do therefore very little if he
has no sanction from God to initiate a seeker in the
spiritual life. He might save himself, but is not capable
of saving others.      The Guru has the double capacity of
saving himself and of saving others, and while perform-
ing the latter function, the Guru takes the burden of all
the past sins. the present miseries and the dangers of the
future upon his own head, and protects his disciples from
all temptations, bewilderment and fear. In doing so, he
never becomes fatigued. nor moved to anger by being
aware of their innumerable faults. On the contrary, he
forgives them again and again a thousand times and
brings them back on the path of virtues and godliness.
Let him be far away from them, they all are very close
to him during his meditation. He prays to God for them
and begs pardon for all their sins and follies,
                      The Summit at Nimbal                                 203

    (ix)   On no account Ranade would allow a compro-
mise between a spiritual and an immoral life on the false
pretext or the wrong notion that saintliness goes beyond
both morality and           immorality.   He         could    not    tolerate
even the effort of the author of the great devoticnal work,
‘Bhagavata,’    in     allowing      promiscuous           sexual    relation
between the Gopis and the Lord Krsna under the sup-
position that it was nothing but an innocent sport on the
part of the Lord or that the Lord had gone beyond papa
and punya. If perfection in the moral life cannot be had
without spiritual life, the latter too without the back-bone
of morality would simply be a hideous picture to look at.
If the predorninance of the sattvaguna can be had cnly
by conquering the rajas and tamas, the predominance of
the virtuous life.      This only means that the virtuous life
without going beyond the egoistic pride of having attained
the Suddha sattva (pure spiritual                  life)   cannot   be    had
must be maintained at all costs for the very                    sustenance
of the spiritual life; only the arrogance of being morally
perfect’ must be dropped. But if in the attempt to drop
the egoism of being moral one would like to drop the
Moral- life also,    it would be like throwing away the baby
along with the bath.         Morality is like gunpowder without
which there will be          no explosion by merely possessing
the spiritual   spark.       St.   Tukarama holds that the               Para-
martha will not be possible unless one goes beyond the
three gunas of sativa, raja and tama, but at the same time
reminds us that       it is sattvaguna        (a    virtuous life)    which
alone has got the power of introducing us to the life divine
and of nourishing it day and night. Ranade is firm and
resolute in maintaining that the spiritual life must move
on the hinges of morality on the one hand                     and   medita-
‘tion on the Name of God, on the other.

     (x)   No saint has attained the spiritual heighits all
of a sudden,  Ranade must have observed in his own life
as Well as in the lives of other saints in the course of his
Studies and come to the. same, conclusion to which the
Saint Mirabai       came,    namely,   that    the     real   spiritual   life
204    Prof. R. D. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


is very often a continuous progressive realization of God,
or that there is always an asymptotic approximation to
the   Goc-head,    excepting however         that in    certain    cases
there might be a sudden rise to that state.             This doctrine
of Approximation,         we   think   is nothing    but a corollary
from Professor         Ranade’s another doctrine, namely,           that
the spiritual life is a process and not a series of isolated
events,   Just    as    the darkness   of    the    night   diminishes
gradually and changes into the light of the day, even so
Ranade believes that the aspirant makes a steady progress
from sin and ignorance towards virtue and knowledge.
The decrease and the increase in the two opposite modes
Of life Occur by small degrees. There are degrees of
Reality in the philosophies of Plato, Aristotle, Bradley
and Sankaracharya, and not of kind or rather we should
say that there are degrees in the             apprehension        of the
Reality. Instead of saying with H. G. Wells that God is
in the making we should say that the spiritual aspirant is
in the making. What good will be there in merely intel-
lectually understanding that the            Ideal   world    of    Ideas
differs   from   the sensible world or that the world of
appearance differs from that of the Reality or that the worlds
ot dream, wakefulness and of illusion differ from that of
the direct vision of God, if we do not understand how to
make the transition from the one to the other? When
the Upanishad (Katha 1, 2.20) tells us that the Brahman
is smaller than the smallest and larger than the largest,
how do we know 108६ notwithstanding the apparent
logical contradiction, it is a statement of fact of experi-
ence ? Would it only remain as a metaphysical proposition
in spite of the logica] contradiction, or become the expres-
sion of a state which 35 actually lived by someone, and.
therefore, raised to the dignity of a psychological and
epistemological experience? When the saint Ramadasa
describes the vision of Hanuman (in fact the vision of the
Lord Rama, the innermost Atman) as growing larger and
larger from an ‘anu’ to the universe-enveloping Form, or
when saint Tukarama is describing himself being as large

प
                   The Summit at Nimbal                            205

as the infinite akasa, they are not describing an immov-
able static vision, but an all-pervading dynamic vision of
the Atman which is nothing but the ever-growing mysti-
cal experience of themselves.     Perhaps,      William       James’
conception of ‘Stream of consciousness’ comes very close
to thig experience provided the stream is one which
increases in volume, beauty, glory and sublimity.



    (xi)   The    Svetasvatara   Upanishad      (2.11)      mentions
several mystical experiences     such as the vision of the
sun, the fire, the fire fly, the moon ete. It will however
be a mistake, says Ranade to suppose that the Upanishad
has stated any order in the occurrence of mystical experi-
ences, The Upanishad has made only a catalogue or an
inventory of mystical    experiences.    There       need    not   he
any Specific order in which they are experienced.             In the
domain of the Spirit who can determine which                 of    the
several] experiences 15 earlier and which later?           For, some
of them might even       synchronise.    In    the    phenomenal
world, the lightning is seen first and the thunder is heard
afterwards, because the light travels with a speed which
is far more tremendous than that of the sound. But in
the spiritual realm there may be light only and no sound
or there may be sound only and no light, or there may be
both light and sound at the same time.           Similarly, light
May emerge out of the innumerable cells of the bady, or
there may come forth the musical notes or indetermin-
able sounds, Perhaps, there may spring up different
colours, or that the whole body may           vibrate      with    the
waves of 01788.   What might take place at what time and
in the case of which sadhaka       can    never       ke    foretold.
Similarly, whether the photic or the morphic experience
will continue and the others cease to manifest, none can
Say anything definitely.    They say that there are seven
colours Vibgyor or that there are ten sounds such as those
of a bell, a conch, a drum, a flute or that of the roaring
of the sea or of a lion and so on; but this is only illustra-
tive, There may be many more colours and sounds. Who
206       Prof. R. 9. Ranade and His Spiritual Lineage


can     measure-the
                  immeasurable?      None: of these experi-
ences can be Said to be the effect of one’s own effort; they

are 211 dependent on the grace of God.          At the best, one
might say that- they constitute: God, but on that account
it ¢anno{ be said that they exhaust the content or adequ-
ately. describe the nature ‘of God.  One should    remain
Satisfied with what he hears or sees.


        (xii)   Ranade also refers to the erroneous          notion,
namely, that the placing. of the hand by the Guru on the
head of the disciple is a necessary sign of the grace of the
Guru; as'if there occurs the flow of magnetic influence
from-the Guru to the disciple.         The touch of the hand
may or may not be there. The grace can be conveyed by
making the disciple know the Name of Gcd               he    should
repeat, or by Opening his heart and sight by a mere look
at him or even by mere wish of the teacher.            Of course,
Ranade belonged to the school in which predecessors used
to impart the divine Name to the seekers of spiritual life.
It is    a miracle no doubt that what is sown in the ear, and
nurtured and       cultivated on the   tongue    is   ‘grown    and
revealed by eyes, and then reverberated         throughout      the
body. Not only the tongue becomes the threshold, as
Tulsidasa says, of the light within and the light without, but
the throat, the heart, and the breaths also become endowed
 with tongues and ears as if, and the whole body begins to
‘Shine with the lustre of God. An astrologer may say that
the twelve houses in the horoscope of a man which repre-
sent the entire life-history of that man become purified
One after another by the repetition of a particular mantra;
the mystic says that all the limbs of the         body      become
sound, healthy and lustrous simultaneously by the repeti-
-tion of the. Name imparted by the Guru.


  - (xiii)      The Nyaya system of philosophy has recognis-
‘ed four sources of valid knowledge. They are perception,
 inference, comparison and verbal testimony. It is remark-
 able no doubt that Carvaka, the atheist, should recognize
only one of them, namely, the. perceptual knowledge as
                   The: Summit at Nimbal                       207

the most reliable source of:validity. Inference to him had
only formal and indirect validity and in no case the con-
crete or the material and direct certainty.         How can the
known be compared with the unknown, when all the
human effort is directed towards knowing the unknown
Reality. Similarly, he would say that there is no grarantee
that testimony is of an honest person.          To Ranade also
perception is the only source of having valid knowledge.
But one need not rest- content by saying that extremes
meet, as if atheism and theism are one and the same.

                    BHAVA AND RASA

    Professor Ranade regards Rasa (sentiment) as a mani-
festation of Dhwani (suggestion) which Anandawardhana
calls -as the soul of poetry.      Bhava (emotion) being the
08815 of Rasa is superior to the latier. ‘The Bhavas are
related to the Rasas as function to ihe structure in modern
physioiogy,   Ranade tinds the traditional scheme of Rasas
delective cheiefly because-of its non-recognition’ of Bhakti
(devotion) as a Rasa. As he puts it, “A Gedless philosophy
and   a soulless   psychology are just’ the prototypes of          a
devotionless rhetoric.” In his view, Bhakti is not only a
Rasa in its own right but also the Murdhanyarasa    — the
king of all the Rasas. All the other ‘Rasas, such as Sringara,
hasya, karuna, vira, raudra   etc. can become aspects of
the Bhakti-rasa. That there is an intimate connection bet-
ween Bhavas (emotions) and Rasas sentiments) on the one
hand and Anubhavas        (expression of them), on the other
has been recognised       by     the   Indian   rhetoricians   and
‘Western   Psychologists,      But opinions     differ in   saying
Whether emotion is the cause of expression or vice versa.
The famous James-lange controversy brings out this point
clearly,   It would make emotion-only an            appendage to
expression,   According     to   it expression is the cause
                                                       of
emotion or for that matter, it is the essence of emotion.
But Ranade differs from the view and holds that emotion
is the fundamental thing, and expression’ an appendage.
The so-called Astasattvikabhavas have their proper habitat
in the Bhakti-rasa.

1.    There is no other way to liberation except the utter-
ance of the divine name imparted by the spiritual teacher
and the consequent vision of God.


2.    Ranade was moved to tears by the thought that the
worldly people are being lost in ignorance.


3.    Incarnation of   God means the vision    of God that
manifests before our eyes.


4.    He agrees with Baba in saying that what God wills
and does is true, neither self-efforts nor fate.    Therefore
surrender your will to the will of God.


5.    The name given by the Guru alone has the saving
power and is the essence of any other mantra (Name).

the Atman,

also said that if all periods of time were good, all would
become kings, but that whether the time is favourable or
